History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
10 min
The Matn-Fatwa Shift in the Sixth Era
Historical analysis of matn evolution and fatwa book proliferation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the original pedagogical purpose of matn literature and explain how the rise of fatwa collections in the sixth era preserved practical Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Matn works began as simple primers; later they became cryptic—fatwa collections restored practical fiqh.

This passage by Mustafa al-Zarqa reveals a critical turning point: the very tools designed to simplify fiqh became obstacles, leading to a surge in fatwa literature that preserved the living tradition. Understanding this helps us see why fatwa collections like al-Hindiyya are indispensable for a mufti today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original purpose of mutun
Find the phrase describing the initial goal of matn works: to gather simple issues in concise texts for beginners, similar to al-Ajurrumiyya in grammar.
The passage explicitly states this was a 'wise' (حكيماً) purpose.
2
Contrast with later practice
Note how later authors turned mutun into complex, cryptic texts requiring extensive commentaries, stifling innovation and making fiqh difficult for non-specialists.
Al-Zarqa criticizes this as producing 'leaves instead of fruit'.
3
Recognize the era's first advantage
The passage lists three advantages; focus on the first: the abundance of fatwa books. Understand that these recorded real legal questions and answers, arranged by fiqh chapters.
Fatwa books represent the applied side of fiqh.
4
Understand the style and importance
Fatwa books typically present a question followed by a madhhab-grounded answer. They are vital because later events often resemble past occurrences.
Examples: al-Fatawa al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Hindiyya.
5
Connect to modern fatwa practice
Reflect on how these collections remain key references for Hanafi muftis, providing precedent for new cases.
This illustrates the principle that historical fatwas guide contemporary ifta.

٣/١٥ - وتاريخ المتون في الحقيقة يرجع إلى أقدم من هذا العصر. ولكنها عندما ظهرت قديماً كان الغرض منها حكيماً: وهو جمع المسائل الأولية البسيطة في متون صغيرة بعبارة سهلة لتكون مبادىء لشداة الفقه على نحو الآجرومية في علم النحو. وهذا الغرض يقضي بأن يبقى المتن مختصراً بسيطاً سهلاً للمسائل الأولية مقصوراً عليها للغاية التعليمية، فلا توضع عليه الشروح الواسعة ولا الحواشي المعقدة. ولكن المتون انقلبت في العصر المبحوث عنه إلى طريقة عامة تعقيدية في تأليف الفقه؛ حتى أن أن يترك له أثراً وذكراً علمياً لا يفكر أن من يريد يخدم العلم بمؤلف مستقل يعمد به إلى التجديد في أسلوب الفقه ولغته، وفي تنقيحه وتقسيمه، وترتيبه وتبويبه والرجوع بمسائله المشتتة في غير أبوابها إلى أبوابها ومناسباتها فيضيف إلى جهود المتقدمين الحميدة جهوداً جديدة؛ بل كان كل مؤلف متأخر يحصر جهده في وضع حاشية على شرح، أو شرح على متن معقد، أو يضع متناً على نسق سائر المتون الاختزالية اللغزية التي تقدمته. ومن هنا نرى أن طريقة المتون بدأت قديماً معقولة الفكرة والغرض لوضع مبادىء العلم البسيطة تسهيلاً على المبتدئين، ثم أصبحت بالعوامل التقليدية على أيدي المتأخرين أشواكاً وتعاريج توعر بها طريق الفقه، واعتاص على غير ذوي الاختصاص، وأصبحت دوحة الفقه في أخريات هذا الدور تحمل الورق الكثير عوضاً عن الثمر اليانع.
١٥/ ٤ - مزايا هذا الدور :
وعلى كل يتميز هذا الدور الفقهي عن سواه بأمور ثلاث ذات بال:
١٥/ ٥ - (الأمر الأول) - نشاط حركة التدوين في التطبيقات الفقهية وما أنتجه من كثرة كتب الفتاوى التي كانت ظهرت قبل هذا العصر ولكنها كثرت فيه. فإن كثيراً ممن تقلدوا مهمة الافتاء الرسمية، أو بلغوا من العلمية ما كانوا فيها مرجعاً للناس في استفتاءاتهم، قد سجلوا فتاواهم في كتب خاصة، ورتبوها غالباً على أبواب الفقه. وكتب الفتاوى هذه تمثل الناحية التطبيقية العملية من الفقه، وتظهر نتائج المبادىء النظرية والأحكام المقررة، ومدى ملاءمتها للمصلحة التطبيقية عند وقوع الحوادث غير المتوقعة؛ وهي أيضاً مصدر صالح للوقوف على المكانة الفقهية في الحوادث المتوقعة كل وقت لأن الحوادث المتأخرة كثيراً ما تتشابه مع وقائع الماضي. وأسلوب كتب الفتاوى يجري غالباً على طريقة ذكر السؤال وإردافه بالجواب مع النصوص المذهبية التي يستند الجواب إليها. وقد يقتصر فيها على سرد أحكام الوقائع دون الأسئلة.
وقد وجد من كتب الفتاوى في هذا الدور ما كان ولا يزال من أهم المراجع الفقهية؛ كالفتاوى التتارخانية، والخانية والبزازية، والحامدية والهندية.
Chapter (15): The Sixth Era: 651-1286 AH. 3/15 - The history of texts (mutun) actually goes back earlier than this era. But when they appeared early on, their purpose was wise: to gather simple elementary issues in small texts with easy wording to serve as principles for beginners in fiqh, like al-Ajurrumiyya in grammar. This purpose required that the text remain concise, simple, easy for elementary issues, confined to them for educational purposes, so that extensive commentaries or complex glosses would not be placed upon them. But the texts in the era under discussion turned into a generally complicating method in writing fiqh, so much so that one who wanted to serve knowledge with an independent work aiming at innovation in the style and language of fiqh, in its revision and division, arrangement and chapterization, and returning its scattered issues to their proper chapters and contexts, adding new efforts to the commendable efforts of the predecessors, would not think of doing so; rather, every later author limited his effort to writing a gloss on a commentary, or a commentary on a complex text, or writing a text in the pattern of the previous reductionist and cryptic texts. Hence we see that the method of texts began early with reasonable idea and purpose: to place simple principles of knowledge for the ease of beginners, then through traditional factors by later scholars, it became thorns and twists making the path of fiqh rugged, and difficult for non-specialists, and the tree of fiqh in the latter part of this era produced much foliage instead of ripe fruit. 15/4 - Advantages of this era: In any case, this fiqh era is distinguished from others by three important things: 15/5 - (First thing) - Activity of compilation in fiqh applications and its result: the abundance of fatwa books that had appeared before this era but increased in it. For many who undertook the task of official fatwa, or who reached such scholarly standing that they were reference for people in their legal questions, recorded their fatwas in special books, usually arranged according to fiqh chapters. These fatwa books represent the practical applied side of fiqh, and show the results of theoretical principles and established rulings, and the extent of their suitability for practical benefit when unforeseen events occur; and they are also a valid source for understanding the jurisprudential status regarding only-occurring events at all times because later events often resemble past occurrences. The style of fatwa books usually proceeds by mentioning the question and appending the answer with the madhhab texts on which the answer is based. Sometimes it confines itself to listing rulings of events without the questions. Among the fatwa books in this era, there were and still are some of the most important fiqh references, such as al-Fatawa al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Khaniyya, al-Bazzaziyya, al-Hamidiyya, and al-Hindiyya.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a historical analysis of the sixth era of Islamic jurisprudence (651-1286 AH) by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa. It first discusses the evolution of matn (text) literature. Originally, matn works were simple primers for beginners, akin to al-Ajurrumiyya in grammar. Their purpose was educational: to present basic issues concisely without need for extensive commentaries. However, by this era, the method had changed: later scholars composed complex, cryptic texts that required elaborate commentaries and glosses, stifling innovation. Al-Zarqa criticizes this as producing leaves without fruit—complexity without substance. He praises Imam al-Kasani's 'Bada'i' al-Sana'i' as a rare example of true reform. The passage then turns to the main advantage of this era: the proliferation of fatwa collections. These books, compiled by official muftis and senior scholars, recorded practical responses to real-world problems. They represent the applied dimension of fiqh, bridging theory and practice. The style typically presents a question followed by a madhhab-grounded answer. Notable examples include al-Fatawa al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Khaniyya, al-Bazzaziyya, al-Hamidiyya, and the famous al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya (also known as al-Fatawa al-?Alamgiriyya). These remain key references in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
المتون (al-mutun): Concise legal texts (matn, pl. mutun) that summarize basic fiqh principles, often used as textbooks for beginners.
كتب الفتاوى (kutub al-fatawa): Books compiling real or hypothetical legal questions (istifta') and their answers (fatawa), arranged by fiqh chapters.
التدوين (al-tadwin): The activity of systematic compilation and recording of legal rulings and applications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the original purpose of matn literature in fiqh?
A) To provide a comprehensive reference for advanced scholars
B) To gather simple elementary issues in small texts for beginners
C) To replace the need for fatwa collections
D) To introduce cryptic language to test students

Key Takeaway
The transformation of mutun from simple primers to complex texts necessitated the rise of fatwa collections as the primary applied source of Hanafi fiqh.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Rise of Fatwa Books: Applied Fiqh in Action
Describes the rise of fatwa collections as the first feature of the sixth fiqh stage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features of fatwa books (kutub al-fatawa) as described in the passage and explain their role as practical applications of fiqh with reference to Hanafi methodology.

Core Idea
Fatwa books bridge theoretical fiqh and real-world cases; they are not historical artifacts but active tools for analogy.

The sixth stage of Islamic jurisprudence witnessed a surge in the compilation of fatwa books. These works were not mere records—they became essential references for muftis, documenting real questions and answers with authoritative evidence. In this lesson, we will dissect the passage by Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa to understand how these books functioned as the practical arm of fiqh, especially within the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical context
Note that the passage discusses the sixth stage (651-1286 AH), a period of maturity where fiqh became specialized and fatwa collections proliferated. Recognize the author's claim that this stage's first feature is the active codification of fatwa responses.
This sets the stage for understanding why these books emerged.
2
Analyze the purpose of fatwa books
The passage states that fatwa books 'represent the practical applied aspect of fiqh' and show the results of theoretical principles. This means they are not abstract but grounded in actual incidents.
Think of them as case law that illustrates how rulings work in practice.
3
Examine the typical structure
Fatwa books often follow a question-answer format, with the response supported by madhhab texts. Some list rulings without questions. This structure makes them easy to navigate for muftis seeking precedents.
This format became standard in later Hanafi fatwa collections.
4
Appreciate their timeless utility
The author argues that fatwa books are 'a valid source for understanding the juristic method in expected incidents at all times' because new incidents often resemble past ones. This enables analogical reasoning (qiyas) from documented cases.
Fatwa books thus have ongoing relevance, not just historical interest.
5
Recognize key examples
The passage lists important Hanafi fatwa compilations: al-Fatawa al-Tatarkhaniyya, al-Khaniyya, al-Bazzaziyya, al-Hamidiyya, and especially al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya (also called al-Fatawa al-'Alamgiriyya), which was a monumental team effort under Emperor Aurangzeb.
Understanding their authority helps a mufti prioritize sources.

Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. And it became difficult for non-specialists, and the tree of fiqh in the latter part of this stage bore many leaves instead of ripe fruit. 15/4 - Advantages of this stage: Overall, this juristic stage is distinguished from others by three important matters: 15/5 - (First matter) - Activity of codification in juristic applications and what it produced of the abundance of fatwa books that had appeared before this era but increased in it. Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage outlines the first distinguishing feature of the sixth stage (circa 651-1286 AH) of Islamic jurisprudence: the proliferation of fatwa books. The author, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa, explains that these collections were compiled by official muftis and scholars who recorded their responses to real questions, arranging them by fiqh topics. They serve as a bridge between theoretical principles and practical application, providing models for new cases. The passage also lists key Hanafi fatwa compilations, with a footnote detailing the famous al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya (also called al-Fatawa al-'Alamgiriyya), commissioned by Emperor Aurangzeb. This compilation, produced by a team of Hanafi jurists led by Shaykh Nizam al-Din, selected the soundest rulings from the entire Hanafi corpus and became a monumental reference. The passage emphasizes that fatwa books are not merely historical records but living sources for deriving rulings for contemporary situations, as similar incidents recur.

Key Terms
كتب الفتاوى (kutub al-fatawa): Compilations of legal responses (fatwas) issued by qualified muftis, arranged by fiqh topics, documenting actual questions and answers with supporting textual evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary value of fatwa books as described in the passage?
A) They preserve the opinions of early scholars only.
B) They represent the practical application of theoretical fiqh and help in new incidents.
C) They are literary works without legal force.
D) They replace the need for primary texts like the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
Fatwa books are not mere archives; they are living tools that connect theoretical fiqh to real-world cases, providing precedents for analogical reasoning in new incidents.

The Beginning of Codification (Taqnin) in the Ottoman Empire
Discussing the start of codification (taqnin) in the late Ottoman period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical context and rationale behind the Ottoman codification (taqnin) and evaluate its impact on Hanafi fatwa practice.

Core Idea
Codification amends Hanafi rulings to meet new needs without abandoning the school's foundations.

The late Ottoman period saw a significant development: the codification of Islamic law into state-enacted codes. This passage introduces the factors that drove this movement, highlighting the flexibility of Hanafi fiqh to respond to changing economic and social circumstances. Understanding taqnin is key to grasping how classical fiqh can be applied in modern states.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal development
Recognise that 'taqnin' refers to codification: the issuance of state laws based on Hanafi fiqh.
The term is not just 'writing down' but involves adaptation and selection.
2
Determine the driving factors
Note the two factors: new local needs and economic ties with Europe.
Codification was a response to changing circumstances, not arbitrary change.
3
Analyse the Hanafi selection
The Ottoman state chose Hanafi doctrine as its basis; understand why this school was dominant.
Hanafi was the official school, but other opinions could be included if needed.
4
Evaluate the flexibility claim
The passage states that Islamic fiqh's tolerance and flexibility enabled this adaptation.
Flexibility is not weakness but a capacity to fulfill interests of times and generations.
5
Reflect on implications for fatwa practice
Consider how codification affects a mufti: it creates a hierarchy between codified law and classical scholarship.
Muftis must navigate between state law and tradition.

Source Text

٧/١٥ - (الأمر الثالث) - بدء حركة التقنين أواخر هذا العصر أي إصدار القوانين التي عدلت من الأحكام الفقهية الشرعية المعمول بها في المذهب الحنفي الذي هو مذهب الدولة العثمانية نظراً لما كان يلحظ من حاجات جديدة محلية وأخرى ناشئة عن ارتباط العلائق الاقتصادية ببلاد أوروبا وقوانينها .
وهذا من سماحة الفقه الإسلامي وسجاحته ومرونته التي أكسبته قابلية للوفاء بمصالح الأزمان والأجيال.
7/15 - (The Third Matter) - The beginning of the codification movement at the end of this era, i.e., the issuance of laws that amended the Shari'a juristic rulings that were in practice in the Hanafi school, which was the school of the Ottoman state, due to new local needs and others arising from the connection of economic relations with the countries of Europe and their laws. And this is from the tolerance, facility, and flexibility of Islamic fiqh, which gave it the ability to fulfill the interests of times and generations.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage introduces the third development of the Ottoman era: the move toward codification (taqnin). The author explains that laws were issued to amend the Hanafi rulings that were standard in the Ottoman Empire, driven by new local needs and the influence of European economic relations and laws. This reflects the flexibility of Islamic jurisprudence, which can adapt to changing circumstances.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnīn): Codification of Islamic law into legal codes, often adapted from existing fiqh rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) To replace Shari'a with European law
B) To address new needs arising from economic ties with Europe
C) To create a new madhhab
D) To abolish the Hanafi school

Key Takeaway
The Ottoman codification illustrates Islamic law's capacity to adapt to new times while remaining rooted in Hanafi doctrine.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
6 min
The Codification Movement in the Late Ottoman Era: Flexibility of Hanafi Fiqh
Describes the commencement of issuing codified laws modifying Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical context and legal rationale behind the Ottoman codification (taqnin) of Hanafi fiqh and identify its significance as a manifestation of the flexibility of Islamic jurisprudence.

Core Idea
Islamic jurisprudence's flexibility allowed the Ottoman state to codify Hanafi fiqh, adapting rulings to new economic realities without abandoning the madhhab.

This passage introduces a pivotal moment in Islamic legal history: the late Ottoman codification movement. It emphasizes the inherent flexibility (sijahah) of Islamic fiqh, which enabled it to respond to changing societal needs. The focus is on al-taqnin—the process of issuing codified laws that modified existing Hanafi rulings to address new local and international economic pressures. Understanding this history is essential for appreciating how Hanafi fiqh has been applied in modern state contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Historical Context
Locate the time period (late Ottoman era) and the official madhhab of the state (Hanafi). The passage mentions the Ottoman state and the Hanafi school as the basis for the laws.
2
Understand the Concept of al-Taqnin
Define al-taqnin as the issuance of codified laws that amend or regulate Sharia rulings. Note that these laws did not replace fiqh but modified it for practical application.
The term 'qawanin' (laws) is used, distinct from fiqh itself.
3
Recognize the Driving Factors
Identify two reasons: new local needs and the connection of economic relations with Europe. The passage directly states these as causes for codification.
4
Appreciate the Principle of Flexibility
Link the codification to the broader characteristic of Islamic jurisprudence: flexibility that allows it to fulfill the interests of times and generations. The passage describes this as 'سماحة الفقه الإسلامي وسجاحته ومرونته' (tolerance, ease, flexibility).
This flexibility is a key Hanafi usul al-fiqh principle (istihsan, custom, etc.).

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته وهذا من سماحة الفقه الإسلامي وسجاحته ومرونته التي أكسبته قابلية للوفاء بمصالح الأزمان والأجيال. ٧/١٥ - (الأمر الثالث) - بدء حركة التقنين أواخر هذا العصر أي إصدار القوانين التي عدلت من الأحكام الفقهية الشرعية المعمول بها في المذهب الحنفي الذي هو مذهب الدولة العثمانية نظراً لما كان يلحظ من حاجات جديدة محلية وأخرى ناشئة عن ارتباط العلائق الاقتصادية ببلاد أوروبا وقوانينها.
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. This is part of the tolerance, ease, and flexibility of Islamic jurisprudence that gave it the ability to fulfill the interests of times and generations. 7/15 - (The Third Matter) - The beginning of the codification movement at the end of this era, i.e., issuing laws that amended the Sharia juristic rulings practiced in the Hanafi school, which is the school of the Ottoman state, due to new local needs and others arising from the connection of economic relations with European countries and their laws.

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the third stage of Islamic jurisprudence development. It highlights a key characteristic: the flexibility of Islamic fiqh, which allowed it to adapt to changing circumstances. The focus is on the late Ottoman period when a formal codification (taqnin) movement began. This involved issuing laws that modified existing Hanafi rulings to address new local needs and the influence of European economic relations. The text underscores that this adaptability is a strength of Islamic law.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnin): The process of issuing codified laws (qawanin) that amend or regulate Sharia rulings.
المرونة (al-murunah): Flexibility; a characteristic of Islamic jurisprudence that allows it to adapt to changing circumstances.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
The Ottoman Majalla: Codifying Hanafi Mu'amalat
Historical analysis of the need for the Majalla and its compilation process

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the reasons for the codification of the Ottoman Majalla and describe its structure and scope.

Core Idea
The Ottoman Majalla codified Hanafi mu'amalat into 1851 articles to make fiqh accessible to judges.

Islamic fiqh rulings were scattered across vast books, varying in quality and accessibility. When the Ottoman Empire established regular courts, there was a pressing need to provide judges—especially non-sharia judges—with a clear, authoritative reference. This led to the creation of the Majalla al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya, a landmark codification that shaped Islamic legal practice for generations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize that fiqh rulings were scattered across many books, often in non-obvious places, requiring extensive expertise to locate authoritative positions.
This difficulty was compounded by differences between schools and even within the Hanafi school itself.
2
Understand the catalyst
The establishment of regular courts (mahakim nizamiyya) created a need for non-sharia judges to apply fiqh in mu'amalat cases. They could not be expected to navigate traditional fiqh literature.
The Majalla was designed for these judges and for consistency across the empire.
3
Analyze the solution
A sultanic committee selected Hanafi rulings on transactions (mu'amalat), organized them into 16 books with 1851 numbered articles, and even included some weaker opinions for public interest (maslaha).
The numbering made citation and amendment easy, similar to modern codes.
4
Reflect on the significance
The Majalla represents the first codification of Islamic fiqh in statute form, influencing later Arab legal systems and setting a precedent for taqnin.
It focused only on mu'amalat, leaving personal status to traditional sharia courts.

Chapter Sixteen: The Seventh Juristic Period - From the Appearance of the Majalla to the End of World War II (1287-1354 AH). 1/16 - This period is distinguished by two features of historical importance in the style of Islamic jurisprudence and its judicial sovereignty: Hanafi. First: The appearance of the Majalla al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya as a general civil code from fiqh. Second: The expansion of codification that began at the end of the previous era, and the establishment of a huge legal structure alongside the edifice of Islamic jurisprudence. We will explain these two aspects below. Section One: The Appearance of the Majalla and its Cause. 2/16 - Indeed, the rulings of Islamic jurisprudence are scattered in fiqh books in every mujtahid school. Many rulings are found in places other than where they are researched, and mentioned by way of digression in other than their proper chapters. These books vary in their comprehensiveness of scholarly rulings and school opinions according to whether they are abridged or lengthy. You may find in one book rulings that you do not find in another, or that contradict another, just as they vary in language, expressions, and style as do the commentaries on modern codes in these respects. Chapter Three: Stages of the Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and its Characteristics. Since every mujtahid school is not free from different opinions and statements on the same issue, due to differences in transmissions in the original school from its imam, or differences in the extractors of rulings for new events and the preferrers mentioned earlier, the degree of consideration of the school's books and the transmitted juristic opinions in them varies according to the scholarly standing of the authors of those books or the holders of the transmitted opinions and statements. Searching for the texts of juristic rulings in those books and extracting them from their proper and improper places, and knowing the strong preponderant from the weak outweighed, requires long juristic ability and flexibility that is difficult for many. When the regular courts began to be established in the Ottoman Empire, and jurisdiction over types of lawsuits that were previously referred to the sharia courts came back to them, and there was a need to facilitate review of juristic rulings for non-sharia judges and to acquaint them with the strong operative opinions versus the weak abandoned ones, without having to delve into the vast fiqh books, a sultanic decree was issued to appoint a committee to compile a collection of sharia rulings that most frequently recurred in events. So the committee in the year 1286 AH compiled this collection selected from the section of transactions (mu'amalat) of the Hanafi school, which was the state's operative school, and arranged its topics on the usual fiqh books and chapters, but detailed the rulings in articles with serial numbers like modern codes to facilitate reference and citation. Its total came to (1851) articles. It adopted some weak opinions in the school for the temporal interest that required it. Its subjects are sixteen books divided into chapters, and chapters into sections; the first is the Book of Sales and the last is the Book of Judiciary, in the following order:

Commentary
This passage describes the seventh juristic period in the development of Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on the Ottoman Majalla. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, highlights two major features: the codification of Hanafi fiqh into a civil code (the Majalla) and the expansion of legal codification in general. He explains the necessity for the Majalla: Islamic fiqh rulings were scattered across many books, with varying degrees of reliability and style, making it difficult for judges, especially non-sharia judges, to access authoritative opinions. In response, the Ottoman government formed a committee in 1286 AH to compile a collection of Hanafi mu'amalat (transactions) rulings, organized into 1851 articles across 16 books, with numbered articles like modern codes. The committee even included some weaker opinions when necessitated by contemporary public interest. This codification was a landmark event in Islamic legal history, streamlining fatwa and judicial practice.

Key Terms
مجلة الأحكام الشرعية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya): The Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi fiqh, consisting of 1851 articles covering transactions (mu'amalat) and judicial procedure.
التقنين (al-taqnin): The process of codifying Islamic fiqh into statutory articles, as opposed to the traditional narrative fiqh books.
المعاملات (al-mu'amalat): Transactions and civil dealings, the area of fiqh covered by the Majalla, excluding worship and personal status.

The Problem of Multiple Opinions and the Need for Codification
Historical-legal analysis of pre-Majalla difficulties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the historical problem of multiple opinions in Hanafi fiqh and explain why the Ottoman state needed a codified legal text.

Core Idea
The Majalla was born from the need to make Hanafi law accessible to non-specialist judges.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work describes a pivotal moment in Islamic legal history: the creation of the Ottoman Majalla. It begins by explaining the difficulty of navigating the vast Hanafi literature with its multiple opinions, and then recounts how the establishment of secular courts created a practical need for a simplified, authoritative compilation of fiqh rulings on transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize that the Hanafi school contains many opinions due to different narrations and later jurists' ijtihad. This made it hard for non-specialist judges to find the strong operative opinion.
2
Understand the institutional shift
The Ottoman state established regular courts that took over some cases from Sharia courts. These judges were not trained in fiqh.
3
Comprehend the solution
A sultanic decree formed a committee to compile a collection of frequently needed rulings from Hanafi muamalat.
4
Note the selection process
The committee selected opinions, sometimes adopting weaker ones for public benefit.

٢٢٧
Chapter Three: Periods of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics
Since every ijtihadi madhhab is not free from different opinions and statements on a single issue due to differing narrations in the foundation of the madhhab from its imam, or due to the differences among the extractors of rulings for new incidents and the preferrers mentioned previously, therefore the degree of consideration of the books of the madhhab and the juristic opinions transmitted therein varies and differs according to the variation in the scholarly status of the authors of those books or the owners of the opinions and statements transmitted therein.
Thus, researching the texts of juristic rulings in those books and extracting them from their proper and improper places, and knowing the strong and preponderant from the weak and outweighed, requires long juristic ability and flexibility that is difficult for many.
When the establishment of regular courts in the Ottoman state began, and their jurisdiction came to include types of lawsuits that previously belonged to the Sharia courts, and the need arose to facilitate the review of juristic rulings for non-Sharia judges and to acquaint them with the strong and operative opinions as opposed to the weak and abandoned ones without them having to delve into the extensive books of fiqh, a supreme sultanic decree was issued for the formation of a committee to compile a collection of Sharia rulings that occur most frequently in incidents.
So the committee compiled in the year 1286 AH this collection, selected from the section of transactions (mu'amalat) from the Hanafi school of law which was the operative law of the state, and arranged its topics according to the customary books and chapters of fiqh, but detailed the rulings in articles with sequential numbers like modern laws to facilitate reference and citation. Thus its total came to 1851 articles. It adopted some outweighed opinions in the madhhab for the temporal benefit that necessitated them.
Its subjects are sixteen books, divided into chapters and the chapters into sections; the first is the Book of Sales and the last is the Book of Judicature, and they are in the following order:
226
Section 16: The Seventh Period: 1287-1354 AH
Sales - Leases - Guarantee - Transfer of Debt - Pledge - Trusts - Gift - Usurpation and Destruction - Interdiction, Coercion, and Pre-emption - Partnerships - Agency - Settlement and Release - Acknowledgment - Lawsuit - Evidence and Oath - Judicature
3/16 - The committee named this collection 'Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyya' (The Journal of Judicial Rulings) and prefaced it with a report of the 'List of Reasons' i.e., the explanatory memorandum; then they presented it with an introduction comprising two sections:
The first: on the definition of fiqh and its division.
The second: in which they mentioned a set of universal principles (qawa'id kulliyya), each of which is considered a fiqh principle upon which many rulings revolve; they derived ninety-nine rules: the first is the rule 'Matters are determined by their objectives' and the last is the rule 'Whoever seeks to undo what has been completed from his side, his effort is rejected.'
The supreme sultanic decree was issued in Sha'ban of the year 1293 AH mandating its implementation and application of its rulings in the state courts.
Thus this Majalla became a general civil law selected from fiqh rulings; so what is found in it is not to be relied upon if it contradicts the books of fiqh due to its association with the sultanic order. Rather, judges refer to the texts of the jurists for what is not explicitly provided in the Majalla, which is the general law.
4/16 - The prominent new thing in the Majalla is that it manifested the distinction and differentiation in the composition of fiqh between the style of scientific or educational sources and the style of judicial references in terms of arrangement and numbering, simplification of expression, and restriction to a single operative opinion in each issue without mentioning the extensive differences of jurists found in fiqh books. This is a requirement of legal drafting, for the law must not contain anything other than the ruling to be applied; as for the field of opinions, it is only in the commentaries that clarify to be a scientific or educational source.
227
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This is a historical-legal analysis from Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa describing the need for and creation of the Ottoman Majalla. The passage begins by noting the inherent multiplicity of opinions within any ijtihadi madhhab, and the difficulty of navigating the vast fiqh literature to find the strong, operative ruling. This difficulty became acute when the Ottoman state established 'regular' (non-Sharia) courts, whose judges were not trained in the classical fiqh methodology. To address this, a sultanic decree commissioned a committee to compile a collection of Hanafi rulings on frequently occurring transactions (muamalat). The committee produced the Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyya in 1286 AH (1869 CE), containing 1851 sequentially numbered articles across 16 books—from Sales to Judicature. Importantly, the committee sometimes adopted minority (marjuh) opinions for temporal benefit. The Majalla was prefaced with 99 universal legal maxims (qawaid kulliyya), beginning with 'Al-umur bi-maqasidiha' and ending with the rule on preventing contradictory actions. In 1293 AH (1876 CE), a sultanic decree made the Majalla binding law, superseding contradictory opinions in fiqh books on points it covered, while judges still referred to classical fiqh for matters not in the Majalla. The key innovation of the Majalla, as al-Zarqa emphasizes, is the shift from a scholarly, multi-opinion style to a legislative, single-ruling style—a necessary feature of codified law. This marks a turning point in Islamic legal history: the first successful codification of Sharia-based civil law.

Key Terms
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyya): The first Islamic civil code, compiled in 1286 AH (1869 CE) by an Ottoman committee, comprising 1851 articles on Hanafi muamalat.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that serve as foundational principles for many fiqh rulings; the Majalla included 99 such rules in its introduction.
الإرادة السنية السلطانية (al-irada al-suniyya al-sultaniyya): A sultanic decree (firman) issued by the Ottoman sultan making the Majalla binding law in state courts.

The Majalla's Structure and Its Legal Innovation
Description of Majalla's structure and innovation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the structure of the Ottoman Majalla and explain how it innovated the transition from scholarly fiqh literature to codified law.

Core Idea
The Majalla shifted fiqh from a scholarly, multi-opinion format to a legislative, single-ruling style.

In this continuation of his analysis, Dr. al-Zarqa details the actual output of the Ottoman codification committee. The Majalla was arranged into 16 books with 1851 sequentially numbered articles, and its introduction included 99 universal legal maxims. Most significantly, the Majalla introduced a fundamental distinction between educational fiqh sources and judicial references, requiring each legal article to state only one operative ruling.

Guided Path
1
Examine the scope
The Majalla covers only muamalat (transactions), not ibadat or other areas. Its 16 books range from Sales to Judicature.
2
Understand the numbering system
Each ruling is an article with a sequential number, like modern laws, making reference and citation easy.
3
Recognize the role of qawa'id
The introduction includes 99 universal legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyya), such as 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha', which serve as overarching principles.
4
Identify the key innovation
The Majalla distinguishes between the style of scientific/educational fiqh (multiple opinions) and judicial references (single ruling). This is a necessary feature of codified law.
5
Note the legal force
A sultanic decree made the Majalla binding law; its rulings supersede contrary opinions in fiqh books on covered matters.

٢٢٧
Chapter Three: Periods of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics
Since every ijtihadi madhhab is not free from different opinions and statements on a single issue due to differing narrations in the foundation of the madhhab from its imam, or due to the differences among the extractors of rulings for new incidents and the preferrers mentioned previously, therefore the degree of consideration of the books of the madhhab and the juristic opinions transmitted therein varies and differs according to the variation in the scholarly status of the authors of those books or the owners of the opinions and statements transmitted therein.
Thus, researching the texts of juristic rulings in those books and extracting them from their proper and improper places, and knowing the strong and preponderant from the weak and outweighed, requires long juristic ability and flexibility that is difficult for many.
When the establishment of regular courts in the Ottoman state began, and their jurisdiction came to include types of lawsuits that previously belonged to the Sharia courts, and the need arose to facilitate the review of juristic rulings for non-Sharia judges and to acquaint them with the strong and operative opinions as opposed to the weak and abandoned ones without them having to delve into the extensive books of fiqh, a supreme sultanic decree was issued for the formation of a committee to compile a collection of Sharia rulings that occur most frequently in incidents.
So the committee compiled in the year 1286 AH this collection, selected from the section of transactions (mu'amalat) from the Hanafi school of law which was the operative law of the state, and arranged its topics according to the customary books and chapters of fiqh, but detailed the rulings in articles with sequential numbers like modern laws to facilitate reference and citation. Thus its total came to 1851 articles. It adopted some outweighed opinions in the madhhab for the temporal benefit that necessitated them.
Its subjects are sixteen books, divided into chapters and the chapters into sections; the first is the Book of Sales and the last is the Book of Judicature, and they are in the following order:
226
Section 16: The Seventh Period: 1287-1354 AH
Sales - Leases - Guarantee - Transfer of Debt - Pledge - Trusts - Gift - Usurpation and Destruction - Interdiction, Coercion, and Pre-emption - Partnerships - Agency - Settlement and Release - Acknowledgment - Lawsuit - Evidence and Oath - Judicature
3/16 - The committee named this collection 'Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyya' (The Journal of Judicial Rulings) and prefaced it with a report of the 'List of Reasons' i.e., the explanatory memorandum; then they presented it with an introduction comprising two sections:
The first: on the definition of fiqh and its division.
The second: in which they mentioned a set of universal principles (qawa'id kulliyya), each of which is considered a fiqh principle upon which many rulings revolve; they derived ninety-nine rules: the first is the rule 'Matters are determined by their objectives' and the last is the rule 'Whoever seeks to undo what has been completed from his side, his effort is rejected.'
The supreme sultanic decree was issued in Sha'ban of the year 1293 AH mandating its implementation and application of its rulings in the state courts.
Thus this Majalla became a general civil law selected from fiqh rulings; so what is found in it is not to be relied upon if it contradicts the books of fiqh due to its association with the sultanic order. Rather, judges refer to the texts of the jurists for what is not explicitly provided in the Majalla, which is the general law.
4/16 - The prominent new thing in the Majalla is that it manifested the distinction and differentiation in the composition of fiqh between the style of scientific or educational sources and the style of judicial references in terms of arrangement and numbering, simplification of expression, and restriction to a single operative opinion in each issue without mentioning the extensive differences of jurists found in fiqh books. This is a requirement of legal drafting, for the law must not contain anything other than the ruling to be applied; as for the field of opinions, it is only in the commentaries that clarify to be a scientific or educational source.
227
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This is a historical-legal analysis from Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa describing the need for and creation of the Ottoman Majalla. The passage begins by noting the inherent multiplicity of opinions within any ijtihadi madhhab, and the difficulty of navigating the vast fiqh literature to find the strong, operative ruling. This difficulty became acute when the Ottoman state established 'regular' (non-Sharia) courts, whose judges were not trained in the classical fiqh methodology. To address this, a sultanic decree commissioned a committee to compile a collection of Hanafi rulings on frequently occurring transactions (muamalat). The committee produced the Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyya in 1286 AH (1869 CE), containing 1851 sequentially numbered articles across 16 books—from Sales to Judicature. Importantly, the committee sometimes adopted minority (marjuh) opinions for temporal benefit. The Majalla was prefaced with 99 universal legal maxims (qawaid kulliyya), beginning with 'Al-umur bi-maqasidiha' and ending with the rule on preventing contradictory actions. In 1293 AH (1876 CE), a sultanic decree made the Majalla binding law, superseding contradictory opinions in fiqh books on points it covered, while judges still referred to classical fiqh for matters not in the Majalla. The key innovation of the Majalla, as al-Zarqa emphasizes, is the shift from a scholarly, multi-opinion style to a legislative, single-ruling style—a necessary feature of codified law. This marks a turning point in Islamic legal history: the first successful codification of Sharia-based civil law.

Key Terms
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyya): The first Islamic civil code, compiled in 1286 AH (1869 CE) by an Ottoman committee, comprising 1851 articles on Hanafi muamalat.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyya): Universal legal maxims that serve as foundational principles for many fiqh rulings; the Majalla included 99 such rules in its introduction.
الإرادة السنية السلطانية (al-irada al-suniyya al-sultaniyya): A sultanic decree (firman) issued by the Ottoman sultan making the Majalla binding law in state courts.

Key Takeaway
The Majalla's innovation lies in its conversion of fiqh from a multi-opinion scholarly discipline into a single-ruling codified law, with numbered articles and a set of foundational legal maxims.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah: Codification of Hanafi Fiqh as State Law
Historical description of the Majallah's compilation, content, and legal impact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the key features of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah and identify how it transformed fiqh from scholarly discourse into a binding legal code, focusing on its use of qawa'id kulliyyah and its organizational structure.

Core Idea
The Majallah transformed Hanafi fiqh into state law by codifying one ruling per issue and introducing 99 universal maxims as founding principles.

The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, the Ottoman civil code, represents a pivotal moment in Hanafi legal history. It was the first systematic codification of fiqh that became authoritative state law. This lesson explores its structure, its reliance on universal legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyyah), and its distinction between academic fiqh writing and judicial codes. You will learn how the Majallah organized fiqh topics, why it omitted ikhtilaf (differences of opinion), and how it set a precedent for modern legal drafting.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical context
When and why was the Majallah compiled? It was created in the 19th century to standardize court rulings and unify legal practice in the Ottoman Empire.
The passage indicates the compilation period: 1287-1354 AH (19th-20th century).
2
Identify the content areas
List the civil law topics covered by the Majallah as mentioned in the passage: sales, leases, guarantee, transfer of debt, pledge, trusts, gift, usurpation and damage, restriction/coercion/preemption, companies, agency, settlement and release, confession, lawsuit, evidence and oath, judiciary.
3
Analyze the introduction's two articles
The Majallah is prefaced with an introduction containing two articles: the first defines fiqh and its divisions; the second presents 99 universal legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyyah). The first maxim is 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are determined by their objectives) and the last is about nullification of self-contradictory acts.
These maxims serve as foundational principles for deriving rulings.
4
Grasp the legal authority
The Majallah was given imperial sanction (iradeh seniyye) in 1293 AH, making it binding law in Ottoman courts. For matters covered by the Majallah, conflicting opinions in fiqh books are not relied upon, though judges may consult jurists for uncodified issues.
This established the Majallah as the primary source over other Hanafi texts.
5
Recognize the stylistic innovation
The Majallah distinguishes between academic fiqh writing and judicial codes. It uses clear, numbered articles, provides one authoritative ruling per issue (no ikhtilaf), and organizes material systematically by subject (e.g., placing mudarabah under companies).
This reflects the requirements of legal drafting (al-siyaghah al-qanuniyyah).

Chapter (16) The Seventh Period: 1287-1354 AH. Sales - Leases - Guarantee - Transfer of Debt - Pledge - Trusts - Gift - Usurpation and Damage - Restriction, Coercion, and Preemption - Companies - Agency - Settlement and Release - Confession - Lawsuit - Evidence and Oath - Judiciary. 3/16 - The committee named this collection 'Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah' and prefaced it with a report of the reasons (explanatory memorandum); then presented it with an introduction containing two articles: the first on the definition of fiqh and its divisions; the second: in which she mentioned a group of universal legal maxims, each of which is considered a juristic principle upon which many rulings revolve; she presented ninety-nine maxims: the first is the maxim 'Matters are determined by their objectives', and the last is the maxim 'Whoever attempts to nullify what has been completed from his side, his effort is rejected.' The royal imperial will was issued in Sha'ban 1293 AH requiring its adoption and application of its provisions in the courts of the state. Thus this Majalla became a general civil law selected from juristic rulings; whatever is found in it, no contrary ruling in the fiqh books is relied upon because it is coupled with the imperial order, and judges refer to the texts of jurists only for matters not stipulated in the Majalla, which is the general law. 4/16 - The new and prominent thing in the Majalla is that it manifested the distinction and differentiation in the composition of fiqh between the style of scientific or educational sources and the style of judicial references in terms of arrangement and numbering, simplification of expression, and restriction to one authoritative ruling for every issue without mentioning the extensive differences of jurists in fiqh books. This is a requirement of legal drafting, for the law must not contain anything other than the applicable ruling; as for the field of opinions, it is only in commentaries that clarify to be a scientific or educational source. The Majalla was very careful to return discussions and branches to their appropriate contexts and proper places. For example, the contract of mudarabah is mentioned by jurists outside the topic of companies, but the Majalla mentioned it under companies because mudarabah is nothing but a contract of partnership with capital from one side and work from the other. Similarly, the Majalla divided the issues of each book into basic and sub-topics such as definitions, conditions, rulings, etc., and detailed them in well-distinguished chapters, attaching each issue to its subject.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a historical description of the Ottoman Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, a landmark codification of Hanafi fiqh. It begins by listing the broad fiqh topics covered in the seventh period (1287-1354 AH), which reads like a table of contents for a corpus of commercial and civil law. The author then explains that the compilation was named 'Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah' and introduced with an explanatory memorandum and two introductory articles. Notably, the second article contains ninety-nine universal legal maxims (qawa'id kulliyyah) that serve as fundamental juristic principles for deriving many rulings. These maxims start with 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are determined by their objectives) and end with the maxim against self-contradiction. The Majalla was given imperial sanction in 1293 AH, making it binding law in Ottoman courts, superseding conflicting fiqh texts for matters it covered. The text stresses a key feature: the Majalla distinguishes between academic fiqh writing, which includes variant opinions, and judicial codes, which must state one definitive ruling per issue. This is essential for law application. The Majalla also improved organization by placing each branch (furu') under its appropriate main topic (asl), correcting the scattered treatment in classical works—for example, placing mudarabah under companies rather than as a separate subject. The last part describes how the Majalla systematically divided each book into foundational and subsidiary topics with clear chapters.

Key Terms
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah): The Ottoman civil code compiled in the 19th century based on Hanafi fiqh, intended to unify legal practice in courts.
القواعد الكلية (al-qawa'id al-kulliyyah): Universal legal maxims that summarize core principles of fiqh and guide deduction of rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) 96

Key Takeaway
The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah codified Hanafi civil law into a state code, using 99 universal maxims as guiding principles and providing one binding ruling per issue, thereby transforming the nature of fiqh application.

The Expansion of Codification in Islamic Lands
A historical overview of codification in the late Ottoman era.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main branches of codification affected by the modern codification movement and explain the relationship between statutory legislation and classical fiqh in the Ottoman and post-Ottoman contexts.

Core Idea
Modern codification penetrated nearly every chapter of fiqh, especially in civil, land, and procedural law.

How did classical Islamic jurisprudence transform into modern legal codes? In the late Ottoman era, codification (al-taqnin) expanded rapidly across Muslim lands, touching civil, criminal, and administrative law. This lesson examines the scope of that transformation and the factors behind it, setting the stage for understanding how muftis today navigate between inherited fiqh and codified statutes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three branches of codification
The passage lists civil, criminal, and administrative law as the three branches that underwent codification. Note that only civil codification is directly tied to fiqh history.
Civil codification includes commercial, land, and procedural laws.
2
Recognize the depth of legal transformation
The text states that fiqh became 'thoroughly interwoven' with legislation to the point that nearly every chapter of fiqh saw amendment or partial abrogation. This shows the comprehensive impact of codification.
Think of the Majallah as a Hanafi example of this codification.
3
Understand the geographical scope
The movement covered the Ottoman state and its separated regions like Syria, Palestine, and Iraq. Codification was not limited to one area but spread across the Islamic world.
This historical context explains why many modern Arab legal systems have civil codes rooted in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Anticipate the factors behind codification
The passage introduces a list of factors (not fully quoted) that drove the codification movement. Later study will delve into these causes, but for now, note that the factors were numerous and significant.
Factors often include legal reform, unification, and Western influence.

Source Text

Second Subsection: The Expansion of the Scope of Codification and Its Factors. 5/16 - In this recent era, the scope of codification expanded boundlessly, and laws were issued on various subjects and aspects in all Islamic countries. Codification in the three branches (civil, criminal, and administrative) progressed rapidly. The most important aspects of civil codification—which alone is relevant to this historical account of fiqh—are manifested in three: commercial laws, land laws, and procedural laws such as the law of procedure and the civil procedure law called in our country the law of principles of legal proceedings. Jurisprudence (fiqh) became thoroughly interwoven and entangled with legal legislation in the Ottoman state and in the countries separated from it, such as Syria, Palestine, and Iraq, to the extent that there is hardly any chapter of fiqh that has not been subject to amendment or abrogation by law, whether in many or few of its rulings. 6/16 - Among the most important factors for the expansion of the codification movement were the following matters:
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage outlines the historical expansion of legal codification in the Muslim world during the late Ottoman and modern periods. The author states that in the 'last era' (presumably the late 13th/19th century CE), codification spread massively across all Islamic lands, covering civil, criminal, and administrative law. The focus here is civil codification, which has direct relevance to fiqh history. Three major types of civil codification are highlighted: commercial laws, land laws, and procedural laws (like civil procedure codes). The text then notes that fiqh became deeply entangled with statutory legislation in the Ottoman Empire and its successor states (Syria, Palestine, Iraq) to such an extent that nearly every chapter of fiqh saw at least some amendments or partial abrogation by modern codes. Finally, the passage introduces a list of factors responsible for this codification movement, which the author will enumerate next (not fully shown here). This historical context is crucial for understanding the shift from classical fiqh to modern legal systems in Muslim-majority countries.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnin): The process of compiling and enacting legal rules into a systematic code, often replacing or supplementing classical fiqh.
القوانين التجارية (al-qawanin al-tijariyya): Commercial laws regulating trade, sales, and business contracts.
القوانين العقارية (al-qawanin al-aqariyya): Land laws governing property ownership, registration, and transfer.
القوانين الأصولية (al-qawanin al-usuliyya): Procedural laws, including civil procedure and court regulations.

Key Takeaway
Modern codification in the late Ottoman era comprehensively penetrated fiqh, with civil, commercial, land, and procedural laws all being codified, altering the landscape for Islamic legal practice.

History and Development of Fiqh

Six Factors Behind the Expansion of Taqnin in the Late Ottoman Era
Historical analysis of factors behind expansion of legal codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the six factors that drove the expansion of legal codification (taqnin) in the late Ottoman period, and evaluate their significance for the practice of Hanafi iftaa.

Core Idea
Codification of Hanafi fiqh was driven by economic change, juristic stagnation, and the state’s need for legal certainty.

This passage from al-Zarqa's Madkhal analyzes why the late Ottoman era saw an unprecedented wave of legal codification. While often seen as Westernization, al-Zarqa demonstrates that internal factors – from new commercial practices to the stagnation of later Hanafi scholarship – compelled the state to enact codified laws that often superseded classical fiqh. Understanding these factors is crucial for the mufti today, as they explain the shift from pluralistic fatwa to a single codified law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of codification
Note the three branches (civil, criminal, administrative) and the focus on civil codification (commercial, land, procedural laws). Al-Zarqa states that codification expanded boundlessly.
The passage is about civil codification; criminal and administrative are mentioned but not detailed.
2
List each of the six factors
Extract from the text the six numbered factors: 1) evolution of economic relations, 2) need to reconsider contractual conditions, 3) government regulation of real estate, 4) need for procedural laws, 5) stagnation of later Hanafi scholarship, 6) compilation of the Majallah exclusively from Hanafi fiqh.
Factors 1-4 are external/societal; factors 5-6 are internal to the legal system.
3
Analyze the role of juristic stagnation (jumud)
Factor 5 is key: al-Zarqa says the later scholars became stagnant, losing the ability to produce new rulings. This created a vacuum that codification filled.
Jumud is a critical concept in Hanafi usul al-iftaa – it justifies the need for state intervention.
4
Connect to Hanafi iftaa practice
Before codification, muftis could choose among multiple opinions; after codification (like the Majallah), the state imposed one official ruling. This changed the mufti's role from independent jurist to applicator of codified law.
Al-Zarqa implicitly critiques the loss of ijtihad, but acknowledges the necessity.
5
Evaluate the significance for modern fatwa
Muftis in countries with codified law must know which areas are covered by taqnin and which remain open. Understanding the historical factors helps contextualize the current relationship between fiqh and state law.
This is the 'the_mufti_applies' perspective: the mufti's discretion is constrained by codification.

۲۲۹
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. The Majallah was very careful to refer topics and branches to their appropriate places and contexts. For example, the contract of mudarabah is mentioned by jurists in other than the section on partnerships, but the Majallah mentioned it in partnerships because mudarabah is nothing but a partnership contract with capital from one side and labor from the other. Similarly, the Majallah divided the issues of each book into basic and subsidiary topics such as definitions, conditions, rulings, etc., detailing them in clearly distinguished chapters, and attaching each issue to its subject. Second Subsection: Expansion of the Scope of Codification and Its Factors 5/16 - In this recent era, the scope of codification has expanded boundlessly, and laws have been enacted in various subjects and aspects in all Islamic countries. Codification in the three branches (civil, criminal, and administrative) proceeded rapidly. The most important aspects of civil codification – which alone is relevant to this historical discussion of fiqh – manifest in three: commercial laws, land laws, and procedural laws such as the law of procedure and the law of civil procedure called in our country the Law of Principles of Rights Proceedings. Legal legislation became deeply intertwined with the body of fiqh in the Ottoman Empire and in the countries separated from it, such as Syria, Palestine, and Iraq, to the extent that there is hardly a chapter of fiqh that has not been subject to legal amendment or abrogation in many or few of its rulings. 6/16 - Among the most important factors for the expansion of the codification movement are the following: 1. The evolution of economic relations internally and externally, and the generation of new types thereof in these countries, some local customary and some borrowed from European countries, such as types of legal companies and commercial methods like commission trade (commissio), insurance (soucoura), commitments, etc. 2. The need to consider contractual conditions, some types of which are prohibited by the prevailing Hanafi ijtihad and some other sharia ijtihads. 3. The governmental trend to tie real estate transactions and contracts to formal systems that place those contracts under government supervision for financial, legal, and political purposes (1), for which the land registry and its systems and office were established, and real estate contracts concluded outside it were considered ineffective. 4. The need to organize formal procedures, i.e., the methods and principles to be followed in transactions, references, lawsuits, arbitration of disputes, execution of judgments, registration of contracts, etc., for which procedural laws were established, such as the Law of Principles of Proceedings, the Law of Execution, and the Law of Notaries. 5. The stagnation of the later scholars and the paralysis of its productive movement that accompanied this great economic and civil development, as we explained in the discussion of the previous stage. The layers of renewers and extractors (mujaddidin and mukhrijin) who expanded and elevated fiqh in the past were cut off, and it ultimately turned into theoretical memorization study, not productive therapeutic. 6. The compilation of the Majallah of sharia rulings from the Hanafi school only, for then... (1) The financial purpose here is to impose tax on real estate contracts; the legal purpose is to prevent the owner from deceiving by selling his property to multiple buyers successively and collecting from them without each knowing of the prior purchase; and the political purpose is to limit foreign ownership of real estate. All this cannot be achieved without land systems. [229]
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's "Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm" is a historical and methodological analysis of the expansion of legal codification (taqnin) in the late Ottoman and post-Ottoman period. The author first highlights the Majallah's systematic approach to organizing fiqh topics, such as placing mudarabah under partnerships. He then lists six key factors that drove the codification movement: (1) new economic relations requiring modern commercial structures; (2) the need to accommodate contractual conditions that classical Hanafi ijtihad had restricted; (3) government regulation of real estate through formal registration; (4) procedural laws for litigation and enforcement; (5) the stagnation of later Hanafi scholarship, which became theoretical rather than productive; and (6) the compilation of the Majallah exclusively from Hanafi fiqh. The passage underscores that codification was not merely a Western import but a response to internal legal and societal needs. It also implicitly critiques the rigidity of later Hanafi jurists, whose inability to produce new solutions prompted legislative intervention. This sets the stage for understanding the modern relationship between fiqh and state law.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnīn): The process of enacting codified laws (qawānīn) by the state, often replacing or supplementing classical fiqh rulings.
المجلة (al-majallah): The Ottoman Majallah al-Ahkam al-'Adliyyah, a codification of Hanafi fiqh in the area of civil transactions, completed in 1876.
جمود (jumūd): Stagnation or rigidity of juristic thought, leading to an inability to produce new rulings for emerging issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as a factor for the expansion of taqnin in the passage?
A) Evolution of economic relations and new commercial practices
B) Need to consider contractual conditions prohibited by Hanafi ijtihad
C) Desire to unify Islamic law with Shafi'i jurisprudence
D) Government regulation of real estate transactions through formal systems

Key Takeaway
Codification of Hanafi fiqh in the late Ottoman era was a response to internal economic, legal, and scholarly crises, not merely a Western import.

Codification and Procedural Laws in the Seventh Period
Historical analysis and classification of laws

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between substantive and procedural laws and explain the necessity of procedural laws as administrative measures independent of fiqh.

Core Idea
Procedural laws are administrative necessities, not derived from fiqh, but essential for implementing substantive rulings.

This lesson explores the seventh period of Islamic fiqh development (1287-1354 AH), where social and economic changes, along with stagnation in later Hanafi scholarship, prompted the codification of laws. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa identifies six factors that led to this movement and classifies the resulting laws into two categories: substantive laws (qawanin al-ahkam) that establish legal rulings, and procedural laws (qawanin al-murasam) that govern formalities. Understanding this distinction is crucial for appreciating the relationship between shari'a and state legislation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six factors leading to codification
List and briefly describe each factor mentioned by al-Zarqa: economic changes, contractual needs, government regulation of real estate, procedural needs, scholarly stagnation, and the Majalla.
Focus on how each factor contributed to the need for codification.
2
Differentiate between qawanin al-ahkam and qawanin al-murasam
Using the author's definitions and examples (e.g., selling a minor's property vs. registration requirements), articulate the essential difference.
Qawanin al-ahkam are substantive; qawanin al-murasam are procedural.
3
Analyze the maxim about a single madhhab
Examine the statement 'A single madhhab cannot fulfill all needs' and its implication for codification across multiple schools.
This supports adopting rulings from other madhahib when needed.
4
Understand why procedural laws are a necessity
Explain al-Zarqa's argument that procedural laws are not based on fixed principles of justice but are policy measures within the competence of public authorities.
They are essential for organizing the application of substantive laws.

Chapter (16) The Seventh Period: 1287-1354 AH. 1 - Development of economic relations internally and externally, and generation of new types from them in these lands, some of which are local customary and some imported from European countries, such as types of legal companies and commercial methods like commission trade, insurance, contracts, etc. 2 - The need to consider contractual conditions, some types of which are prevented by the prevailing Hanafi ijtihad and other legal ijtihads. 3 - Governmental trend to link real estate transactions and contracts with formal systems that place those contracts under government supervision for financial, legal, and political purposes, for which the real estate registry, its systems, and office were established, and real estate contracts concluded outside it were considered non-productive of their effects. 4 - The need to organize formal procedures, i.e., the methods and principles that must be followed in transactions, references, lawsuits, settlement of disputes, execution of judgments, registration of contracts, etc., for which procedural laws were established, such as the Code of Civil Procedure, the Execution Law, and the Notary Law. 5 - The stagnation that accompanied this great civil economic development at the hands of later scholars and the paralysis of its generative movement, which we clarified in the discussion of the previous period, for the layers of renewers and extractors on whose hands fiqh expanded and rose in the past were cut off, then it finally turned into theoretical memorization study, not productive therapeutic study. 6 - The construction of the Majalla of Islamic Rulings from Hanafi fiqh only, for... (footnote: The financial purpose here is imposing tax on real estate contracts; the legal purpose is to prevent the owner of a property from selling it to multiple buyers successively and collecting from them without each knowing of the previous purchase; the political purpose is to restrict foreign ownership of real estate. All that cannot be achieved without real estate systems.) 229. Book Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics. A single madhhab, no matter how extensive, cannot fulfill all temporal needs and evolving interests that may lose their legislative treatment in that madhhab. And there exist other ijtihads. 7/16 - The opinion on the necessity of this codification and its lack alongside fiqh: Moreover, the laws created by those factors and necessities, like all laws that can exist here or elsewhere, are of two types: First: Substantive laws (Qawanin al-Ahkam). They impose and establish for each saying or act its legal effect and ruling. Second: Procedural laws (Qawanin al-Murasam or al-Usul). They outline and explain the methods that must be followed, and the forms according to which the action must be issued; they are also called 'formal laws'. For example, the permissibility or impermissibility of selling a minor's property falls under substantive laws. As for the necessity of conducting the contract in the real estate office and registering it in its register, it falls under procedural laws. Also of this kind is the Code of Civil Procedure, today called 'Code of Pleadings'; its provisions are formal provisions. All of this... 8/16 - And we conclude from this to an opinion that we hold regarding the relationship between Islamic fiqh and codification: We say: Procedural laws are an unavoidable necessity because they are not based on principles of rights and stable rules of justice; rather, they are measures related to the policy of legislation and the organization of its application more than they are related to those principles and rules. And this organization is a right of the public authorities and within their competence. 230.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a historical and methodological overview of the seventh period of Islamic fiqh development (1287-1354 AH), focusing on the factors that led to the codification of laws alongside traditional fiqh. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, identifies six key factors: (1) economic development introducing new commercial practices like companies, insurance, and commissions; (2) the need to consider contractual conditions that Hanafi ijtihad might restrict; (3) governmental regulation of real estate transactions through formal registration systems; (4) the need to organize procedural rules for transactions, litigation, and execution; (5) the stagnation of later Hanafi scholarship, which had become a theoretical memorization rather than a productive, problem-solving discipline; and (6) the creation of the Ottoman Majalla based solely on Hanafi fiqh. The passage then discusses the classification of laws into two types: substantive laws (qawanin al-ahkam), which establish the legal rulings themselves (e.g., whether selling a minor's property is permissible), and procedural laws (qawanin al-murasam or al-shakliyya), which govern the formalities and methods of implementation (e.g., registration requirements). The author argues that procedural laws are a necessity independent of fiqh because they are administrative measures related to legislative policy and organization, not derived from immutable principles of justice. He asserts that such regulation is within the competence of public authorities. This analysis is foundational for understanding the relationship between shari'a and state codification in modern contexts.

Key Terms
قوانين الأحكام (qawanin al-ahkam): Substantive laws that impose legal rulings for actions and statements.
قوانين المراسم (qawanin al-murasam): Procedural laws that prescribe methods and formalities for legal actions.
التقنين (al-taqnin): Codification of laws from fiqh sources into a structured legal code.
الاجتهاد الحنفي (al-ijtihad al-hanafi): The interpretive effort of Hanafi jurists to derive rulings from sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Mustafa al-Zarqa, procedural laws (qawanin al-murasam) are based on:
A) The Quran and Sunnah exclusively
B) Hanafi fiqh principles
C) Fixed principles of justice
D) Measures related to legislative policy and organization of application

Key Takeaway
Procedural laws are a necessary component of modern legal systems, distinct from substantive fiqh, and fall within the authority of the state as administrative measures.

Bay' al-Wafa: Deriving Rulings for a Hybrid Sale
Case study of bay' al-wafa as a hybrid contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Hanafi jurists applied qiyas and qawaid to derive rulings for bay' al-wafa, identifying the composite nature of its classification.

Core Idea
Fiqh adapts by classifying new contracts as composites of existing ones, assigning rulings from each part.

Bay' al-wafa emerged as a legal stratagem to avoid riba, combining elements of sale and pledge. Hanafi jurists did not reject it outright but analyzed its structure using qiyas and qawaid. The passage below shows how they derived its rulings by treating it as a hybrid of valid sale, invalid sale, and rahn, giving it from each contract what suited its purpose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the novel transaction
Read the description of bay' al-wafa: a sale with a condition to repurchase upon repayment of the price. Note its hybrid nature: it secures debt (like rahn) but allows the buyer to use the property (like a sale).
This transaction was known in Bukhara in the late 5th century AH.
2
Analyze its resemblance to existing contracts
The jurists identified three contracts it resembles: valid sale, invalid sale, and rahn. List their key features: valid sale transfers ownership irrevocably; invalid sale is void but may have partial effects; rahn is a security without transfer of beneficial ownership.
Each contract contributes different rulings.
3
Apply the Hanafi method of takhrij using qiyas
Using qiyas, the jurists assigned from each contract the rulings that align with the purpose of bay' al-wafa. For example, the creditor's right to use the property comes from the valid sale aspect, while the debtor's right to reclaim it upon payment comes from the rahn aspect.
The governing qawida here is 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' (matters are judged by their objectives).
4
Determine the composite ruling
The final ruling is a composite: it shares elements of all three contracts. The passage states that the jurists gave it from each contract 'what suits its purpose of rulings.'
This approach shows fiqh's adaptability.
