Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics
Insurance of risks known today as insurance contract or (soukra), and great economic project companies with tradable shares called 'the neglected companies' (1); and other transactions and economic situations brought about by the passage of time and foreign relations. So it is very easy to derive the necessary rulings for them through deduction and generation from the mother principles and jurisprudential rules by way of qiyas or istislah, just as the jurists previously derived rulings and generated them for many types of transactions that newly occurred in their times, such as bay' al-wafa, istisna', and al-rahn al-musta'ar (3) and other types of newly occurring transactions that have composite similarities requiring their rulings to be precisely derived from multiple rules; and as they dealt with and derived comprehensive rulings for the rights of qarār established on waqf properties according to a new custom (1).

(1) [... footnote about companies ...] (2) Here is a brief explanation of these three contracts: a - Bay' al-wafa is when a person in need of cash sells property on the condition that whenever he pays back the price, he reclaims the property. It differs from rahn in purpose: the purpose of rahn is merely security, while the purpose of bay' al-wafa is security of debt and the buyer's profit from the property. This sale occurred in Bukhara at the end of the fifth century AH, and finally the jurists' opinion settled that it resembles three contracts: valid sale, invalid sale, and rahn, so it was given from each of these contracts what suits its purpose of rulings. Evidence for this date is that Imam Najm al-Din Abu Hafs Umar ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi said in his fatwas: 'The sale known among the people of our time as a stratagem for riba and called bay' al-wafa is in reality a rahn, etc.' (see: end of sales from al-Durar). Najm al-Din al-Nasafi was born in 461 AH and died in 537 AH. b - Istisna' is purchasing what will be manufactured according to order, and it was known before Islam. Initially, the jurists were uncertain: is it a salaf sale (i.e., salam), requiring its conditions, or an ordinary sale, or a promise to sell? Then, is the sold item the substance to be made or the work? etc. Does the option of inspection after manufacture apply as in ordinary sale or not, to avoid harm to the manufacturer as he made it specifically for the customer? All of this the jurists settled with rulings suitable to the rules and needs.

Chapter (16) Seventh Period: 1287-1354 AH
It contains composite similarities whose rulings require precise derivations from many rules; and as they dealt with and derived comprehensive rulings for the rights of qarār established on waqf properties according to a new custom (1).
c - Al-rahn al-musta'ar is when a person borrows something from his friend not to use it, but to pawn it and take a loan against it.
It is established that borrowing (i'ara) is a non-binding gift contract, so the lender may revoke it whenever he wishes, while rahn is binding to safeguard the right of the mortgagee. Also, borrowed property is a trust not guaranteed by destruction, while mortgaged property is guaranteed. Thus, in al-rahn al-musta'ar there are conflicting implications. But the Hanafi jurists derived for it rulings from various rules containing accuracy, public interest, and safeguarding the rights of all three parties. (See: al-Majalla, article 372).
(1) Such as hukr, ijiratayn, mursad, kadik, khulu, etc., which are well-known in their place in the provisions of awqaf. An explanation of these rights in rem on awqaf will come in the third part of this series titled 'Introduction to the General Theory of Obligations in Islamic Jurisprudence' in the discussion of types of real right, so refer there, 4/6-4/07.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a discussion from a modern introduction to Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on the flexibility of fiqh in adapting to new economic transactions. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, argues that novel contracts such as insurance, joint-stock companies, bay' al-wafa, istisna', and al-rahn al-musta'ar can be handled by Hanafi jurists through deriving rulings from the foundational principles and legal maxims (qawaid) using methods like qiyas and istislah. He illustrates this with three historical examples: First, bay' al-wafa, a sale with a right of redemption that emerged in Bukhara as a means to circumvent riba. The Hanafi jurists analyzed it as a hybrid of valid sale, invalid sale, and rahn, assigning rulings from each to suit its purpose. Second, istisna', a contract for manufacturing goods, which was known pre-Islam but raised questions about its nature—whether it is salam, an ordinary sale, or a promise. Jurists resolved its rulings considering both rules and practical needs. Third, al-rahn al-musta'ar, where a borrowed item is pawned, creates conflicting obligations due to the differing natures of i'ara (revocable, trust) and rahn (binding, guarantee). Hanafi jurists derived balanced rulings from multiple maxims to protect all parties. The passage emphasizes that fiqh is not static but evolves through diligent application of principles to new circumstances.

Key Terms
بيع الوفاء (bay' al-wafa): A sale with a condition that when the seller returns the price, the buyer returns the property; used as a legal stratagem for securing a loan without interest.
القياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence to derive a ruling for a new case based on an existing case with a shared cause (illah).
القاعدة الفقهية (qawida fiqhiyya): A general legal maxim that summarizes many rulings in similar cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Why did Hanafi jurists rule that bay' al-wafa resembles three contracts (valid sale, invalid sale, and rahn)?
A) Because it was invented in the fifth century AH and they had no precedent.
B) Because it combines elements of payment, security, and profit, requiring composite rulings.
C) Because it was a stratagem for riba and thus fully invalid.
D) Because it is identical to rahn but was called a sale.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh adapts to new transactions by analyzing their components and assigning rulings from existing contracts through qiyas and qawaid.

Istisna': Manufacturing Contract and the Role of Custom
Explanation of istisna' as a distinct contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Hanafi jurists resolved the nature of istisna' using qiyas and custom, and identify the key differences between istisna' and salam.

Core Idea
Custom can override strict qiyas in Hanafi fiqh, allowing istisna' as a separate contract from salam.

Istisna' is a contract to manufacture an item according to specific order. It was known before Islam, but early jurists were uncertain about its legal nature: is it a type of salam (forward sale) or a regular sale? Hanafi jurists eventually distinguished it from salam based on custom and necessity, establishing its own rules. This lesson explores that process.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of istisna'
Istisna' is a contract to purchase something that will be manufactured. The buyer specifies the item, and the manufacturer agrees to make it. It is similar to salam but differs because the item is not yet in existence.
It was known before Islam.
2
Step
The passage says jurists were initially unsure: is it a salaf sale (salam), an ordinary sale, or a promise to sell? Also, is the sold item the raw material or the work? Does the option of inspection apply?
These questions needed resolution.
3
Apply the Hanafi method using qiyas and custom
The Hanafi jurists considered qiyas: if it is treated as salam, it would require immediate payment and strict conditions. But custom (urf) allowed payment at delivery or in installments. They prioritized custom over qiyas, making istisna' an exceptional contract permitted by necessity.
The qawida 'al-mustaqirr bi al-urf ka al-mustaqirr bi al-nass' applies.
4
Determine the rulings regarding options and payment
They ruled that the buyer has the option of inspection after manufacture (khiyar al-ru'yah) to protect against defects, but the manufacturer is not bound to accept returns for mere change of mind, as the item was made specifically for the buyer.
This balanced the interests of both parties.

Source Text

Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics
Insurance of risks known today as insurance contract or (soukra), and great economic project companies with tradable shares called 'the neglected companies' (1); and other transactions and economic situations brought about by the passage of time and foreign relations. So it is very easy to derive the necessary rulings for them through deduction and generation from the mother principles and jurisprudential rules by way of qiyas or istislah, just as the jurists previously derived rulings and generated them for many types of transactions that newly occurred in their times, such as bay' al-wafa, istisna', and al-rahn al-musta'ar (3) and other types of newly occurring transactions that have composite similarities requiring their rulings to be precisely derived from multiple rules; and as they dealt with and derived comprehensive rulings for the rights of qarār established on waqf properties according to a new custom (1).

(1) [... footnote about companies ...] (2) Here is a brief explanation of these three contracts: a - Bay' al-wafa is when a person in need of cash sells property on the condition that whenever he pays back the price, he reclaims the property. It differs from rahn in purpose: the purpose of rahn is merely security, while the purpose of bay' al-wafa is security of debt and the buyer's profit from the property. This sale occurred in Bukhara at the end of the fifth century AH, and finally the jurists' opinion settled that it resembles three contracts: valid sale, invalid sale, and rahn, so it was given from each of these contracts what suits its purpose of rulings. Evidence for this date is that Imam Najm al-Din Abu Hafs Umar ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi said in his fatwas: 'The sale known among the people of our time as a stratagem for riba and called bay' al-wafa is in reality a rahn, etc.' (see: end of sales from al-Durar). Najm al-Din al-Nasafi was born in 461 AH and died in 537 AH. b - Istisna' is purchasing what will be manufactured according to order, and it was known before Islam. Initially, the jurists were uncertain: is it a salaf sale (i.e., salam), requiring its conditions, or an ordinary sale, or a promise to sell? Then, is the sold item the substance to be made or the work? etc. Does the option of inspection after manufacture apply as in ordinary sale or not, to avoid harm to the manufacturer as he made it specifically for the customer? All of this the jurists settled with rulings suitable to the rules and needs.

Chapter (16) Seventh Period: 1287-1354 AH
It contains composite similarities whose rulings require precise derivations from many rules; and as they dealt with and derived comprehensive rulings for the rights of qarār established on waqf properties according to a new custom (1).
c - Al-rahn al-musta'ar is when a person borrows something from his friend not to use it, but to pawn it and take a loan against it.
It is established that borrowing (i'ara) is a non-binding gift contract, so the lender may revoke it whenever he wishes, while rahn is binding to safeguard the right of the mortgagee. Also, borrowed property is a trust not guaranteed by destruction, while mortgaged property is guaranteed. Thus, in al-rahn al-musta'ar there are conflicting implications. But the Hanafi jurists derived for it rulings from various rules containing accuracy, public interest, and safeguarding the rights of all three parties. (See: al-Majalla, article 372).
(1) Such as hukr, ijiratayn, mursad, kadik, khulu, etc., which are well-known in their place in the provisions of awqaf. An explanation of these rights in rem on awqaf will come in the third part of this series titled 'Introduction to the General Theory of Obligations in Islamic Jurisprudence' in the discussion of types of real right, so refer there, 4/6-4/07.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a discussion from a modern introduction to Islamic jurisprudence, focusing on the flexibility of fiqh in adapting to new economic transactions. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, argues that novel contracts such as insurance, joint-stock companies, bay' al-wafa, istisna', and al-rahn al-musta'ar can be handled by Hanafi jurists through deriving rulings from the foundational principles and legal maxims (qawaid) using methods like qiyas and istislah. He illustrates this with three historical examples: First, bay' al-wafa, a sale with a right of redemption that emerged in Bukhara as a means to circumvent riba. The Hanafi jurists analyzed it as a hybrid of valid sale, invalid sale, and rahn, assigning rulings from each to suit its purpose. Second, istisna', a contract for manufacturing goods, which was known pre-Islam but raised questions about its nature—whether it is salam, an ordinary sale, or a promise. Jurists resolved its rulings considering both rules and practical needs. Third, al-rahn al-musta'ar, where a borrowed item is pawned, creates conflicting obligations due to the differing natures of i'ara (revocable, trust) and rahn (binding, guarantee). Hanafi jurists derived balanced rulings from multiple maxims to protect all parties. The passage emphasizes that fiqh is not static but evolves through diligent application of principles to new circumstances.

Key Terms
الاستصناع (al-istisna'): A contract to purchase something that will be manufactured according to the buyer's specifications, known pre-Islam.
السلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid fully in advance for a defined commodity to be delivered later.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection, allowing the buyer to return an item if not satisfied upon seeing it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did Hanafi jurists resolve the nature of istisna'?
A) They ruled it is exactly salam and must follow its conditions.
B) They allowed it as an exceptional contract based on custom and need.
C) They prohibited it because the item does not exist.
D) They considered it a mere promise, not a binding contract.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh validates istisna' as a distinct contract based on custom, overriding strict qiyas to meet practical needs.

10 min
Al-Rahn al-Musta'ar: Reconciling Conflicting Rules via Qawaid
Case study of conflicting rules resolved via qawaid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Hanafi jurists used multiple qawaid to derive balanced rulings for al-rahn al-musta'ar, reconciling the conflicting natures of i'ara and rahn.

Core Idea
Conflicting legal principles can be reconciled by applying multiple qawaid to protect all parties' rights.

Al-rahn al-musta'ar is a situation where a person borrows an item not for use but to pawn it for a loan. This creates conflicting legal rules: i'ara (borrowing) is revocable and a trust (amana), while rahn (pledge) is binding and guaranteed. Hanafi jurists derived specific rulings from various qawaid to balance the rights of the borrower, lender, and original owner. This lesson explores that derivation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conflicting legal natures
I'ara (borrowing) is a non-binding gift contract, revocable at any time. The borrowed item is a trust (amana) not guaranteed if destroyed. Rahn (pledge) is binding to protect the creditor's right. The pawned item is guaranteed (damān) if destroyed. These two natures conflict.
The conflict is between trust and guarantee, revocability and bindingness.
2
Identify the three parties and their interests
The three parties are: the borrower (who borrows to pawn), the lender (who loans against the pawn), and the original owner (who lent the item). Each has rights: the owner can revoke, the lender has a security interest, the borrower wants the loan.
The jurists had to protect each party.
3
Apply the Hanafi method using multiple qawaid
The jurists applied qawaid such as 'al-ajr bi al-daman' (profit comes with liability), 'al-umur bi maqasidiha' (matters are judged by intentions), and 'dar' al-mafasid muqaddam 'ala jalb al-masalih' (preventing harm is prioritized). They derived rulings such as the borrower's liability for loss, the lender's priority, and the owner's limited right to revoke.
These maxims helped harmonize the conflicting rules.
4
Derive the balanced rulings
Outcome: the borrower is liable if the pawned item is lost (as in rahn), but the owner cannot revoke the loan of the item once it is pawned until the debt is paid, to protect the lender. The lender's right is secured, and the borrower must compensate the owner if the item is lost.
These rulings are detailed in al-Majalla article 372.

Source Text

Key Terms

العقد التبرع (al-aqd al-tabarru'): A gratuitous contract, like i'ara (loan for use), which is non-binding and revocable.

History and Development of Fiqh

Historical Background of the Majalla: Need for Codification
Historical narrative on codification and the Fatawa Hindiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical motivations for codifying Hanafi rulings and identify the role of the Fatawa Hindiyya in paving the way for the Majalla.

Core Idea
The Majalla was not the first attempt; Ibn al-Muqaffa' and Fatawa Hindiyya foreshadowed its need.

This lesson traces the early calls for unifying judicial rulings in Islamic history, focusing on the Abbasid-era proposal of Ibn al-Muqaffa' and the later compilation of the Fatawa Hindiyya. Understanding this background clarifies why the Ottoman Majalla became necessary and how it drew on prior efforts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the early proposal
Find who proposed unifying judicial rulings and to which caliph. This shows the ancient awareness of the need for standardization.
2
Understand the problem
Recognize the issue of contradictory judgments by mujtahid judges in the Abbasid era, which motivated the proposal.
3
Recognize later developments
After ijtihad stopped, madhhabs multiplied opinions, causing further need for organization. Note the layers of scholars (mukharrijun, munaqqihun, etc.).
4
Connect to Fatawa Hindiyya
The Fatawa Hindiyya served as a comprehensive compilation that organized Hanafi rulings, paving the way for the Majalla.
5
Reflect on significance
Consider why this history matters for a mufti today: knowing the sources and their hierarchy is essential for authoritative fatwa.

Supplement to Chapter Sixteen: The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyya: Its history, description, evaluation, and destiny.
16/10 - We will soon see in the second appendix to Chapter Twenty-Two that the feeling of the necessity of codifying rulings from among the multiple juristic ijtihads is an ancient feeling dating back to the early ages of ijtihad.
The discrepancy in the judgments issued by judges in the Abbasid era, as judges were mujtahids, each judging according to his own opinion and ijtihad on the legal ruling, was a motive for Ibn al-Muqaffa' to propose to the Abbasid Caliph al-Mansur a method to unify these judgments on the same subject where contradictory judgments were issued (1).
11/16 - The Fatawa Hindiyya paved the way for the Majallah.
Then, after the cessation of the movement of ijtihad and the restriction to the fiqh schools that arose, and around each of them followers and imitators emerged, each school occupied zones of influence where it had dominance, and layers of 'mukharrijun' (extractors) emerged in each school, and layers of revisers, correctors, and preferrers, and the juristic opinions within a single school multiplied on each subject due to the multiplicity of aspects of analogy and its probabilities. Books and compilations increased in each school, and there came to be juristic opinions and preferences in some school books that did not exist in others. And those school books were...
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This supplement discusses the historical context that led to the creation of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, the Ottoman civil code. It notes that the need for codification was felt early, with Ibn al-Muqaffa' proposing to Caliph al-Mansur a method to unify judicial rulings to avoid contradictions. After the closure of ijtihad, each madhhab developed a large body of rulings, and within each madhhab, many opinions existed. The Fatawa Hindiyya (a comprehensive Hanafi legal work) served as an important stepping stone that prepared the way for the Majalla. The footnote references Ibn al-Muqaffa's 'Risalah fi al-Sahabah' for further details.

Key Terms
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-‘Adliyya): The Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi fiqh, compiled in the 19th century
الفتاوى الهندية (al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya): A comprehensive collection of Hanafi fatwas compiled under the patronage of Emperor Aurangzeb Alamgir

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It paved the way by compiling Hanafi rulings
C) It was a critic of the Majalla
D) It had no relation

Key Takeaway
The Majalla was built upon earlier efforts, with the Fatawa Hindiyya being a pivotal compilation that organized Hanafi rulings for codification.

Ibn al-Muqaffa's Proposal: The Early Call for Legal Unification
Narrates the early awareness of codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the historical problem of contradictory judgments in the Abbasid era and explain Ibn al-Muqaffa's proposed solution, connecting it to later Hanafi codification efforts.

Core Idea
The need to unify legal rulings arose early in Islamic history due to conflicting judicial decisions.

This passage traces the earliest awareness of the need for codification in Islamic law. It begins with the Abbasid period, where mujtahid judges each issued rulings according to their own ijtihad, leading to contradictory judgments on identical cases. Ibn al-Muqaffa' proposed a method to unify these rulings, an early precursor to later systematic codification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The passage mentions that judges in the Abbasid era were independent mujtahids. What was the consequence? They issued contradictory judgments on the same subject.
2
Locate the proposed solution
Who suggested unifying the rulings? Ibn al-Muqaffa' proposed to Caliph al-Mansur a method to unify these judgments.
3
Connect to later history
The passage states that this was an early awareness of codification. How does this link to the later Hanafi projects? It shows the persistent need.
4
Note the Hanafi significance
Though the proposal wasn't implemented, it highlights that the challenge of multiple opinions existed even before the schools crystallized, and the Hanafi school later undertook major codification projects.

١٦ / ١٠ - سنرى قريباً في الملحق الثاني بالفصل الثاني والعشرين أن الشعور بضرورة تقنين الأحكام من بين الاجتهادات الفقهية المتعددة شعور قديم منذ عصور الاجتهاد الأولى.
وكان اختلاف الأقضية التي يصدرها القضاة في العصر العباسي، إذ كان القضاة مجتهدين يقضي كل منهم برأيه واجتهاده في الحكم الشرعي حافزاً لابن المقفع أن يقترح على الخليفة المنصور العباسي طريقة لتوحيد هذه الأقضية في الموضوع الواحد الذي كانت تصدر فيه أقضية متناقضة(۱)
١١/١٦ - الفتاوى الهندية مهدت الطريق إلى المجلة.
ثم بعد توقف حركة الاجتهاد والاكتفاء بالمذاهب الفقهية التي قامت وقام من حول كل منها أتباع مقلدون احتل كل مذهب مناطق نفوذ كانت له فيها السيادة، ونشأت طبقات المخرجين في كل مذهب، وطبقات المنقحين والمصححين والمرجحين، وتعددت الآراء الفقهية ضمن المذهب الواحد في كل موضوع لتعدد وجوه القياس واحتمالاته، وكثرت الكتب والتأليف في كل مذهب وأصبح يوجد من الآراء الفقهية والترجيحات في بعض كتب المذهب ما لا يوجد في سواه، وكانت تلك الكتب المذهبية

٢٣٥
الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
كلها مخطوطة ومتفرقة نسخها في البلدان والخزائن لا تصل إليها كل يد بات من المتعذر أن يستطيع الفقيه المذهبي نفسه أن يحيط بكل ما في كتب مذهبه المتفرقة من آراء وترجيحات وكان المذهب الحنفي في طليعة المذاهب الفقهية التي تتعذر الإحاطة بما في مؤلفاتها حول كل مسألة السعة رقعة نفوذه، وكثرة مؤلفاته وغزارة فروعه وتشعب الآراء، واختلاف التصحيح والترجيح فيها، فتمخضت الحاجة الفقهية بعد طول المدى عن محاولة شبه تقنينية في المذهب الحنفي في عهد الملك (الامبراطور) المغولي العظيم في الهند محمد أورنك زيب ومعناه : زينة العرش الملقب بعالمكير أي) فاتح العالم فكان الكتاب الفقهي العظيم الشأن في المذهب. الحنفي الذي سمي : الفتاوى الهندية (أو العالمكيرية).
وقصة هذا الكتاب باختصار : أنَّ هذا الملك المؤمن التقي الذي وهـ نفسه للإسلام ومجده، وتسنّم عرش دهلي في عام / ١٦٥٨ ميلادية - أي حوالي / ١٠٦٩ هجرية، وكان عالماً فقيهاً - أراد تعبيد الطريق إلى الفقه ليسهل الرجوع إلى أحكامه في الفتيا، ويتيسر التقنين منها لشؤون الإدارة والقضاء، فألف في سنة ١٠٧٣ هـ «مجلس الفقهاء برئاسة الفقيه الملا نظام الدین برهان ،بوری ومعه ما يقرب من أربعين فقيهاً من أكابر فقهاء. عصره منهم القضاة والمفتون والعلماء الراسخون وهَيَّأ لهم مكتبة حافلة أمر بجمعها من جميع الأقطار، وأجرى لهم جراية كافية من الرزق ليفرغوا إلى العمل المطلوب وأمرهم أن يجمعوا أحكام الفقه في شتى الموضوعات، ولا سيما ما يتصل بالمعاملات والقضاء والإدارة، والتوثيق، تلك الأحكام المبعثرة في شتى كتب الفقه مما لا تصل إليه كل يد، وأن يودعوها في كتاب جامع باللغة العربية تُعرض فيه الأحكام الفقهية بتنسيق وترتيب وتبويب وتنقيح يجعلها سهلة المتناول. وذلك مع عزو كل حكم إلى مرجعه المأخوذ منه . وقد رَصَد لهم ميزانية كافية لتمويل المشروع.
وقد انقسموا لذلك إلى أربع زمر ترأس الأولى الشيخ وجيه الدين
٢٣٦
Appendix to Chapter Sixteen: The Majallat al-Ahkam al-‘Adliyyah: Its History, Description, Evaluation, and Fate. 16/10 - We will soon see in the second appendix to Chapter Twenty-Two that the awareness of the necessity to codify rulings among the multiple juristic ijtihads is an ancient awareness dating back to the early ages of ijtihad. The divergence of judgments issued by judges in the Abbasid era, since judges were mujtahids each ruling according to his own opinion and ijtihad on the legal ruling, was a stimulus for Ibn al-Muqaffa‘ to suggest to the Caliph al-Mansur al-‘Abbasi a method to unify these judgments on the single subject for which contradictory judgments were issued. 11/16 - The Fatwa al-Hindiyya paved the way for the Majallah. Then after the cessation of the movement of ijtihad and contentment with the juristic schools that arose, and around each of them arose followers who were blind imitators, each school occupied zones of influence in which it had sovereignty. Layers of ‘mukharrijūn’ (extractors) emerged in each school, and layers of revisers, correctors, and preferrers; juristic opinions multiplied within a single school on every subject due to the multiplicity of aspects of analogy and its possibilities. Books and compositions multiplied in each school, and there came to be found in some school books juristic opinions and preferences that did not exist in others. Those school books were all manuscripts and scattered copies in countries and libraries, not accessible to every hand. It became impossible for even a school jurist himself to encompass all the opinions and preferences in his school’s scattered books. The Hanafi school was at the forefront of the juristic schools that were difficult to encompass due to the breadth of its influence, the abundance of its compositions, the profusion of its branches, the multiplicity of opinions, and the divergence of correction and preference within it. So the juristic need, after a long time, gave birth to a quasi-codification attempt in the Hanafi school during the reign of the great Mughal emperor in India, Muhammad Aurangzeb (meaning: ornament of the throne), titled ‘Alamgir (i.e., conqueror of the world). So the great and significant juristic book in the Hanafi school was produced, which was named: al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya (or al-‘Alamgiriyya). The story of this book in brief: that this faithful and pious king, who devoted himself to Islam and its glory, ascended the throne of Delhi in the year 1658 CE - i.e., around 1069 AH - and he was a knowledgeable jurist - wanted to pave the way to fiqh to make it easy to refer to its rulings for ifta’ and to facilitate codification from it for administrative and judicial affairs. So in the year 1073 AH he formed a council of jurists under the chairmanship of the jurist Mulla Nizam al-Din Burhanpuri, along with nearly forty jurists from the senior jurists of his time, including judges, muftis, and deeply rooted scholars. He prepared for them a rich library which he ordered to be collected from all regions, and gave them sufficient stipends so that they could devote themselves to the required work. He ordered them to collect the rulings of fiqh on various subjects, especially those related to transactions, judiciary, administration, and documentation - those rulings scattered in various books of fiqh that are not accessible to every hand - and to deposit them in a comprehensive book in the Arabic language, presenting the juristic rulings with organization, arrangement, chaptering, and revision to make them easy to access, while attributing each ruling to its source from which it was taken. He allocated for them a sufficient budget to finance the project. They divided into four groups for this purpose, the first headed by Sheikh Wajih al-Din. 236
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents a historical narrative tracing the origins of the Majallat al-Ahkam al-‘Adliyyah, the famous Ottoman codification of Hanafi fiqh in transactions. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by noting that the need for codification was recognized early in Islamic history. He cites the example of the Abbasid era, where judges, being independent mujtahids, often issued contradictory rulings on the same matter. This prompted Ibn al-Muqaffa‘ to propose a method of unification to Caliph al-Mansur—an early call for legal uniformity. The passage then jumps to the later period after the closure of ijtihad, when the mujtahid schools became entrenched, and followers emerged as blind imitators (muqallidun). Each school occupied distinct regions. Within each school, layers of scholars emerged: mukharrjūn (those who extracted rulings), munaqqihūn (revisers), musahhijūn (correctors), and murajjihūn (those who gave preference). This led to a proliferation of opinions within the same school on every matter, due to multiple potential qiyas (analogical deductions) and differences in preferment. Books multiplied, but they were all manuscript copies scattered across libraries, making it impossible for even a school jurist to master all the rulings and preferences of his own school. The Hanafi school, being the most widespread in terms of influence and possessing the greatest volume of writings, faced this difficulty acutely. The juristic need gave birth to a quasi-codification effort under Emperor Aurangzeb ‘Alamgir of the Mughal Empire. He was a pious and knowledgeable ruler, and in 1073 AH (c. 1662 CE) he assembled a council of nearly forty scholars, including judges and muftis, under the chairmanship of Mulla Nizam al-Din al-Burhanpuri. He provided a rich library collected from all regions, stipends, and a budget, and instructed them to compile all the scattered rulings of the Hanafi school—especially those on transactions, judiciary, administration, and documentation—into a single comprehensive Arabic work, with each ruling attributed to its source. The scholars divided into four groups for this purpose, the first headed by Sheikh Wajih al-Din. This work became known as al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya (or al-‘Alamgiriyya) and it paved the way for the later Ottoman Majallah. The passage highlights both the historical need for codification and the significant preliminary project that directly influenced the Majallah.

Key Terms
الفتاوى الهندية (al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya): A comprehensive compilation of Hanafi legal rulings produced under Emperor Aurangzeb Alamgir in the late 11th/17th century, covering various fields especially mu'amalat.
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyyah): The Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi fiqh, codified in the 19th century for transactions and procedures.
تقنين (taqnin): The process of codification, i.e., gathering authoritative rulings into a systematic legal code.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What problem did Ibn al-Muqaffa' identify in the Abbasid judiciary?
A) Lack of qualified judges
B) Contradictory judgments from different judges
C) Use of non-Hanafi sources
D) Overabundance of fatwas

Key Takeaway
The need to codify and unify Hanafi rulings was recognized centuries before the Majallah, with early proposals by Ibn al-Muqaffa' and a major precursor in the Fatawa Hindiyya.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
The Fatawa al-Hindiyya: Aurangzeb's Paving of the Path to the Majallah
Narrates the Fatawa Hindiyya project as a response to scattered rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical circumstances that led to the compilation of the Fatawa Hindiyya and describe its methodology and significance for Hanafi ifta.

Core Idea
Emperor Aurangzeb’s Fatawa Hindiyya was a monumental collaborative project that collected dispersed Hanafi rulings, paving the way for the Majallah.

After the closure of ijtihad, the Hanafi school expanded enormously, producing layers of scholars and a vast, scattered corpus. Access to rulings became nearly impossible. This lesson explores how the Mughal emperor Aurangzeb 'Alamgir initiated a comprehensive compilation project that became a cornerstone for later codification.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem of scattered rulings
The passage describes how Hanafi opinions multiplied due to different qiyas and preferences, and books were manuscripts in scattered libraries.
2
Identify the royal patron
Who sponsored the project? Emperor Aurangzeb, a knowledgeable jurist.
3
Describe the project structure
He formed a council of 40 scholars under Mulla Nizam al-Din, provided a library, budget, and stipends.
4
Define the scope and method
They were to collect rulings on all subjects, especially transactions, judiciary, administration, and documentation, in Arabic, each attributed to its source.
5
Recognize its legacy
The work, al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya, paved the way for the later Ottoman Majallah.

Key Terms
الفتاوى الهندية (al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya): A comprehensive compilation of Hanafi legal rulings produced under Emperor Aurangzeb Alamgir in the late 11th/17th century, covering various fields especially mu'amalat.
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-'Adliyyah): The Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi fiqh, codified in the 19th century for transactions and procedures.
تقنين (taqnin): The process of codification, i.e., gathering authoritative rulings into a systematic legal code.
المخرجون (al-mukharrijun): Scholars who extract (takhrij) rulings from the foundational texts of the school, often deriving new rulings based on the school's principles.
المنقحون (al-munaqqihun): Scholars who revise and refine the rulings of the school, clarifying the preferred positions.
المصححون (al-musahhijun): Scholars who correct and verify the accuracy of transmitted rulings.
المرجحون (al-murajjihun): Scholars who give preference (tarjih) to one opinion over others within the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the primary instruction given to the jurists convened by Aurangzeb?
A) Create new rulings
B) Collect and compile existing rulings with attribution
C) Translate Persian texts
D) Produce a commentary on the Quran

Key Takeaway
The Fatawa Hindiyya exemplifies a state-sponsored project to systematically compile Hanafi rulings, serving as a critical precursor to the modern codification of Islamic law.

The Compilation of al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya: A Milestone in Hanafi Fatwa Literature
Historical narrative about the need and process of compiling the Fatawa Hindiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the historical context, compilation methodology, and authoritative status of al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The Fatawa Hindiyya was a quasi-codification of Hanafi fiqh, compiled under imperial patronage, and remains a primary reference for fatwa in the Indian subcontinent.

This lesson explores the story behind one of the most important Hanafi fatwa collections: al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya (also known as al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya). Commissioned by Emperor Aurangzeb Alamgir, this work was produced by a council of scholars to make Hanafi rulings accessible and authoritative for administration and judiciary. Understanding its compilation helps you appreciate how later Hanafi authorities organized and transmitted fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Context of the Compilation
Identify the problem that necessitated the work: the vastness and inaccessibility of Hanafi literature made it difficult for jurists to find rulings.
The passage mentions that even a madhhab jurist could not encompass all scattered opinions and preferences.
2
The Commission and the Council
Emperor Aurangzeb commissioned a council of around forty scholars headed by Mulla Nizam al-Din. They were divided into four groups, each covering a quarter of the work.
The four group leaders are named: Shaykh Wajih al-Din, Shaykh Jalal al-Din, Shaykh al-Qadi Muhammad Hasan, and Mulla Hamid Junpuri.
3
Methodology and Scope
The scholars collected rulings from scattered Hanafi works, organized them by topics (especially transactions, judiciary, administration), and attributed each ruling to its source. The work is exclusively Hanafi, not including other madhhabs.
This made the work a practical reference for fatwa and qada.
4
Supervision and Completion
The emperor personally reviewed progress daily; the council presented completed sections each day. The rate was about four pages per day, and the project took eight years.
The imperial supervision ensured accountability and thoroughness.
5
Authority and Impact
After completion, the emperor issued a decree making the rulings enforceable in all courts. Today, it is printed in six volumes and considered one of the most important authoritative references in the Hanafi school.
Its authority weight is that of a later authoritative fatwa collection (fatawa mutakhkhirin), widely followed in the Indian subcontinent.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
كلها مخطوطة ومتفرقة نسخها في البلدان والخزائن لا تصل إليها كل يد بات من المتعذر أن يستطيع الفقيه المذهبي نفسه أن يحيط بكل ما في كتب مذهبه المتفرقة من آراء وترجيحات وكان المذهب الحنفي في طليعة المذاهب الفقهية التي تتعذر الإحاطة بما في مؤلفاتها حول كل مسألة السعة رقعة نفوذه، وكثرة مؤلفاته وغزارة فروعه وتشعب الآراء، واختلاف التصحيح والترجيح فيها، فتمخضت الحاجة الفقهية بعد طول المدى عن محاولة شبه تقنينية في المذهب الحنفي في عهد الملك (الامبراطور) المغولي العظيم في الهند محمد أورنك زيب ومعناه : زينة العرش الملقب بعالمكير أي) فاتح العالم فكان الكتاب الفقهي العظيم الشأن في المذهب. الحنفي الذي سمي : الفتاوى الهندية (أو العالمكيرية).
وقصة هذا الكتاب باختصار : أنَّ هذا الملك المؤمن التقي الذي وهـ نفسه للإسلام ومجده، وتسنّم عرش دهلي في عام / ١٦٥٨ ميلادية - أي حوالي / ١٠٦٩ هجرية، وكان عالماً فقيهاً - أراد تعبيد الطريق إلى الفقه ليسهل الرجوع إلى أحكامه في الفتيا، ويتيسر التقنين منها لشؤون الإدارة والقضاء، فألف في سنة ١٠٧٣ هـ «مجلس الفقهاء برئاسة الفقيه الملا نظام الدین برهان ،بوری ومعه ما يقرب من أربعين فقيهاً من أكابر فقهاء. عصره منهم القضاة والمفتون والعلماء الراسخون وهَيَّأ لهم مكتبة حافلة أمر بجمعها من جميع الأقطار، وأجرى لهم جراية كافية من الرزق ليفرغوا إلى العمل المطلوب وأمرهم أن يجمعوا أحكام الفقه في شتى الموضوعات، ولا سيما ما يتصل بالمعاملات والقضاء والإدارة، والتوثيق، تلك الأحكام المبعثرة في شتى كتب الفقه مما لا تصل إليه كل يد، وأن يودعوها في كتاب جامع باللغة العربية تُعرض فيه الأحكام الفقهية بتنسيق وترتيب وتبويب وتنقيح يجعلها سهلة المتناول. وذلك مع عزو كل حكم إلى مرجعه المأخوذ منه . وقد رَصَد لهم ميزانية كافية لتمويل المشروع.
وقد انقسموا لذلك إلى أربع زمر ترأس الأولى الشيخ وجيه الدين وعهد إليها بالربع الأول من العمل، وترأس الثانية الشيخ جلال الدين وعهد إليها بالربع الثاني، وترأس الثالثة الشيخ القاضي محمد حسن وعُهد إليها بالربع الثالث منه وترأس الرابعة الملا حميد جونبوري وعُهد إليها بالربع الرابع والكل يعملون تحت رئاسة الشيخ نظام الدين، فأتموا في ثماني سنوات هذا المشروع الجليل الذي تمخض عن كتاب الفتاوى الهندية الآنفة الذكر.

وهذه المجموعة (الفتاوى العالمكيرية مطبوعة متداولة اليوم في ستة مجلدات ضخمة، وتعتبر من أعظم وأهم المراجع الموثوقة المعتمدة في المذهب الحنفي.
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics
All of it is handwritten and scattered, copies in countries and libraries not accessible to every hand. It became impossible for even a madhhab jurist himself to encompass all the opinions and preferences in his madhhab's scattered books. The Hanafi school was at the forefront of the schools that are difficult to survey comprehensively due to the expanse of its influence, the abundance of its works, the richness of its branches, the diversity of opinions, and the differences in correction and preference therein. So the juristic need produced over a long period an attempt at quasi-codification in the Hanafi school during the reign of the great Mughal emperor in India, Muhammad Aurangzeb (meaning 'Ornament of the Throne'), called Alamgir (i.e., Conqueror of the World). Hence the great juristic book in the Hanafi school called al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya (or al-Alamgiriyya).
The story of this book in brief: This pious, faithful king who devoted himself to Islam and its glory ascended the throne of Delhi in 1658 CE (about 1069 AH) and was a scholar-jurist. He wanted to pave the way to fiqh to ease reference to its rulings for fatwa and to facilitate codification for administration and judiciary. So in 1073 AH he formed a council of jurists headed by the jurist Mulla Nizam al-Din Burhan Buri, along with nearly forty jurists from the greatest scholars of his time, among them judges, muftis, and firmly grounded scholars. He prepared for them an abundant library that he ordered collected from all regions and provided them with sufficient sustenance to devote themselves to the required work. He ordered them to collect juristic rulings on various subjects, especially those related to transactions, judiciary, administration, and documentation—those rulings scattered in various fiqh books not accessible to every hand—and to deposit them in a comprehensive book in Arabic presenting juristic rulings with organization, arrangement, chaptering, and refinement to make them easy to access, along with attributing every ruling to its source. He allocated a sufficient budget to finance the project.
They divided into four groups: the first headed by Shaykh Wajih al-Din, entrusted with the first quarter of the work; the second headed by Shaykh Jalal al-Din, entrusted with the second quarter; the third headed by Shaykh al-Qadi Muhammad Hasan, entrusted with the third quarter; and the fourth headed by Mulla Hamid Junpuri, entrusted with the fourth quarter. All worked under the presidency of Shaykh Nizam al-Din. They completed this noble project in eight years, resulting in the aforementioned book al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya.
Since the Hanafi school was the prevalent school throughout the empire, this work was confined to the rulings of the Hanafi school. Al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya did not include the differences and ijtihad opinions from other schools. The king himself supervised the work and followed it daily; the head of the council would present to him the completed work from the previous day. The king would daily offer his observations and criticism and point out any shortcomings to be completed. The progress was at a rate of approximately four pages per day.
After completing this great comprehensive book of the Shari'a law, represented by Hanafi ijtihad, and its release for copying and general distribution—like printing and distribution in our time—the king issued an imperial decree (royal will) to put its rulings into effect throughout the kingdom and to implement them in the judicial circuits (1).
This collection (al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya) is printed and circulated today in six huge volumes and is considered one of the greatest and most important authoritative and reliable references in the Hanafi school.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's work provides a historical account of the compilation of the Fatawa Hindiyya (also known as Fatawa Alamgiriyya), a monumental reference in the Hanafi madhhab. The author explains the background: the vastness of Hanafi literature made it difficult even for qualified jurists to access all rulings. Under Emperor Aurangzeb Alamgir (r. 1658–1707), a council of forty scholars led by Mulla Nizam al-Din was formed to compile a comprehensive, well-organized Arabic fiqh work focusing on Hanafi rulings. The work took eight years, with scholars divided into four groups covering different quarters. The emperor personally supervised the project, and after completion, it was made the official code for courts across the Mughal Empire. The Fatawa Hindiyya remains a trusted and authoritative reference in the Hanafi school, printed in six volumes. Note that it exclusively contains Hanafi positions and does not document intra-madhhab disputes or other schools' views. Its authority weight is that of a later authoritative fatwa collection (Fatawa collection) from the Mughal period, widely followed in the subcontinent.

Key Terms
الفتاوى الهندية (al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya): The comprehensive Hanafi fiqh compilation commissioned by Emperor Aurangzeb, also known as al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya.

Key Takeaway
Al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya is a landmark compilation of Hanafi fiqh that streamlined legal reference for fatwa and judiciary under Mughal rule.

History and Development of Fiqh

Two Major Codifications in Hanafi Law: Fatawa Hindiyya and Majalla
Historical overview of two major Hanafi codification projects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical context and significance of al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya and Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, and distinguish between a traditional fiqh compilation and a modern codification.

Core Idea
The Fatawa Hindiyya is a comprehensive Hanafi fiqh collection, while the Majalla is a modern civil code: both shaped the application of Hanafi law.

This lesson explores two monumental works in Hanafi legal history: the Fatawa al-Hindiyya, compiled under Emperor Aurangzeb, and the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, the Ottoman civil code. Understanding their differences helps appreciate the evolution from traditional fiqh compilations to state-enacted codifications. We will examine the methods, scope, and impact of each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two works
Name the two codifications discussed: al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya (also called al-Fatawa al-'Alamgiriyya) and Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya. Note their empires: Mughal and Ottoman respectively.
2
Understand the compilation process of the Fatawa Hindiyya
Describe how four scholarly committees worked under Sheikh Nizam al-Din for eight years, with Emperor Aurangzeb supervising daily. Note that it exclusively contained Hanafi rulings and was enforced by imperial decree.
This shows state involvement in codifying fiqh.
3
Analyze why it is a 'quasi-codification'
Identify the features: included worship (ibadat), lacked modern legal article format, omitted proofs and discussions, and selected a single preponderant opinion. Thus it resembles fiqh books more than a modern code.
The passage explicitly calls it a 'quasi-codification attempt'.
4
Examine the motive for the Majalla
Recall the background: expansion of commerce, need for a civil code to fill gaps left by commercial and penal codes, and the desire to systematically codify Hanafi transaction law.
The Majalla was a response to practical legal needs.
5
Contrast the two works
Compare form (fiqh book vs. article-based code), scope (ibadat included vs. excluded), and legal effect (imperial enforcement vs. state law). Recognize the Majalla as the first true modern codification of Islamic law.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the concept of taqnin.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
وقد وصفنا آنفاً هذا العمل الجليل الخطير بأنه محاولة شبه تقنينية من الفقه الإسلامي، لأن الكتاب المذكور لم يكن من حيث الشكل في صورة قانون بالمعنى المعروف اليوم، بل هو كتاب فقهي جامع للأحكام والمسائل من العبادات والمعاملات، لكنه يشبه إلى حد ما صياغة القوانين إجمالاً بعض وجوه أسلوب الصيغة القانونية وفي الخلو من الأدلة الأصولية ومناقشاتها والاقتصار على قول واحد في حكم المسألة هو القول الراجح في المذهب الحنفي. فبذلك يتقرب من طريق التقنين الأمر، ولكنه يبقى إلى كتب الفقه الفروعية أقرب منه إلى المجموعات التقنينية، ولا سيما اشتماله على أحكام العبادات التي لا تدخل في التقنين المدني. أما التقنين الفقه الإسلامي بالمعنى الحديث لكلمة التقنين فقد برز في مجلة الأحكام العدلية في عهد الدولة العثمانية سنة ١٢٩٣ هجرية، كما تقدم.
من
١٢/١٦ - مجلة الأحكام العدلية، والباعث على وضعها:
الباعث على وضع مجلة الأحكام العدلية وإصدارها بإرادة سنية (سلطانية)، لتكون قانوناً مدنياً عاماً في الدولة العلية العثمانية هو ما بينته جمعية المجلة (اللجنة الرسمية) التي الفت من فقهاء عرب وأتراك، وكلفت وضعها. فقد بينت هذا الباعث في تقريرها الذي رفعته إلى الصدر الأعظم (رئيس الوزراء) عالي باشا في غرة المحرم ١٢٨٦ هـ مصحوباً بباكورة أعمالها، وهي المقدمة وكتاب البيوع. وخلاصة ما جاء في التقرير المذكور عن الباعث على وضع المجلة أنه : لما اتسعت المعاملات التجارية وازداد الاتصال فيها بالعالم الخارجي، وهي معاملات ذات طبيعة تستوجب فيها أحكاماً كثيرة استثنائية من الأحكام العامة التي يتألف منها ما يسمى بالقانون المدني، ووضع قانون للتجارة وقانون للعقوبات وأنشئت محاكم التجارة ومحاكم الجزاء، وكان ما لا نص عليه في قانون التجارة (وكذا الحقوق العادية المدنية) الناشئة عن الجرائم مرجعها إلى القانون الأصلي وهو الأحكام الفقهية الوافية بجميع
Appendix to Chapter (16) Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya: And the first quarter of the work was entrusted to it, and the second committee was headed by Sheikh Jalal al-Din and entrusted with the second quarter, and the third committee was headed by Sheikh al-Qadi Muhammad Hasan and entrusted with the third quarter, and the fourth committee was headed by Mulla Hamid Junbouri and entrusted with the fourth quarter, and all worked under the presidency of Sheikh Nizam al-Din. Thus they completed this great project in eight years, which resulted in the aforementioned book al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya. • And since the Hanafi school was the prevailing school throughout the empire, this work was confined to the rulings of the Hanafi school. And al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya did not include the differences and ijtihad opinions of the jurisprudence of other schools. The emperor himself supervised the progress of the work and followed it daily, and the head of the council would present to him what had been completed from the previous day's work, so the emperor would give his observations and criticism every day, and point out to him any shortcomings he saw for completion. And progress averaged about four pages each day. After completing this great comprehensive book of Sharia law, represented by Hanafi ijtihad, and releasing it for copying and dissemination (akin to printing and distribution today), the emperor issued an imperial decree (royal will) to put its rulings into effect in all parts of the kingdom and to act upon them in the judicial circuits. (1) This collection (al-Fatawa al-‘Alamgiriyya) is currently printed and circulated in six massive volumes, and is considered one of the greatest and most important reliable and authoritative references in the Hanafi school. (1) Summarized from a lecture on al-Fatawa al-‘Alamgiriyya in English by Professor Anwar Ahmad Qadri, professor of law at the Sind College of Islamic Law in the Pakistani capital Karachi. Translated for us by Dr. Burhan al-Shatti (unpublished). 237 [PAGE_226] Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics We have previously described this great and momentous work as a quasi-codification attempt from Islamic jurisprudence, because the aforementioned book was not, in terms of form, in the image of law in the sense known today, but rather it is a comprehensive fiqh book containing rulings and issues from acts of worship and transactions. However, it somewhat resembles the drafting of laws overall in some aspects of legal drafting style, and in being free from fundamental evidence and its discussions, and in confining itself to one opinion on the ruling of the issue, which is the preponderant opinion in the Hanafi school. Thus it approaches the path of codification in that matter, but it remains closer to the substantive fiqh books than to codified collections, especially as it contains rulings on acts of worship that are not included in civil codification. As for the codification of Islamic jurisprudence in the modern sense of the word codification, it emerged in the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya during the Ottoman Empire in the year 1293 AH, as previously mentioned. From 12/16 - Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, and the motive behind its compilation: The motive behind the compilation of Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya and its issuance by a (sultanic) will, to be a general civil law in the Ottoman state, is what was explained by the Codification Assembly (the official committee) composed of Arab and Turkish jurists and charged with its compilation. They explained this motive in their report submitted to the Grand Vizier (Prime Minister) Ali Pasha on the first day of Muharram 1286 AH, accompanied by the first fruits of their work, which was the introduction and the book of sales. The summary of what is mentioned in that report about the motive behind compiling the Majalla is: Since commercial transactions expanded and contact with the outside world increased, and these are transactions whose nature requires many exceptional rulings from the general rulings that constitute what is called civil law, and a commercial code and a penal code were issued, and commercial courts and criminal courts were established, and what is not stipulated in the commercial code (and the ordinary civil rights) arising from crimes refer to the original law, which is the comprehensive fiqh rulings covering all
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage, an appendix to the chapter on Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, describes two major milestones in the history of Hanafi codification: first, the Fatawa al-Hindiyya (also known as al-Fatawa al-‘Alamgiriyya), compiled under the patronage of Emperor Aurangzeb Alamgir in the 11th century AH (17th century CE), and second, the Majalla itself, compiled in the 13th century AH (19th century CE). The text details the monumental collaborative project of the Fatawa Hindiyya, involving four scholarly committees working under the leadership of Sheikh Nizam al-Din. The emperor personally supervised the work, ensuring it adhered solely to the Hanafi school. The finished work was an imperial code, literally enforced throughout the Mughal empire. The text then explains why the Fatawa Hindiyya is considered only a 'quasi-codification' – it lacks the formal structure of modern law, includes acts of worship, and still resembles a fiqh compendium. The first true codification of Islamic law (in the modern sense) was the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, produced by a committee of Arab and Turkish Hanafi jurists in the Ottoman Empire. The motive was practical: the expansion of commerce and the introduction of secular commercial and penal codes created a need for a comprehensive civil code based on Hanafi fiqh to fill gaps. The Majalla thus became the foundational civil code of the Ottoman state, covering transactions and selected areas of law, excluding worship. This passage underscores the Hanafi school's adaptability and its authoritative role in shaping state law across centuries.

Key Terms
الفتاوى الهندية (al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya): Also known as al-Fatawa al-'Alamgiriyya; a comprehensive compilation of Hanafi fatwas and rulings produced in the Mughal Empire under Emperor Aurangzeb Alamgir.
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya): The Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi fiqh, promulgated in 1293 AH (1876 CE), covering transactions and general legal principles in 1,851 articles.
التقنين (al-Taqnin): The process of codifying fiqh rulings into a state-enacted law with a structured, article-based format, omitting foundational proofs and selecting one authoritative opinion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya from al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya?
A) The Majalla is a comprehensive fiqh book covering worship, while the Fatawa Hindiyya focuses only on transactions.
B) The Majalla is a true codification in the modern sense, while the Fatawa Hindiyya is a quasi-codification still resembling traditional fiqh books.
C) The Majalla was compiled under royal patronage, while the Fatawa Hindiyya was not.
D) The Majalla includes rulings from all four Sunni schools, while the Fatawa Hindiyya is exclusively Hanafi.

Key Takeaway
The Fatawa Hindiyya is a quasi-codification of Hanafi fiqh; the Majalla is the first true civil code based on Hanafi law, reflecting the school's adaptation to modern state structures.

History and Development of Fiqh

Why the Majalla? The Need for a Hanafi Civil Code
Explains the historical context and motivation for compiling the Majalla

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three main challenges that motivated the compilation of the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya.

Core Idea
The Majalla solved the crisis of judges untrained in fiqh navigating a vast, disputed Hanafi corpus.

The Ottoman Majalla represents the first modern codification of Hanafi civil law. But why was it necessary? In this lesson, we explore the specific legal and institutional problems that drove the state to commission this monumental work. Understanding these motives helps us appreciate how the Majalla shaped Hanafi legal practice in the modern era.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary legal gap
The passage states that commercial expansion and new Western-style codes created a situation where judges lacked fiqh knowledge. They were unable to navigate traditional fiqh sources to resolve cases.
2
Analyze the internal complexity of Hanafi fiqh
The Hanafi madhhab is described as 'a sea without a shore' with immense internal disagreement (ikhtilaf) and varying authoritativeness. Distinguishing the correct opinion from the weak was extremely difficult.
This refers to the multiple riwayat and levels of mujtahids within the Hanafi school.
3
Recognize the temporal dimension
Changes in customs and times created a second layer of ikhtilaf: temporal vs. substantive. Jurists needed skill to differentiate which differences were due to changing urf and which were core legal disagreements.
Al-ikhtilaf al-zamani vs al-ikhtilaf al-burhani.
4
Understand the solution
The committee compiled a work that selected only preponderant opinions (mufta bihi), omitted evidence discussions, and was organized for easy reference. This was the Majalla.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
وقد وصفنا آنفاً هذا العمل الجليل الخطير بأنه محاولة شبه تقنينية من الفقه الإسلامي، لأن الكتاب المذكور لم يكن من حيث الشكل في صورة قانون بالمعنى المعروف اليوم، بل هو كتاب فقهي جامع للأحكام والمسائل من العبادات والمعاملات، لكنه يشبه إلى حد ما صياغة القوانين إجمالاً بعض وجوه أسلوب الصيغة القانونية وفي الخلو من الأدلة الأصولية ومناقشاتها والاقتصار على قول واحد في حكم المسألة هو القول الراجح في المذهب الحنفي. فبذلك يتقرب من طريق التقنين الأمر، ولكنه يبقى إلى كتب الفقه الفروعية أقرب منه إلى المجموعات التقنينية، ولا سيما اشتماله على أحكام العبادات التي لا تدخل في التقنين المدني. أما التقنين الفقه الإسلامي بالمعنى الحديث لكلمة التقنين فقد برز في مجلة الأحكام العدلية في عهد الدولة العثمانية سنة ١٢٩٣ هجرية، كما تقدم.

من مجلة الأحكام العدلية، والباعث على وضعها:
الباعث على وضع مجلة الأحكام العدلية وإصدارها بإرادة سنية (سلطانية)، لتكون قانوناً مدنياً عاماً في الدولة العلية العثمانية هو ما بينته جمعية المجلة (اللجنة الرسمية) التي الفت من فقهاء عرب وأتراك، وكلفت وضعها. فقد بينت هذا الباعث في تقريرها الذي رفعته إلى الصدر الأعظم رئيس الوزراء) عالي باشا في غرة المحرم ١٢٨٦ هـ مصحوباً بباكورة المقدمة وكتاب البيوع. أعمالها، وهي وخلاصة ما جاء في التقرير المذكور عن الباعث على وضع المجلة أنه : لما اتسعت المعاملات التجارية وازداد الاتصال فيها بالعالم الخارجي، وهي معاملات ذات طبيعة تستوجب فيها أحكاماً كثيرة استثنائية من الأحكام العامة التي يتألف منها ما يسمى بالقانون المدني، ووضع قانون للتجارة وقانون للعقوبات وأنشئت محاكم التجارة ومحاكم الجزاء، وكان ما لا نص عليه في قانون التجارة وكذا الحقوق العادية المدنية) الناشئة عن الجرائم مرجعها إلى القانون الأصلي وهو الأحكام الفقهية الوافية بجميع الاحتياجات ظهرت من ذلك مشكلة جديدة هي أن قضاة تلك المحاكم النظامية ومجالس تمييز الحقوق لا إطلاع لهم على علم الفقه وأحكامه، وهو «بحر لا ساحل له ولا سيما المذهب الحنفي منه، كما أن الاختلاف الكثير في الآراء الفقهية والروايات ضمن المذهب، وتفاوت أصحابها في الطبقة الاعتبارية جعل تمييز القول الصحيح من الضعيف عسيراً جداً. وهنالك أيضاً اختلاف ناشيء من تبدل الزمان والأعراف والعادات بين عصر المتقدمين والمتأخرين من الفقهاء، فتمييز الاختلاف الزماني عن الاختلاف البرهاني أمر صعب يحتاج إلى نظر فقهي دقيق. فلذلك دعت الحاجة إلى كتاب في المعاملات الفقهية يكون مضبوطاً سهل المأخذ، عارياً من الاختلافات حاوياً للأقوال المختارة سهل المطالعة (المراجعة) على كل أحد، واجتمعت الجمعية بموجب الإرادة العلية في دائرة ديوان الأحكام وبادرت إلى ترتيب مجلة مؤلفة من المسائل والأمور الكثيرة الوقوع اللازمة جداً من قسم المعاملات الفقهية مجموعة من الأقوال الموثوق بها في المذهب الحنفي، وسميت مجلة الأحكام العدلية ورؤي تصديرها بالقواعد الفقهية لأن لها فائدة كلية في ضبط المسائل.
بدأ عمل اللجنة رسمياً في تحريرها خلال عام ١٢٨٦ هـ وسبقه قبل ذلك عمل إعدادي من قبل جمعية علمية في إدارة مجلس التنظيمات) حرر فيه كثير من المسائل واستخرجت نصوص فقهية ولكن لم يبرز شيء منها إلى حيّز الفعل ولم ندر كم استغرق ذلك العمل الإعدادي من الزمن كما أنه كان متقطعاً. أما بعد تأليف اللجنة الرسمية على أساس التفرغ والانصراف إلى العمل على وضع المجلة في شكل قانون مدني عام فقد استغرق تحريرها بين سبع إلى ثماني سنوات من خلال عام ١٢٨٦ هـ حتى أواسط عام ۱۲۹۳ هـ وكتبت المجلة أولاً باللغة التركية.
وقد كانت اللجنة ترفع ما ينجز تحريره تباعاً كتاباً فكتاباً، فكان أول ما حرر ورفع منها كتاب البيوع مع المقدمة المشار إليها آنفاً.
Chapter Three: Stages of the Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics
We have previously described this great and serious work as a quasi-codification attempt from Islamic jurisprudence, because the mentioned book was not, in terms of form, in the shape of a law in the known sense today. Rather, it is a comprehensive fiqh book of rulings and issues from acts of worship and transactions, but it somewhat resembles the drafting of laws in some aspects of legal style and in being free from usul evidences and their discussions, and restricting to one opinion on the ruling of the issue, which is the preponderant opinion in the Hanafi madhhab. Thus it approaches the method of codification, but it remains closer to the fiqh books of branches than to codificatory collections, especially its inclusion of rulings on acts of worship which are not included in civil codification. As for the codification of Islamic jurisprudence in the modern sense of the word codification, it emerged in the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya during the Ottoman state in the year 1293 AH, as previously mentioned.

From the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya, and the motive for its compilation:
The motive for compiling and issuing the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya by royal will, to be a general civil law in the Ottoman state, is what was explained by the Majalla committee (the official committee) composed of Arab and Turkish jurists and tasked with its compilation. They explained this motive in their report submitted to the Grand Vizier Ali Pasha on the first of Muharram 1286 AH, accompanied by the first product of their work: the introduction and the Book of Sales. The summary of what came in the mentioned report about the motive for compiling the Majalla is: When commercial transactions expanded and contact with the outside world increased—these being transactions of a nature requiring many exceptional rulings from the general rulings that constitute what is called civil law—and a commercial code and a penal code were enacted, and commercial courts and criminal courts were established, and whatever was not provided for in the commercial code, as well as ordinary civil rights arising from crimes, had to be referred to the original law, which is the fiqh rulings that suffice for all needs, a new problem emerged: that the judges of those regular courts and appellate councils had no knowledge of the science of fiqh and its rulings, which is 'a sea without a shore, especially the Hanafi madhhab'. Moreover, the great difference in fiqh opinions and transmissions within the madhhab, and the disparity of their proponents in the hierarchical rank, made distinguishing the correct opinion from the weak extremely difficult. There was also a difference arising from the change of time, customs, and practices between the era of the early and later jurists, so distinguishing temporal difference from substantive difference is a difficult matter requiring precise juristic consideration. Therefore, the need arose for a book on fiqh transactions that is well-organized, easy to access, free of differences, containing the chosen opinions, and easy to refer to for everyone. So the committee convened by royal order in the office of the Diwan al-Ahkam and proceeded to arrange a journal composed of the most frequently occurring and highly necessary issues and matters from the section of fiqh transactions, collected from reliable opinions in the Hanafi madhhab, and it was named Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya. It was deemed appropriate to preface it with the fiqh maxims because they have a comprehensive benefit in controlling the issues.
The committee's official work on drafting it began during the year 1286 AH, preceded by preparatory work by a scientific society in the administration of the Council of Regulations, which drafted many issues and extracted fiqh texts, but nothing of it came into actual existence, and we do not know how long that preparatory work took, and it was intermittent. As for after the formation of the official committee on the basis of dedicating themselves to the work of compiling the Majalla in the form of a general civil law, its drafting took between seven to eight years from the year 1286 AH until the middle of 1293 AH. The Majalla was first written in Turkish.
The committee would submit the completed parts sequentially, book by book. The first part drafted and submitted was the Book of Sales along with the introduction mentioned above.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage, drawn from the section on the stages of Islamic jurisprudence, describes the emergence of the Ottoman Majalla as the first modern codification of Hanafi fiqh in the form of a civil code. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that earlier works like the 'Mukhtasar al-Quduri' approximated codification by omitting evidence and multiple opinions, but still included acts of worship which are not part of civil law. The Majalla, however, covered only transactions (mu'amalat) and was intended as a civil code. The impetus for the Majalla is detailed: with the expansion of commerce and the introduction of Western-style commercial and penal codes, a gap existed because judges in the new secular courts lacked knowledge of Islamic jurisprudence. The Hanafi madhhab's vastness, internal disagreements, and varying authoritativeness of opinions made it nearly impossible for these judges to find the correct ruling. Additionally, changes over time in customs and practices complicated matters further. Thus, the committee formed of Arab and Turkish jurists compiled a work that selected the most reliable Hanafi opinions, omitted differences, and presented rulings in an accessible format. They also prefaced it with fundamental legal maxims (qawa'id fiqhiyya) to aid in applying the rules. The drafting took seven to eight years (1286-1293 AH), initially in Turkish, with the first part being the Book of Sales.

Key Terms
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya): The Ottoman civil code (1293 AH) based on Hanafi fiqh, covering transactions and procedural law, but not acts of worship.
التقنين (al-taqnin): Codification in the modern sense: reducing fiqh to statutory form with a single opinion per issue, for uniformity and ease of application.
الاختلاف الزماني (al-ikhtilaf al-zamani): Differences arising from changing times, customs, and conditions, as opposed to substantive legal disagreements (ikhtilaf burhani).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the primary reason given for compiling the Majalla?
A) To replace Islamic law with Western codes
B) To provide a codified, accessible Hanafi reference for judges unfamiliar with traditional fiqh
C) To harmonize all four Sunni madhhabs
D) To translate fiqh from Arabic into Turkish

Key Takeaway
The Majalla was a necessary response to institutional and epistemological challenges: it transformed the vast, disputed Hanafi corpus into a streamlined civil code for modern courts.

The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah: Codification of Hanafi Mu'amalat
Historical account of the need for and drafting of the Majallah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the factors that led to the codification of the Majallah and describe the drafting process.

Core Idea
The Majallah was a response to judicial confusion — a codification of Hanafi mu'amalat to provide accessible, authoritative rulings.

This lesson examines the historical background and drafting of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, the Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi fiqh. Understanding why and how it was compiled gives insight into the jurisprudential challenges of applying fiqh in changing times and the methodological shifts in the late Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize that judges in the new regular courts lacked knowledge of fiqh, and the vast Hanafi literature with conflicting opinions made it difficult to determine correct rulings.
This lack of training and the sea of opinions created a practical crisis.
2
Distinguish types of disagreement
Understand the difference between temporal disagreements (due to changing customs and times) and substantive disagreements (burhānī) — a key methodological point.
The passage emphasizes the need for precise fiqh reasoning to separate the two.
3
Describe the committee's mandate
The committee was formed by imperial decree to compile a concise book of fiqh transactions from reliable Hanafi opinions, free from differences and easy to use.
The scope was limited to mu'amalat, excluding ibadat, personal status, and criminal law.
4
Outline the drafting process
Drafting took 7-8 years, with each book submitted for review to experts including the Shaykh al-Islam, revised, and then translated from Turkish to Arabic.
The process ensured thorough vetting and refinement.
5
Note the final enactment
The last book (Book of Judiciary) was completed, and the Majallah was officially enacted in 1293 AH (1876 CE).
The code was prefaced with legal maxims (qawā‘id fiqhiyya) for comprehensive guidance.

ملحق بالفصل (١٦) مجلة الأحكام العدلية
الاحتياجات ظهرت من ذلك مشكلة جديدة هي أن قضاة تلك المحاكم النظامية ومجالس تمييز الحقوق لا إطلاع لهم على علم الفقه وأحكامه، وهو «بحر لا ساحل له ولا سيما المذهب الحنفي منه، كما أن الاختلاف الكثير في الآراء الفقهية والروايات ضمن المذهب، وتفاوت أصحابها في الطبقة الاعتبارية جعل تمييز القول الصحيح من الضعيف عسيراً جداً. وهنالك أيضاً اختلاف ناشيء من تبدل الزمان والأعراف والعادات بين عصر المتقدمين والمتأخرين من الفقهاء، فتمييز الاختلاف الزماني عن الاختلاف البرهاني أمر صعب يحتاج إلى نظر فقهي دقيق. فلذلك دعت الحاجة إلى كتاب في المعاملات الفقهية يكون مضبوطاً سهل المأخذ، عارياً من الاختلافات حاوياً للأقوال المختارة سهل المطالعة (المراجعة) على كل أحد، واجتمعت الجمعية بموجب الإرادة العلية في دائرة ديوان الأحكام وبادرت إلى ترتيب مجلة مؤلفة من المسائل والأمور الكثيرة الوقوع اللازمة جداً من قسم المعاملات الفقهية مجموعة من الأقوال الموثوق بها في المذهب الحنفي، وسميت مجلة الأحكام العدلية ورؤي تصديرها بالقواعد الفقهية لأن لها فائدة كلية في ضبط المسائل.
بدأ عمل اللجنة رسمياً في تحريرها خلال عام ١٢٨٦ هـ وسبقه قبل ذلك عمل إعدادي من قبل جمعية علمية في إدارة مجلس التنظيمات) حرر فيه كثير من المسائل واستخرجت نصوص فقهية ولكن لم يبرز شيء منها إلى حيّز الفعل ولم ندر كم استغرق ذلك العمل الإعدادي من الزمن كما أنه كان متقطعاً. أما بعد تأليف اللجنة الرسمية على أساس التفرغ والانصراف إلى العمل على وضع المجلة في شكل قانون مدني عام فقد استغرق تحريرها بين سبع إلى ثماني سنوات من خلال عام ١٢٨٦ هـ حتى أواسط عام ۱۲۹۳ هـ وكتبت المجلة أولاً باللغة التركية.
وقد كانت اللجنة ترفع ما ينجز تحريره تباعاً كتاباً فكتاباً، فكان أول ما حرر ورفع منها كتاب البيوع مع المقدمة المشار إليها آنفاً.
وكلما أنهت تحرير كتاب تقدم منه نسخة لمقام مشيخة الإسلام نسخاً أخرى لمن له مهارة ومعرفة كافية في علم الفقه، وتتلقى الملاحظات الواردة عليه وتجري ما يلزم من التهذيب والتعديل في ضوء تلك الملاحظات، ثم تقوم بتبييضه نهائياً وترجمته إلى اللغة العربية وترفعه إلى الصدر الأعظم لتوشيحه بالخط الهمايوني للعمل بموجبه. وآخر كتاب خُتِمَت به المجلة هو كتاب القضاء، وصدرت الإرادة السنية للعمل بالمجلة ما تضمنته في السادس والعشرين من شعبان ۱۲۹۳ هـ.
Appendix to Chapter (16): The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah. From this, a new problem emerged: the judges of those regular courts and the appellate councils had no knowledge of fiqh and its rulings, and it is a sea without shore, especially the Hanafi madhhab. Also, the great number of differing fiqh opinions and narrations within the madhhab, and the varying levels of their authors, made distinguishing the correct statement from the weak very difficult. There is also disagreement arising from changing times, customs, and habits between the era of the early and later jurists, so distinguishing temporal difference from substantive difference is difficult and requires precise fiqh reasoning. Therefore, the need arose for a book on fiqh transactions that is precise, easy to access, free from differences, containing preferred statements, easy for everyone to review. The society assembled by imperial decree in the office of the dīwān al-aḥkām and proceeded to compile a journal composed of frequently occurring and very necessary matters from the section of fiqh transactions, collected from reliable statements in the Hanafi madhhab, and it was named Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah. It was deemed appropriate to preface it with the fiqh maxims because they have a comprehensive benefit in disciplining the rulings. The committee's work officially began drafting it during the year 1286 AH, preceded by preparatory work by a scientific society in the administration of the Council of Reforms, in which many questions were drafted and fiqh texts extracted, but nothing emerged into actuality, and we do not know how long that preparatory work took, and it was intermittent. After the formation of the official committee on a full-time basis to work on setting the Majallah in the form of a general civil code, its drafting took seven to eight years from 1286 AH until mid-1293 AH. The Majallah was first written in Turkish. The committee would submit completed drafts book by book; the first book drafted and submitted was the Book of Sales with the aforementioned introduction. Whenever it finished drafting a book, it submitted a copy to the office of the Shaykh al-Islam and other copies to those having sufficient skill and knowledge in fiqh, received observations on it, and made necessary refinements and amendments in light of those observations, then finalized it and translated it into Arabic, and submitted it to the Grand Vizier to be adorned with the imperial monogram for implementation. The last book with which the Majallah was concluded was the Book of Judiciary, and the imperial decree to implement the Majallah was issued on the twenty-sixth of Sha'ban 1293 AH.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a historical account of the need for and drafting of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, the Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi fiqh. The author explains that the problem arose because judges in the new regular courts were not trained in fiqh, and the Hanafi madhhab's vast and often conflicting opinions made it difficult to determine the correct ruling. Additionally, changes over time required distinguishing temporal differences (due to changing customs) from substantive differences. Therefore, a committee was formed to compile a concise, reliable book of fiqh transactions, selecting authoritative opinions. The drafting took about seven to eight years, with extensive review and translation from Turkish to Arabic. The final book was the Book of Judiciary, and the Majallah was officially enacted in 1293 AH.

Key Terms
مجلة الأحكام العدلية (Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah): The Ottoman civil code compiled from Hanafi fiqh, covering transactions and excluding worship, personal status, and criminal law.
الاختلاف الزماني (al-ikhtilāf al-zamānī): Difference in rulings due to changing times, customs, and conditions, as opposed to substantive difference (ikhtilāf burhānī).
القواعد الفقهية (al-qawāʿid al-fiqhiyya): Legal maxims that express general principles of fiqh; the Majallah opens with 99 such maxims to guide application.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the primary reason for compiling the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah?
A) To replace all existing Islamic laws with a secular code.
B) To simplify Hanafi fiqh for judges unfamiliar with its complexities.
C) To eliminate all differences between Islamic schools of thought.
D) To translate the Quran into Turkish.

Key Takeaway
The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah was a pioneering codification of Hanafi mu'amalat, driven by the need for accessible, authoritative rules amidst vast juristic differences and changing times.
