Critique of the Majalla's Hanafi Exclusivity
Criticizes the Majalla for its narrow Hanafi base and suggests using other madhhabs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the shortcomings of codifying Islamic law exclusively from one madhhab and explain the benefits of cross-madhhab borrowing (talfiq) for legal codification and ifta.

Core Idea
Codifying law from a single madhhab yields rigidity; adapting from multiple schools ensures legal relevance.

In this passage, Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa critiques the Ottoman Majalla for its exclusive reliance on the Hanafi school. He demonstrates how this narrow approach led to numerous practical deficiencies, forcing frequent amendments and eventual replacement. The lesson underscores the importance of cross-madhhab borrowing (talfiq) as a tool for legal vitality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context: What was the Majalla?
Recognize that the Majalla was a codification of Hanafi fiqh used in Ottoman courts, meant to unify legal practice.
2
Identify the central critique: Why does al-Zarqa blame the Majalla's Hanafi exclusivity?
He argues that confining to one school made it rigid and quickly outdated, requiring surgical amendments.
3
Analyze the examples: How do other madhhab positions solve the problems?
Al-Zarqa gives four illustrations (contractual conditions, usufruct, preemption, evidence) where other schools offer more suitable rulings that align with modern needs.
Focus on the principle 'al-ʿaqd shariʿat al-mutaʿaqidayn' (contract is law of parties) as a key example.
4
Evaluate the historical excuse: Was the Majalla committee to blame?
Al-Zarqa acknowledges the pressure of Hanafi fanaticism at the time but still laments the missed opportunity.
5
Draw the lesson: What does this imply for modern ifta and codification?
A mufti or legislator should not be bound to one madhhab; cross-madhhab borrowing (talfiq) is necessary to keep fiqh relevant.

ولو أن جمعية المجلة لم تقصرها على المذهب الحنفي، بل استمدت فيها من كل المذاهب الاجتهادية ما تراه أصلح وأوفى بالحاجة الزمنية لما احتيج بعد صدورها بمدة قصيرة إلى إعمال المباضع الجراحية في جسمها وما غادرته فيها من تعطيل أعضاء وتفريغ أحشاء مما أدخل عليها من نسخ ومسخ وتعديل .
ففي مجموع المذاهب الفقهية متسع لوفاء الحاجات الزمنية، وكان الاستمداد منها يجعل المجلة أكثر ثباتاً واستقراراً على الزمن . فقد كان الواجب مثلاً في أحكام المشارطات العقدية - وهي أهم ما ظهر فيه ضيق المجلة - أن تستمد من مذهب شُرَيح القاضي وابن شبرمة أو المذهب الحنبلي، فإنّ مذاهب هؤلاء في صحة الشروط التعاقدية وبطلانها يجاري أحسن ما استقرت عليه النظريات القانونية الحديثة في ضابط ما يقبل وما يرفض من المشارطات في ضوء المبدأ القائل: إنَّ العقد شريعة المتعاقدين.
وكان من الواجب مثلاً أيضاً في قضية مالية المنافع، وتقومها في ذاتها، وضمان منافع المغصوب أن تستمد أحكامها من المذهبين الشافعي والحنبلي اللذين يعتبران المنافع أموالاً مضمونة بذاتها كالأعيان. وكان الواجب في طلب الشفعة أن تستمد أحكام المدد المسقطة للشفعة والاختلاف في ثمن العقار المشفوع من المذهب المالكي. وكذا تستمد في قضية الشاهد واليمين وفي رد اليمين على المدعي، وفي عدم تجزؤ الإقرار من المذاهب الاجتهادية التي تقول بذلك لظهور الحاجة إلى كل ذلك مما هو غير مقبول في حكم المجلة المستمد من المذهب الحنفي.
وهكذا يقال في مواطن وقضايا ومسائل أخرى مما لا يتسع المجال لتعداده كمسألة البيع بما سيستقر عليه السعر في المذهب الحنبلي.
ملحق بالفصل (١٦) مجلة الأحكام العدلية
ولكن من الحق أن يقام لجمعية المجلة ،عذرها، فإن الظرف إذ ذاك لم يكن يتحمل هذه الخطوة عند وضع المجلة، فإن العصبية للمذهب الحنفي في المراكز الرئيسية من الدولة العثمانية وهي المراكز التي وضعت المجلة من أجلها تلك العصبية لم يكن في ظلها مجال للانفتاح على ما المذاهب الأخرى من مزايا واجتهادات قيمة معتبرة هي في موضوعاتها أصلح حكماً مما في المذهب الحنفي، فإنَّ تلك العصبية المذهبية كانت تُرِي أهل كل مذهب أنَّ ما في غيره من أحكام اجتهادية لم يألفوها كأنها خروج على الشريعة، لأنَّ الشريعة في نظرهم لا يمثلها إلا مذهبهم !! ١٥/١٦ - مصير المجلة :

وبعد سنوات تلتها العراق فألغت المجلة وأحلت محلها قانوناً مدنياً أجنبي الأصول أيضاً لكنه رصع ترصيعاً في أوله ببعض القواعد الفقهية الكلية التي في مقدمة ،المجلة والتي لها قيمة علمية لا عملية ـ كما سبق بيانه بالنسبة إلى المجلة - لكي توهم الناظر لأول نظرة أن روح وريحها منبثان فيه

المجلة القانون المدني العام
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics
Other exceptions, and that by a legal material which it slipped into the Code of Civil Procedure in Article 64.
Had the Majalla committee not restricted it to the Hanafi madhhab, but derived from all jurisprudential schools what it deemed more suitable and fulfilling for the temporal need, there would have been no need shortly after its issuance to apply surgical scalpels to its body, leaving it with disabled organs and eviscerated entrails from the abrogation, distortion, and amendment introduced to it.
For in the totality of jurisprudential schools there is ample room to meet temporal needs, and deriving from them would have made the Majalla more stable and enduring over time. For example, in rulings on contractual conditions—which is where the narrowness of the Majalla most appeared—it should have derived from the school of Shurayh al-Qadi and Ibn Shubruma or the Hanbali school, for their schools regarding the validity and invalidity of contractual conditions align with the best modern legal theories on the criterion of what is accepted and rejected of conditions in light of the principle: 'The contract is the law of the contracting parties.'
Similarly, in the issue of the financial nature of usufructs, their valuation in themselves, and guarantee of benefits of a usurped property, the rulings should have been derived from the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools, which consider usufructs as properties guaranteed in themselves like physical assets. And in claims of preemption, the rulings on periods that cancel preemption and disputes over the price of the preempted property should have been derived from the Maliki school. Likewise, in the issue of the witness and oath, and in returning the oath to the plaintiff, and in the indivisibility of confession, rulings should be derived from the jurisprudential schools that hold these views, due to the manifest need for all this, which is unacceptable under the Majalla's rulings derived from the Hanafi school.
And so it is said in other places, cases, and issues that cannot be enumerated, such as the issue of selling at the future market price in the Hanbali school.
Appendix to Chapter (16) of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah
But it is only fair to make an excuse for the Majalla committee, for the circumstances at that time could not bear this step when drafting the Majalla. For the fanaticism for the Hanafi madhhab in the main centers of the Ottoman state—the centers for which the Majalla was drafted—left no room for openness to the valuable and significant merits and juristic opinions of other schools, which in their subjects are more appropriate in ruling than what is in the Hanafi madhhab. Indeed, that sectarian fanaticism made the adherents of each school see that the jurisprudential rulings in other schools which they were unfamiliar with were as if they were a departure from Shariah, because in their view Shariah is represented only by their own school!! 15/16 - Fate of the Majalla:
During the Ottoman era, the feeling of the Majalla's inadequacy to meet temporal needs grew among officials, and thus abrogation and amendment kept snatching at it from time to time.
Then in the Arab countries separated from the Ottoman Empire after World War I, the feeling grew of the necessity to replace it with another Majalla or a new civil code formulated from various schools, remedying the shortcomings and defects that appeared in the Majalla. In Syria, the Majalla was abolished in 1949 at the hands of the leader of Syria's first military coup, Mr. Husni al-Za'im, and the devils that surrounded him, and was replaced by a civil law of foreign origin, the new Egyptian Civil Code.
Years later, Iraq followed suit, abolishing the Majalla and replacing it with a civil law also of foreign origin, but studded at its beginning with some of the overarching fiqh principles from the introduction of the Majalla, which have scholarly but not practical value—as previously explained regarding the Majalla—to give the initial impression that its spirit and essence are derived from it.
In Kuwait, the entire Majalla was also the...
the Majalla was the general civil law.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage from al-Zarqa's General Introduction to Fiqh critiques the Ottoman Majalla for its exclusive reliance on the Hanafi madhhab. The author argues that by limiting itself to one school, the Majalla became rigid and quickly required extensive amendments. He advocates for a pluralistic approach, drawing from various madhhabs to better suit modern needs. Specific examples illustrate where other schools offer more suitable rulings: contractual conditions (Hanbali or the school of Shurayh), usufruct as property (Shafi'i/Hanbali), preemption periods (Maliki), and evidence rules (various). The author acknowledges the historical context of Hanafi fanaticism but laments its consequences. Ultimately, the Majalla was replaced in many Arab countries by civil codes of foreign origin, a failure attributed partly to its narrowness. This passage thus serves as a strong argument for talfiq (eclecticism) and the need for fiqh to adapt across schools.

Key Terms
المجلة (al-Majalla): The Ottoman Code of Islamic Law (Majalla al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah) based on Hanafi fiqh.
العصبية المذهبية (al-ʿaṣabiyya al-madhhabiyya): Sectarian fanaticism or blind partisanship to one's own legal school.
المشارطات العقدية (al-mushāraṭāt al-ʿaqdiyya): Contractual conditions or stipulations agreed upon by parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Majalla's failure demonstrates that exclusive reliance on any single madhhab for legal codification leads to obsolescence; cross-madhhab borrowing (talfiq) is essential for sustainable Islamic law.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Rise, Fall, and Revival of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah
Historical-legal analysis of codification and madhhab partisanship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the historical factors that led to the replacement of the Majallah and evaluate the later revival of codification from Islamic fiqh in modern Arab civil laws.

Core Idea
Fanatic adherence to a single madhhab caused the Majallah's limitations; later, broader fiqh codification revived Islamic identity in law.

The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah was a landmark codification of Hanafi fiqh for civil transactions. However, its rigid reliance on one school led to its eventual replacement by Western-style codes in several Arab states. This lesson examines why the Majallah was deemed insufficient, how it was replaced, and how subsequent movements revived the project of codifying Islamic fiqh more comprehensively, drawing from all madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original limitation
Understand the author's defence of the Majallah committee: they were constrained by strong Hanafi partisanship that prevented incorporating more suitable rulings from other madhhabs.
This partisanship is termed 'al-ʿaṣabiyyah al-madhhabiyyah'.
2
Trace the decline
Note the growing awareness of the Majallah's inadequacy in the late Ottoman era, leading to frequent amendments and eventual abrogation in Syria (1949) and Iraq, replaced by foreign-origin civil codes.
Iraq's code superficially included some Hanafi maxims but was essentially foreign.
3
Observe the survival in Kuwait
Contrast with Kuwait, where the Majallah remained in force for civil transactions alongside Maliki fiqh for personal status, though a commercial law encroached on its domain.
This shows partial retention.
4
Examine the revival factors
List the factors that renewed codification from fiqh: popular Islamic identity, the Islamic Fiqh Week conferences (Paris 1951, Damascus 1961), and the Encyclopedia of Fiqh project.
These initiatives demonstrated the viability of a broader fiqh-based civil code.
5
Apply the lesson to mufti practice
Consider how a modern mufti or legal scholar should approach codification: avoid madhhab partisanship, assess rulings from all schools based on suitability (maslahah), and integrate classical fiqh with contemporary needs.
This aligns with the iftaa_usul principle of talfiq (eclecticism) when necessary.

الفقه ،
٢٤٦
Appendix to Chapter (16) of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah: But it is right to excuse the Majallah committee, for the circumstances at that time could not bear this step when drafting the Majallah. Indeed, partisanship for the Hanafi school in the main centers of the Ottoman Empire—for which the Majallah was drafted—left no room for openness to the merits and valuable independent judgments of other schools, which in their subject matter were more suitable rulings than those in the Hanafi school. For that sectarian partisanship made the adherents of each school see that what was in other schools of independent rulings unfamiliar to them was like an aberration from the Sharia, because in their view the Sharia is represented only by their school!! 15/16 - The Fate of the Majallah: In the Ottoman era, the feeling that the Majallah was insufficient to meet the needs of the time was increasing among officials; hence, abrogation and amendment hit it from time to time. Then, in the Arab countries that separated from the Ottoman Empire after World War I, the feeling grew of the necessity to replace it with another code or with a new civil law drafted from various schools, overcoming the deficiencies and defects that appeared in the Majallah. So in Syria, the Majallah was abolished in 1949 by the leader of the first military coup in Syria, Mr. Husni al-Za'im and the devils surrounding him, and was replaced by a civil law of foreign origins—the new Egyptian Civil Code. Years later, Iraq followed, abolishing the Majallah and replacing it with a civil law also of foreign origins, but it was studded at its beginning with some general legal maxims from the preface of the Majallah—which have scientific rather than practical value, as previously explained—to give the first observer the illusion that its spirit and essence emanated from it. In Kuwait, the entire Majallah was also the applicable civil code in civil transactions, alongside the Maliki school jurisprudence applicable in personal status matters, as affirmed by the explanatory memorandum of the Judiciary Regulation Law issued by Amiri Decree No. 19 of 1959, and also the explanatory memorandum of the Commercial Law No. 2 of 1961. But this commercial law exceeded its subject and encroached upon the general civil domain—the sphere of influence of the Majallah—by bringing new rules and rulings for obligations of foreign origins, which are of the essence of civil law (not commercial law, which should normally be limited to exceptional provisions from the general civil law). Thus it seemed that the rulings of the Majallah, and even the idea of deriving civil legislation from Islamic fiqh, were heading towards extinction in the Arab countries themselves!! However, the reality is that the strong popular feeling of the necessity to preserve Islamic identity and its great legal heritage, then the convening of the Islamic Fiqh Week Conference at the French Law Faculty in Paris in 1951, then its second session in Damascus during the union between Syria and Egypt in 1961, and the important researches in those two sessions and the merits of Islamic fiqh highlighted therein, then the establishment of the Encyclopedia of Fiqh project, and other developments which we will mention in the next chapter when discussing the eighth jurisprudential period—all of this renewed the determination to codify from Islamic fiqh, and thus complete civil laws emerged derived from fiqh, avoiding many of the deficiencies noted in the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah. We will mention this briefly at the end of the next chapter (paragraph on codification from fiqh), then detail it in Chapter Twenty-Two and its appendices when discussing civil laws and the virtue of their construction from Islamic fiqh.
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a historical-legal analysis of the fate of the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, the Ottoman civil code. The author begins by acknowledging the excuse for the original Majallah committee: the climate of strong Hanafi partisanship at the time prevented incorporating rulings from other madhhabs, even when those rulings were more suitable. This partisanship led each school to view unfamiliar rulings as deviations. The author then discusses the growing sense of the Majallah's inadequacy, leading to its abrogation in Syria (1949) and Iraq, both replaced by civil laws of foreign origin, though Iraq superficially included some maxims from the Majallah. In Kuwait, the Majallah remained in force for civil transactions. However, the author notes a revival: popular attachment to Islamic identity, the Islamic Fiqh Week conferences (Paris 1951, Damascus 1961), and projects like the Encyclopedia of Fiqh renewed efforts to codify from Islamic fiqh, producing civil laws that address the Majallah's deficiencies. This passage illustrates the oscillation between reliance on classical Hanafi fiqh, adoption of Western codes, and a return to broader Islamic fiqh in modern legislation.

Key Terms
المجلة (al-Majallah): The Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah, the Ottoman civil code based on Hanafi fiqh, covering transactions and obligations.
العصبية المذهبية (al-ʿaṣabiyyah al-madhhabiyyah): Fanatical adherence to one's own school of jurisprudence, preventing objective assessment of rulings from other schools.
التقنين من الفقه الإسلامي (al-taqnīn min al-fiqh al-islāmī): The process of codifying Islamic jurisprudence into statutory legal codes, as opposed to relying on classical fiqh texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Ottoman sultan's decree
B) The Islamic Fiqh Week conferences and the Encyclopedia of Fiqh project
C) The Iraqi civil code's success
D) The abolition of the Majallah in Syria

Key Takeaway
The Majallah's limitation was due to Hanafi partisanship; its revival came through broader, multi-madhhab codification efforts that respected Islamic identity and legal heritage.

The Majallah and the Revival of Islamic Fiqh Codification
Historical narrative on the rise, decline, and revival of codification from Islamic fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the historical context of the decline and subsequent revival of codification from Hanafi fiqh in the modern Arab world, and identify the key arguments for and against using Islamic fiqh as a source of legislation.

Core Idea
The Majallah's decline was reversed by popular and scholarly movements that produced modern civil codes rooted in Islamic fiqh.

This passage from Dr. Mustafa al-Zarqa's work describes the ebb and flow of Islamic jurisprudence in modern legal systems. It begins with the perceived decline of the Ottoman Majallah, a Hanafi-based civil code, due to foreign-influenced commercial laws. However, the narrative turns to a revival spurred by popular sentiment, international conferences, and the Fiqh Encyclopedia project. The passage also introduces the post-WWII period, where Islamic identity movements faced opposition from secularists who argued that no ready modern civil code existed from Islamic fiqh. This lesson unpacks the dynamics that shaped modern codification efforts.

3

4

Observe that despite opposition, some constitutions included Islam as the state religion or Islamic fiqh as a source of legislation.
5
Reflect on the significance for Hanafi iftaa

Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Jurisprudence and Its Characteristics. The Majallah was applied judicially in civil transactions, alongside Maliki school jurisprudence obligatory in personal status matters, as confirmed by the explanatory memorandum of the Judicial Organization Law issued by Amiri Decree No. 19 of 1959, and also the explanatory memorandum of the Commercial Law No. 2 of 1961. But this commercial law exceeded its subject and encroached upon the general civil area, which is the Majallah's sphere of influence, bringing new rules and provisions for obligations of foreign origin, which are part of civil law (not commercial law, which should normally be limited to exceptional provisions from general civil law). Thus it seemed that the provisions of the Majallah—indeed the very idea of deriving civil legislation from Islamic jurisprudence—were heading toward extinction in Arab countries themselves!! But in reality, the strong popular feeling of the necessity to preserve Islamic identity and great jurisprudential heritage, then the convening of the Islamic Jurisprudence Week conference at the French Faculty of Law in Paris in 1951, then its second session in Damascus during the unity between Syria and Egypt in 1961, and the important research in these two sessions highlighting the merits of Islamic jurisprudence, then the establishment of the Fiqh Encyclopedia project, and other developments we will mention in the next chapter when discussing the eighth jurisprudential stage—all of this renewed determination to codify from Islamic jurisprudence, and complete civil laws derived from it emerged, avoiding many of the shortcomings observed in the Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah. We will mention this briefly at the end of the next chapter (the paragraph on codification from fiqh), then detail it in Chapter Twenty-Two and its appendices when discussing civil laws and the merit of building them from Islamic jurisprudence.

Chapter Seventeen
The Eighth Jurisprudential Period
From After World War II to Today
17/1 - Introduction:
Colonialism, whose goals included cutting colonized peoples from their authenticity and past and killing their sense of identity, its demise after World War II awakened self-awareness among Islamic peoples in general, and Arab ones in particular. Movements arose in Egypt, Syria, Iraq, Pakistan, and similarly in North Africa: Libya, Tunisia, Algeria, and Morocco, demanding a return to Islamic authenticity and the supremacy of Islamic jurisprudence.
At the same time, opposing currents stood against these movements (especially from non-Muslims) demanding the preservation of the situation left by colonialism in legislation and administration.
Among the arguments of this second group was the lack of a complete, modern civil law ready-made and derived from Islamic jurisprudence, to which judges and positive law students could easily refer.
The first group was supported by the popular masses, while the second group was supported by rulers and secularists. During that time, new constitutions were drafted for some newly independent Arab countries, in which—out of consideration for the popular voice—texts were inserted stating that the religion of the state is Islam, and in some others, texts stating that Islamic jurisprudence is the
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a historical narrative about the status of Islamic jurisprudence in the modern Arab world. It first describes how the Ottoman Majallah (a civil code based on Hanafi fiqh) was being replaced by foreign-influenced laws, suggesting that Islamic fiqh was losing ground. However, it then highlights a revival: popular sentiment, international conferences, the Fiqh Encyclopedia project, and actual codification of civil laws from Islamic fiqh in several countries. The text emphasizes the irony that just as the Majallah seemed to be disappearing, a resurgence occurred, leading to new civil codes derived from Islamic jurisprudence that avoided the Majallah's shortcomings. The chapter then introduces the eighth jurisprudential period (post-WWII), explaining that the end of colonialism sparked Islamic identity movements, but faced opposition from secularists who argued that no ready modern civil code from Islamic fiqh existed. This lack of a practical legal code was a key obstacle. The passage sets the stage for discussing how Islamic fiqh was eventually codified in modern Arab legal systems.

Key Terms
المجلة (al-Majallah): The Ottoman civil code (Majallah al-Ahkam al-Adliyyah) based on Hanafi fiqh, applied in many Arab countries.
التقنين (al-taqnin): Codification: the process of compiling a set of legal rules from Islamic fiqh into a modern law.
الفقه الإسلامي (al-fiqh al-islami): Islamic jurisprudence as a whole, encompassing all schools, though here often referring to Hanafi and Maliki.

Post-WWII Revival of Islamic Jurisprudence and the Push for Codification
Historical account of post-WWII revival and codification push

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key post-WWII movements calling for codification of Islamic fiqh and explain the obstacles they faced.

Core Idea
Post-colonial revival of Islamic identity demanded a modern civil code from fiqh, but faced secular opposition and political instability.

After World War II, decolonization sparked a renewed sense of Islamic identity across the Arab world. Movements emerged demanding a return to Islamic authenticity and the supremacy of Sharia as the basis of legislation. However, these efforts were met with strong opposition from secular forces who argued that no complete modern civil code derived from Islamic fiqh existed. This lesson traces the historical context, the arguments on both sides, and the beginnings of collective ijtihad as a response.

Chapter Seventeen
The Eighth Phase of Fiqh
From after World War II to Today

17/1 - Introduction:
Colonialism, which among its aims was to cut off the peoples groaning under it from their originality and past and to kill the sense of identity, its demise after World War II awakened self-awareness among Islamic peoples in general, and Arab peoples in particular. Movements arose in Egypt, Syria, Iraq, Pakistan, and also in North Africa: Libya, Tunisia, Algeria, and Morocco, demanding a return to Islamic authenticity and the supremacy of Islamic fiqh.
At the same time, opposing currents arose against these movements (especially from non-Muslims) demanding the preservation of the situation left by colonialism in legislation and administration.
Among the arguments of this second group was the lack of a complete, ready modern civil code derived from Islamic fiqh that judges and students of positive law could easily refer to.
The first group was supported by the popular masses, while the second group was favored by rulers and secularists. During that period, new constitutions were drafted for some newly independent Arab countries, containing—out of consideration for the voice of the masses—texts stating that the religion of the state is Islam, and in others texts stating that Islamic fiqh is the source of legislation, and in still others texts stating that Islamic fiqh is a main source, or the main source of legislation.
Following that, demands began for the formulation of a modern civil code derived from Islamic fiqh. Individual efforts by scholars of Sharia and law began to produce practical models for that, followed by some official efforts: for example, in 1949, the Syrian Ministry of Justice sent a senior judge to Egypt, providing him with a letter to the famous jurist Dr. Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri (the author of the new Egyptian civil code, who had turned to studying and teaching Islamic fiqh) to collaborate on formulating a civil code derived from Islamic fiqh.
Then it happened that in the same year the first military coup in Syria occurred, overthrowing constitutional government and upsetting the balance, and the project of a civil code derived from Islamic fiqh was cancelled, and instead the Egyptian code of foreign origins was adopted. But the current of demand for a return to authenticity and the persistent endeavor to achieve that project remained strong in Syria, Egypt, and a number of Arab and Islamic countries. This modern trend—toward renewing fiqh and deriving from it—resulted in extremely important outcomes and activities that we will summarize below:

17/2 - The Call for Collective Ijtihad and the Emergence of Fiqh Councils:
The idea of a modern Islamic civil code responsive to the needs of the new era required two indispensable things:
- Derivation from Islamic fiqh in its general sense, i.e., from all its recognized schools and doctrines, without confining to a single school as the Majalla was formulated in the late Ottoman period from the Hanafi school only.
- New derivation (takhrij) based on the principles of fiqh (usul al-fiqh) and the objectives of Sharia (maqasid al-shariah), and resorting to
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the post-World War II phase in the development of Islamic fiqh. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that the end of colonialism awakened Islamic identity and led to movements demanding a return to Islamic authenticity and the supremacy of Sharia. However, these movements faced opposition from secular forces who argued that no complete modern civil code derived from Islamic fiqh existed. This led to constitutional texts in some Arab countries recognizing Islam or Islamic fiqh as a source of legislation. Individual and official efforts to produce such a code began, exemplified by the collaboration between Syrian judiciary and Dr. Abd al-Razzaq al-Sanhuri. The project in Syria was aborted by a military coup, but the trend persisted. The passage then introduces the call for collective ijtihad and the emergence of fiqh councils as a response to the need for a comprehensive derivation from all madhhabs and new ijtihad based on usul al-fiqh and maqasid. This sets the stage for the later formation of bodies like the International Islamic Fiqh Academy.

Key Terms
الفقه الإسلامي (al-fiqh al-islami): Islamic jurisprudence in its broad sense, encompassing all recognized schools and doctrines, used here as the basis for modern legislation.
الاجتهاد الجماعي (al-ijtihad al-jama'i): Collective ijtihad, the process of deriving legal rulings through the consensus of qualified scholars, as opposed to individual ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It was successfully enacted and remains in force.
B) It was cancelled after the first military coup in Syria.
C) It was replaced by a code from the Hanafi school only.
D) It was merged with the Egyptian code.

Key Takeaway
Post-WWII revival of Islamic fiqh led to calls for codification from multiple madhhabs and collective ijtihad, though political setbacks occurred.

Collective Ijtihad and Modern Fiqh Academies
Describes the rise of collective ijtihad and fiqh academies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of collective ijtihad and describe how modern fiqh academies operate, including the role of specialist consultation.

Core Idea
Collective ijtihad revives dormant ijtihad through institutional deliberation and expert consultation.

In this lesson, we explore the eighth stage of fiqh development: collective ijtihad. You will learn how modern fiqh academies, both international and national, have revived ijtihad from its slumber, and how they integrate expert knowledge from fields like medicine and economics to issue sound fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stage
Understand that this passage presents the eighth and final stage of fiqh development, starting from 1355 AH (1936 CE) to the present, characterized by collective ijtihad.
This stage follows individual ijtihad and taqlid periods.
2
Distinguish academy types
Note the two types: international academies (members from multiple countries) and national academies, exemplified by the Council of Islamic Ideology in Pakistan.
The passage highlights Pakistan's council as a key example.
3
Recognize the methodology
Learn that when a fiqh ruling depends on other sciences (e.g., medicine, astronomy, biology), scholars consult specialists, record their input, and then issue a fatwa by consensus or majority.
This integration of expertise is a modern innovation in iftaa.
4
Appreciate practical achievements
Consider the council's work on eliminating riba from Pakistan's economy and drafting the zakat law, showing collective ijtihad in action.
These achievements required collaboration with economists and bankers.

وهكذا بُعِثَ الاجتهاد من مرقده في صورة اجتهاد جماعي
ويلاحظ أن المجامع السابقة هي دولية بمعنى أنها تضم أعضاء من دول متعددة. وهناك مجامع وطنية أبرزها مجلس الفكر الإسلامي في باكستان الذي نُصَّ على إنشائه في الدستور منذ أوائل الخمسينيات، ونشط منذ أوائل السبعينيات، وهو يضم علماء من مدارس الفقه المتعددة، ويقدم توصياته الشرعية في شأن التشريعات التي تعرض على البرلمان المجلس الوطني. وكان من أعظم منجزات هذا المجلس الخطة التفصيلية التي أعدها خلال أكثر من سنتين، ثمّ قدمها للحكومة في حزيران/ ۱۹۸۰ عن كيفية إلغاء الفائدة الربوية من الاقتصاد الباكستاني وإحلال معاملات شرعية بديلة محلها واستعان المجلس في إعداد هذه الخطة بمجموعة من الخبراء الاقتصاديين والمصرفيين. كما أنَّ المجلس ساعد في صياغة القانون المهم الخاص بتطبيق الزكاة في باكستان والذي صدر في حزيران ۱۹۸۰ هذا، وجدير بالذكر والتنبيه أن المجامع الفقهية المذكورة، في جميع القضايا التي يتوقف الحكم الفقهي فيها على معلومات من علوم أخرى كالطب، والفلك، وعلم الحياة (البيولوجيا) تدعو الاختصاصيين بتلك العلوم لسماع معلوماتهم وخبرتهم وآرائهم وتسجيلها، ثم يبني أعضاء المجمع قرارهم بالإجماع أو بالأكثرية عن الحكم الشرعي في القضية موضوع البحث بناء على الحقائق العلمية التي شرحها الاختصاصيون.
(1) أول من كتب في موضوع الاجتهاد الجماعي والحاجة إليه في هذا العصر فيما أعلم هو الكاتب الإسلامي الكبير المؤرّخ رفيق بك العظم قبل أكثر من نصف قرن في رسالة صغيرة نشرها في مصر بعنوان: «قضاء الفرد وقضاء الجماعة».
Chapter (17) The Eighth Stage: 1355 AH - Now
Thus ijtihad was revived from its slumber in the form of collective ijtihad.
It is noted that the aforementioned academies are international in the sense that they include members from multiple countries. There are also national academies, most notably the Council of Islamic Ideology in Pakistan, which was stipulated to be established in the constitution since the early 1950s, and became active since the early 1970s. It includes scholars from various schools of fiqh and presents its sharia recommendations regarding legislation presented to parliament (the National Assembly). One of the greatest achievements of this council was the detailed plan it prepared over more than two years, then submitted to the government in June 1980 on how to eliminate usurious interest from the Pakistani economy and replace it with alternative sharia-compliant transactions. The council sought the help of a group of economists and bankers in preparing this plan. The council also helped in drafting the important law on the application of zakat in Pakistan, which was issued in June 1980. This, and it is worth mentioning and noting that the aforementioned fiqh academies, in all cases where the fiqh ruling depends on information from other sciences such as medicine, astronomy, and biology, invite specialists in those sciences to listen to their information, expertise, and opinions, and record them. Then the academy members base their decision, by consensus or majority, on the sharia ruling on the subject under research, based on the scientific facts explained by the specialists.
(1) The first to write on the subject of collective ijtihad and the need for it in this era, as far as I know, is the great Islamic writer and historian Rafiq Bey al-Azm, more than half a century ago, in a small treatise published in Egypt entitled: 'The Judgment of the Individual and the Judgment of the Group'.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth and final stage in the development of Islamic fiqh, from 1355 AH (1936 CE) to the present. The author observes that ijtihad, which had lain dormant, was revived in the form of collective ijtihad through international and national fiqh academies. He highlights the Council of Islamic Ideology in Pakistan as a key national body, noting its landmark work on eliminating riba (interest) and drafting the zakat law. Importantly, the passage explains the methodology of these academies: when a ruling depends on technical expertise (e.g., medicine, astronomy, biology), specialists are consulted to provide facts, upon which the scholars base their fatwa by consensus or majority. A footnote credits Rafiq Bey al-Azm as a pioneer in writing about collective ijtihad. This passage underscores the shift from individual to institutional fatwa-giving in modern times, a key concern in usul al-iftaa.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد الجماعي (al-ijtihād al-jamāʿī): Collective ijtihad: the process of deriving legal rulings through a group of qualified scholars deliberating together, often in institutional settings like fiqh academies.
المجامع الفقهية (al-majāmiʿ al-fiqhiyya): Fiqh academies: institutional bodies composed of scholars from various schools of fiqh that engage in collective ijtihad, issue fatwas, and provide sharia recommendations.
استشارة الاختصاصيين (istishārat al-ikhtiṣāṣiyyīn): Consultation of specialists: the practice of inviting experts from relevant fields (e.g., medicine, economics) to provide factual information before issuing a fatwa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary method used by fiqh academies when a ruling depends on specialized knowledge from other sciences?
A) The scholars study the science themselves before issuing a ruling.
B) They invite specialists to present their information, then base their ruling on that evidence.
C) They delegate the entire ruling to the specialists.
D) They avoid ruling on such issues entirely.

Key Takeaway
Modern fiqh academies institutionalize collective ijtihad by combining scholarly deliberation with expert consultation to address complex contemporary issues.

Renewal in Fiqh Education and Openness to Schools
Modern reforms in fiqh teaching and comparative fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two major dimensions of modern fiqh education renewal and evaluate their impact on contemporary fatwa methodology.

Core Idea
Modern fiqh education breaks madhhab isolation and enriches ifta with comparative methodology.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's introduction traces two pivotal shifts in 20th-century fiqh education: the reform of teaching methods and textbooks after the establishment of Islamic universities, and the unprecedented openness to all madhahib and early jurists. For the mufti, this marks a transition from taqlid to informed comparative analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two reforms
What are the two sub-topics discussed in this passage? Distinguish between the renewal of teaching method and the openness to madhahib.
The first reform concerns how fiqh is taught and authored; the second concerns which sources are considered.
2
Analyze the driving forces
Why were old madhhab books unsuitable for university teaching? The passage states they were written for old-style education or as scholar references, not for modern curricula.
Post-colonial establishment of Islamic universities created a new need.
3
Examine the scope of openness
Which groups are included in this openness? List the four Sunni schools, the other four schools (e.g., Ja'fari), and early jurists (Awza'i, Layth, etc.).
This is broader than the traditional four-school approach.
4
Connect to fatwa practice
How does this openness benefit codification and ifta? The passage mentions that it allows choosing the most suitable opinion from any school and eliminates partisanship.
This is relevant to usul al-ifta as it provides a broader toolkit for the mufti.

٣/١٧ - التجديد في أسلوب تدريس الفقه والتأليف فيه.
منذ إنشاء كليات للشريعة الإسلامية في الجامعات بعد زوال الاستعمار وقيام جامعات إسلامية كاملة، أخذ تدريس الفقه والتأليف فيه طوراً جديداً. ذلك أن الكتب المذهبية القديمة التي كانت مؤلفة للتعليم بأسلوبه القديم أو كمرجع للعلماء، ليست ملائمة لحاجة التدريس الجامعي في هذا العصر فانبرى أساتذة الفقه في كليات الشريعة إلى وضع مؤلفات حديثة في الفقه لملاءمة حاجة الطلاب في مراحل الدراسة العالية والعليا. فلمرحلة الإجازة (البكالوريوس) أسلوب تعليمي ولمرحلة الدكتوراة أسلوب تعمقي في موضوعات خاصة وبذلك وجدت رسائل ماجستير ودكتوراه في موضوعات فقهية كثيرة، تستوعب كل موضوع وتناقشه بتعمق من مختلف جوانبه، وتغني الباحثين والمراجعين، وأصبحت تعد بالعشرات، وبعضها في الفقه الدستوري كنظام الحكم والشورى، وفي الفقه الاقتصادي، وحقوق السلم والحرب في الإسلام (القانون الدولي الإسلامي)، وفي حقوق الإنسان، وحقوق المرأة.
١٧ / ٤ - الانفتاح على المذاهب، والاهتمام بالفقه المقارن.
إن التجديد في تدريس الفقه والتأليف فيه في الجامعات قد صاحبه الانفتاح على المذاهب الفقهية جميعاً، الأربعة الممثلة لفقه أهل السنة والأربعة الأخرى التي سبقت الإشارة إليها في موضعها بل وعلى آراء الصحابة والتابعين وسائر فقهاء السلف الذين لم تدوّن مذاهبهم تدويناً كاملاً وإنما نقلت لهم آراء في موضوعات شتى في كتب اختلاف الفقهاء كالأوزاعي والليث بن سعد وابن شبرمة وابن أبي ليلى، والحسن البصري وسفيان الثوري وغيرهم للاستفادة من آرائهم فيما نقل عنهم .. هذا الانفتاح قد أزال العصبية المذهبية التي كانت بين أتباع المذاهب في الماضي مما سبقت الإشارة إليه وأصبحت الدراسة الفقهية في الجامعات تُظهر للطالب مزايا المذاهب المختلفة وما فيها من ثروة فقهية، وتنمية فكرية وتوسعة للمدارك بمناقشة الأدلة، وتيسير على المكلف في التطبيق، وتسهيل على أولياء الأمور لعملية التقنين من الفقه الإسلامي أن يختاروا في كل موضوع من كل مذهب ما هو أنسب وأوفى بالحاجة الزمنية والمكانية.
3/17 - Renewal in the method of teaching fiqh and writing in it.
Since the establishment of colleges of Islamic Sharia in universities after the end of colonialism and the establishment of complete Islamic universities, teaching fiqh and writing in it took a new turn. This is because the old madhhab books, which were authored for education in the old style or as references for scholars, are not suitable for the needs of university teaching in this era. So the fiqh professors in the Sharia colleges undertook to write modern works in fiqh to suit the needs of students in undergraduate and postgraduate levels. So for the bachelor's degree stage there is an educational style, and for the PhD stage an in-depth style in specialized topics. Thus, master's and PhD theses appeared on many fiqh topics, covering each topic and discussing it in depth from various aspects, enriching researchers and reviewers. They are now counted in the dozens, some of them on constitutional fiqh such as the system of governance and consultation, on economic fiqh, the rights of peace and war in Islam (Islamic international law), and on human rights and women's rights.
17/4 - Openness to the schools of thought and interest in comparative fiqh.
Indeed, the renewal in teaching fiqh and writing in it in universities was accompanied by openness to all fiqh schools: the four representing Sunni fiqh and the other four that were previously mentioned in their place, and even to the opinions of the Companions, the Followers, and all the early jurists whose schools were not fully documented but whose opinions on various topics were transmitted in books of ikhtilaf al-fuqaha, such as al-Awza'i, al-Layth ibn Sa'd, Ibn Shubruma, Ibn Abi Layla, al-Hasan al-Basri, Sufyan al-Thawri and others, to benefit from their opinions as transmitted from them. This openness removed the madhhab partisanship that existed between followers of schools in the past, as previously referred to. Fiqh studies in universities now show the student the advantages of different schools and the fiqh wealth they contain, intellectual development and broadening of horizons through discussion of evidence, facilitation for the obligated person in application, and easing for those in authority in the process of codifying Islamic fiqh to choose in each topic from each school what is most suitable and fulfilling for the temporal and spatial need.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage discusses two sub-topics: (1) Renewal in the style of teaching and writing fiqh: after colonialism, Islamic universities emerged, requiring modern textbooks and pedagogical methods. Professors authored new works for both undergraduate and postgraduate levels, leading to numerous master's and PhD theses on diverse fiqh topics including constitutional, economic, international, and women's rights. (2) Openness to all madhahib and comparative fiqh: this renewal was accompanied by an openness not only to the four Sunni schools but also to other schools (e.g., Ja'fari, Zaydi, etc.) and even to the opinions of early jurists whose schools were not systematically recorded. This openness eliminated past madhhab partisanship and enriches students by exposing them to multiple perspectives, helping them understand the wealth of fiqh, and facilitating the codification of Islamic law by allowing lawmakers to choose the most suitable opinion from any school. This passage reflects a modern approach to fiqh education relevant to usul al-iftaa, as it shows how contemporary fatwa-giving benefits from comparative fiqh and interdisciplinary research.

Key Terms
الفقه المقارن (al-fiqh al-muqāran): Comparative fiqh: the study of legal rulings across different madhahib by comparing their evidence and methodologies, often practiced in modern university curricula.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It eliminated madhhab partisanship
C) It caused confusion among students
D) It led to abandoning the four schools

Key Takeaway
Modern fiqh education, through comparative study and openness to all schools, provides a robust foundation for contemporary ifta and codification.

Opening Fiqh to Positive Law and Comparative Studies
Describes the modern trend of opening Islamic jurisprudence to comparative legal studies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for opening Hanafi fiqh to positive law and state how this approach aids in teaching fiqh in modern universities.

Core Idea
Opening to positive law unlocks fiqh's essence for modern audiences.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa describes a pivotal shift in Islamic juristic thought: engaging with positive law to make fiqh accessible and relevant. Al-Zarqa argues that the complex style of classical madhhab books alienated even those raised in the tradition, necessitating a new comparative approach. This lesson explores why and how this opening occurred, and its lasting impact on Islamic legal education.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
Recognize the obstacle that sparked the opening: the complex style of classical madhhab books made them inaccessible to modern generations, even those well-versed in Islamic tradition.
Al-Zarqa emphasizes that later works of muta'akhkhirin were especially difficult.
2
Understand the solution
The solution was to open a window on positive law, i.e., study modern legal systems and their terminology to present fiqh in a simpler, comparative manner.
This allowed fiqh to be taught in universities and understood by legal professionals.
3
Examine the practical outcome
Concrete measures included introducing principles of law in sharia colleges and, in Syria, requiring Sharia judges to hold law degrees.
This made Sharia judges comparable to regular court judges, bridging the gap between the two systems.
4
Assess the significance
The opening helped preserve and highlight fiqh's original essence and precious contents, which were previously hidden behind complex formulations.
It also fostered cooperation between Islamic jurists and legal scholars.

الفصل (۱۷) الدور الثامن : ١٣٥٥هـ - الآن
١٧/ ٥ - الانفتاح على القانون والدراسات الفقهية المقارنة به. ومن أهم ما أسفر عنه الاتجاه العصري في تجديد الفقه، جانب جديد عظيم الأثر في خدمة الفقه وإبراز جوهره الأصيل ومكنوناته النفيسة التي كانت محجوبة في كتبه المذهبية القديمة بأسلوبها المعقد الذي لم يعد يفهمه نشأ وعاش في أحضانها وتغذى بلبانها، وتلقى لغتها واصطلاحاتها الخاصة كما يتلقى الطفل في أسرته لغة الأم، وبخاصة كتب المتأخرين من فقهاء المذاهب. إلا من ذلك الجانب الجديد هو فتح نافذة على القانون الوضعي، والإطلال منها على آراء علماء القانون في كل موضوع من فقه المعاملات، مما يفيد في تدريس الفقه الإسلامي بالجامعات وتأليف كتبه الحديثة لطلابها. ذلك أن الانفتاح على المذاهب الفقهية والاطلاع على ما فيها من آراء ونظريات ومبادىء فيها النفيس الكثير المفيد، قد جرّ إلى فتح هذه النافذة على علم القانون الوضعي، ورؤية ما فيه من نظريات ومبادىء، وطريقة عرض مبسطة ميسرة لا تعقيد فيها ولا وعورة. وقد كان هذا الاتصال بين الفقه والقانون الوضعي ذا ثمار طيبة وساعد على عرض الفقه مقارناً بالقانون في المؤلفات والبحوث ورسائل الدكتوراه بالأسلوب الذي يفهمه رجال القانون وبالغة والعناوين المألوفة لديهم، ولم يكونوا يعرفون مقابلها في الفقه الإسلامي، وزالت الوحشة الشديدة التي كانت بين فقهاء الشريعة الإسلامية وعلماء القانون في النصف من هذا القرن. وفي الخمسينيات من هذا القرن أدخل تدريس مبادىء علم القانون وموجز النظرية العامة للالتزامات من القانون المدني في بعض كليات الشريعة. ويلفت النظر أكثر من ذلك أنه قد صدر في سورية قانون يشترط في تعيين القضاة الشرعيين أن يكونوا حاصلين على شهادة الإجازة في الحقوق (بكالوريوس في القانون) ومن ثم أصبح كل من يرغب في القضاء الشرعي مضطراً أن يدخل كلية الحقوق ويتخرج منها، وأصبح القاضي الشرعي يضاهي قاضي المحاكم النظامية في كل شيء، بل قد يفضله لأنه درس دراسته ويزيد عليه بثقافته الشرعية. وهذا التدبير لم يحصل مثله في البلاد العربية الأخرى.
Chapter (17): The Eighth Era: 1355 AH - Now. 17/5 - Opening to law and comparative fiqh studies. Among the most important results of the modern trend in renewing fiqh is a new aspect of great impact in serving fiqh and highlighting its original essence and precious contents that were hidden in the old madhhab books due to their complex style that is no longer understood by those who grew up in their midst, were nourished by their milk, and received their language and special terminology as a child learns his mother tongue, especially the works of later jurists of the madhhabs. That new aspect is opening a window on positive law, and looking from it at the opinions of legal scholars on every subject of transactional fiqh, which benefits in teaching Islamic jurisprudence at universities and writing modern books for its students. Indeed, opening to the juristic schools and seeing the valuable and beneficial opinions, theories, and principles contained therein led to opening this window on the science of positive law and seeing its theories, principles, and a simplified and easy method of presentation without complexity or difficulty. This connection between fiqh and positive law had good fruits and helped present fiqh in comparison with law in books, research, and doctoral dissertations in a style understood by legal professionals and in their familiar language and titles, which they did not know the equivalents of in Islamic fiqh, and the severe alienation between Islamic jurists and legal scholars in the first half of this century disappeared. In the 1950s, teaching the principles of law and a summary of the general theory of obligations from civil law was introduced in some sharia colleges. More notably, in Syria a law was passed requiring that Sharia judges hold a law degree (Bachelor of Law), and thus anyone wishing to become a Sharia judge had to enter and graduate from a law faculty, and the Sharia judge became comparable to the judge of regular courts in everything, perhaps even preferred because he studied the same and adds his sharia culture. This measure has no equivalent in other Arab countries.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a historical exposition of the modern era of Islamic fiqh (from 1355 AH/1936 CE onward), highlighting a key development: opening Islamic jurisprudence to positive law and comparative studies. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that the complex style of classical madhhab books made them inaccessible to modern generations, even those raised in the tradition. To remedy this, scholars began to study positive law and present fiqh in a simplified manner using modern legal terminology, making it understandable to lawyers and judges. This led to comparative works and doctoral theses. A concrete example is Syria's law requiring Sharia court judges to hold a law degree, ensuring they are familiar with both legal systems. This approach helped bridge the gap between traditional fiqh and modern legal practice.

Key Terms
القانون الوضعي (al-qānūn al-waḍ‘ī): Positive law; man-made law as opposed to Islamic Sharia.
الفقه المقارن (al-fiqh al-muqāran): Comparative fiqh; studying rulings across different madhhabs and in comparison with positive law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was a significant measure taken in Syria regarding Sharia judges?
A) They must be graduates of a law faculty.
B) They must study only Hanafi fiqh.
C) They must be appointed by the mufti.
D) They must have knowledge of all four madhhabs.

Key Takeaway
The modern trend of opening fiqh to positive law revitalizes Islamic jurisprudence by making it accessible and comparable, without compromising its essence.

History and Purpose of the Fiqh Encyclopedia
A historical account of the Fiqh Encyclopedia project.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the origin and organizing principle of the Fiqh Encyclopedia and identify its importance for modern iftaa.

Core Idea
The Fiqh Encyclopedia makes the rulings of all four madhhabs accessible alphabetically, empowering muftis and non-specialists alike.

This passage traces the development of the modern Fiqh Encyclopedia, a project born from the need to compile comparative fiqh in an accessible format. Understanding its history highlights how Muslim communities adapt classical fiqh to modern needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initiating event
Note that the project began as a recommendation from the Islamic Fiqh Week in Paris (1954). The Faculty of Sharia at Damascus University took the lead.
This shows international collaboration in fiqh development.
2
Describe the organizing principle
The encyclopedia arranges rulings alphabetically by heading words (keywords), covering all four madhhabs in each entry.
This contrasts with classical works organized by madhhab or topic hierarchy.
3
Trace political disruptions
The Egyptian-Syrian union (1958–1961) created a joint committee, but its dissolution halted progress. Later, Kuwait and Egypt pursued separate projects.
Political factors significantly impacted the project's continuity.
4
Recognize the dual outcome
By 1966, two independent encyclopedia projects were underway—one Egyptian and one Kuwaiti. Both have published multiple volumes.
This created two major reference works rather than one.

٦/١٧ - مشروع الموسوعة الفقهية.
بعد أن أنشئت كلية الشريعة الإسلامية بالجامعة السورية (جامعة دمشق حالياً) في عام / ١٩٥٤م كان من باكورة نشاطها تنفيذ توصية أسبوع الفقه الإسلامي بباريس بشأن تأليف موسوعة فقهية فألفت الكلية لجنة لهذا المشروع عام / ١٩٥٦م وبدأت اللجنة أعمالها باستخراج الألفاظ العنوانية لموضوعات الفقه ومسائله وأحكامه من مختلف كتب المذاهب الأربعة لتكوين الهيكل اللفظي للموسوعة التي ستعرض فيها أحكام الفقه جميع المذاهب الأربعة مرتبة بترتيب حروف الهجاء لكلماتها العنوانية، فيستطيع من يشاء مراجعتها ومعرفة ما في المذاهب عن كل موضوع فقهي بمجرد الرجوع إلى كلمته العنوانية بترتيبها الهجائي، تيسيراً على غير المختصين الذين لا يعرفون موضع المسائل في كتب كل مذهب.
وفي عهد الوحدة بين مصر وسورية عام / ١٩٥٨ م أُلفت لجنة مشتركة بين القطرين للتعاون على هذا المشروع العظيم ثم بانفصال الوحدة عام ١٩٦١م توقف العمل، ثم توبع المشروع ببطء في مصر، ثم في عام ١٩٦٦ قررت وزارة الأوقاف والشؤون الإسلامية في دولة الكويت أن تتبنى المشروع، وألفت له جهازاً، ومشت فيه خمس سنوات بخطى سريعة. وقد أثار ذلك حماس وزارة الأوقاف في مصر لمتابعة المشروع لديها .. فنشطت في تنفيذه. وهكذا قام مشروعان للموسوعة الفقهية، أحدهما في مصر والثاني في الكويت. وقد صدر من كل منهما حتى (١٤١٤ هـ).
17/6 - Project of the Fiqh Encyclopedia. After the establishment of the Faculty of Islamic Sharia at the Syrian University (now University of Damascus) in 1954, one of its first activities was to implement the recommendation of the Islamic Fiqh Week in Paris regarding the compilation of a fiqh encyclopedia. So the faculty formed a committee for this project in 1956, and the committee began its work by extracting the heading words for fiqh topics, issues, and rulings from various books of the four madhhabs to form the verbal structure of the encyclopedia, in which the rulings of all four madhhabs would be presented alphabetically by their heading words, so that anyone can refer to it and know what the madhhabs say on each fiqh topic simply by looking up the heading word alphabetically, facilitating the work of non-specialists who do not know the location of issues in the books of each madhhab. During the unity between Egypt and Syria in 1958, a joint committee between the two regions was formed to cooperate on this great project. Then with the end of unity in 1961, work stopped. Then the project continued slowly in Egypt, then in 1966 the Ministry of Awqaf and Islamic Affairs in the State of Kuwait decided to adopt the project, established a body for it, and proceeded with rapid steps for five years. This stirred the enthusiasm of the Ministry of Awqaf in Egypt to continue the project there, so they actively pursued its implementation. Thus two projects of the Fiqh Encyclopedia emerged, one in Egypt and the other in Kuwait. Both have published parts up to (1414 AH).
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage describes the origin and development of the Fiqh Encyclopedia project, a significant modern initiative to compile the rulings of all four Sunni madhhabs in an accessible, alphabetically arranged format. The idea came from the Islamic Fiqh Week in Paris, and was initially undertaken by the Faculty of Sharia at Damascus University in 1956. Political events like the Egyptian-Syrian union and its dissolution affected progress. Eventually, both Egypt and Kuwait pursued separate encyclopedia projects. The goal was to help non-specialists, including legal professionals, find the positions of all madhhabs on any topic quickly. This reflects the modern trend of making fiqh accessible and comparative.

Key Terms
الموسوعة الفقهية (al-mawsū‘ah al-fiqhiyyah): A comprehensive reference work covering all fiqh subjects from the four major Sunni madhhabs, arranged alphabetically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the organizing principle of the Fiqh Encyclopedia as described in the passage?
A) By madhhab, with each volume dedicated to one school.
B) Alphabetically by heading words for each topic.
C) Chronologically by historical development.
D) By complexity, from simple to advanced.

Key Takeaway
The Fiqh Encyclopedia is a vital modern reference that organizes fiqh from all four madhhabs alphabetically for ease of access.

History and Development of Fiqh

The Fiqh Encyclopedia: A Modern Tool for Comparative Fiqh
Discussion of the fiqh encyclopedia project's origin and methodology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose and methodology of the fiqh encyclopedia project and evaluate its significance for Hanafi muftis.

Core Idea
A comprehensive fiqh encyclopedia makes all four schools accessible in one alphabetical reference.

As Islamic law faces increasing complexity in the modern world, scholars have recognized the need for comprehensive reference works that compile rulings from all major Sunni schools. The fiqh encyclopedia project, initiated in the 1950s, represents a monumental effort to organize centuries of legal scholarship into an accessible format. This lesson examines how this project facilitates comparative fiqh and aids Hanafi muftis in navigating contemporary issues.

Guided Path
1
Identify the motivation
Determine why the encyclopedia project was started. The passage mentions it was a recommendation from the Islamic Fiqh Week in Paris, aiming to compile fiqh rulings for easy reference.
The motivation was to make fiqh accessible to non-specialists and to serve as a resource for legislators.
2
Describe the methodology
Explain how the encyclopedia organizes rulings. It extracts index terms from books of the four schools and arranges them alphabetically by topic, so a reader can look up any subject and find the rulings of all schools.
This is a departure from traditional fiqh books organized by chapter (e.g., Kitab al-Tahara, Kitab al-Salat).
3
Assess the target audience
Who benefits? The passage says 'non-specialists who do not know the location of issues in the books of each school.' Also legislators and scholars who need quick comparative access.
Hanafi muftis can use it as a starting point but must still verify with original Hanafi sources.
4
Analyze the Hanafi relevance
How does this serve Hanafi jurisprudence? It facilitates comparative fiqh, allowing Hanafi muftis to see positions of other schools on contemporary issues, especially where Hanafi texts are silent or need adaptation.
The Hanafi school is one of the four included; the encyclopedia does not privilege any school.
5
Consider the current status
What has been achieved? By 1997, the Kuwaiti encyclopedia had reached 35 volumes up to the letter Lam. The project is ongoing. Reflect on its potential impact if completed and translated.
The passage expresses hope that it becomes a global resource for legislation.

الباب الثالث: أدوار تطور الفقه الإسلامي ومميزاته
تعيين القضاة الشرعيين أن يكونوا حاصلين على شهادة الإجازة في الحقوق (بكالوريوس في القانون) ومن ثم أصبح كل من يرغب في القضاء الشرعي مضطراً أن يدخل كلية الحقوق ويتخرج منها، وأصبح القاضي الشرعي يضاهي قاضي المحاكم النظامية في كل شيء، بل قد يفضله لأنه درس دراسته ويزيد عليه بثقافته الشرعية. وهذا التدبير لم يحصل مثله في البلاد العربية الأخرى.

٦/١٧ - مشروع الموسوعة الفقهية.
بعد أن أنشئت كلية الشريعة الإسلامية بالجامعة السورية (جامعة دمشق حالياً) في عام / ١٩٥٤م كان من باكورة نشاطها تنفيذ توصية أسبوع الفقه الإسلامي بباريس بشأن تأليف موسوعة فقهية فألفت الكلية لجنة لهذا المشروع عام / ١٩٥٦م وبدأت اللجنة أعمالها باستخراج الألفاظ العنوانية لموضوعات الفقه ومسائله وأحكامه من مختلف كتب المذاهب الأربعة لتكوين الهيكل اللفظي للموسوعة التي ستعرض فيها أحكام الفقه جميع المذاهب الأربعة مرتبة بترتيب حروف الهجاء لكلماتها العنوانية، فيستطيع من يشاء مراجعتها ومعرفة ما في المذاهب عن كل موضوع فقهي بمجرد الرجوع إلى كلمته العنوانية بترتيبها الهجائي، تيسيراً على غير المختصين الذين لا يعرفون موضع المسائل في كتب كل مذهب.
وفي عهد الوحدة بين مصر وسورية عام / ١٩٥٨ م أُلفت لجنة مشتركة بين القطرين للتعاون على هذا المشروع العظيم ثم بانفصال الوحدة عام ١٩٦١م توقف العمل، ثم توبع المشروع ببطء في مصر، ثم في عام قررت وزارة الأوقاف والشؤون الإسلامية في دولة الكويت أن تتبنى المشروع، وألفت له جهازاً، ومشت فيه خمس سنوات بخطى سريعة. وقد أثار ذلك حماس وزارة الأوقاف في مصر لمتابعة المشروع لديها .. فنشطت في تنفيذه. وهكذا قام مشروعان للموسوعة الفقهية، أحدهما في مصر والثاني في الكويت. وقد صدر من كل منهما حتى (١٤١٤ هـ) ١٩٩٤ م أكثر من عشرين مجلداً، ولم يبلغ كل منهما في موضوعاته منتصف الحروف الهجائية.
وإذا كُتِبَ لهذا المشروع التمام، وأصبحت الموسوعة الفقهية في المكتبات العالمية، وتُرجمت للغات البلاد المعنية بالقانون، فستكون هذه الموسوعة مدداً من الفقه الإسلامي غزير المادة وميسراً للمتشرعين، يحسب له حساب كبير بين مصادر التشريع في عالم المستقبل، وليس في البلاد العربية والإسلامية فحسب.

۷/۱۷ - فقه الأعمال المصرفية وطرق الاستثمار الإسلامية:
ظهر في العالم الإسلامي وخارجه منذ منتصف السبعينيات وحتى اليوم عشرات من المصارف والمؤسسات المالية والاستثمارية التي ترغب في الالتزام بالشريعة الإسلامية في أعمالها وبخاصة فيما يتصل بتحريم الفوائد الربوية المصرفية أخذاً وإعطاء. كما برز توجه قوي في عدد من الدول في العالم الإسلامي للتخلص من الفوائد في نظامها المصرفي كله.
وشكّل هذا التطور تحدياً كبيراً للفقه الإسلامي وللفقهاء أن يمارسوا الاجتهاد ويطوروا صيغاً عملية للتمويل والاستثمار مقبولة شرعاً وناجعة اقتصادياً.
ونلاحظ أنَّ استجابة الفقهاء لهذا التحدي المعاصر كانت جيدة جداً وتعبر تعبيراً عملياً عن المنهج العصري نحو التجديد في الفقه:
أ - فقد أُنشئت في كثير من المصارف الإسلامية هيئات شرعية تمارس الاجتهاد الجماعي بصورة مصغرة. كما برز هذا النوع من الاجتهاد من خلال عشرات المؤتمرات وحلقات العمل المتخصصة التي عقدتها تلك المصارف والتي أصدرت كثيراً من التوصيات والفتاوى الفقهية المحددة.
(۱) الآن هذا الإخراج الجديد لهذا المدخل الفقهي عام ١٤١٧هـ = ١٩٩٧م بلغ ما أخرج من الموسوعة الكويتية خمسة وثلاثين جزءاً ووصلوا بها إلى حرف اللام فقاريت الانتهاء .
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics
Appointment of Shariah judges to be holders of a license in law (Bachelor of Law). Hence, anyone wishing to be a Shariah judge was forced to enter and graduate from a law faculty. The Shariah judge became comparable to the judge of the regular courts in everything, perhaps even superior because he studied his studies and exceeds him in Shariah culture. This measure did not occur in other Arab countries.

6/17 - The Fiqh Encyclopedia Project.
After the College of Islamic Shariah was established at the Syrian University (currently University of Damascus) in 1954 AD, one of its earliest activities was implementing the recommendation of the Islamic Fiqh Week in Paris regarding the compilation of a fiqh encyclopedia. The college formed a committee for this project in 1956 AD. The committee began its work by extracting the index terms for fiqh topics, issues, and rulings from various books of the four schools to form the verbal structure of the encyclopedia, in which the rulings of fiqh from all four schools would be presented arranged alphabetically by their index terms. Thus anyone who wishes can refer to it and know what the schools say about each fiqh topic simply by looking up its index term alphabetically, facilitating access for non-specialists who do not know the location of issues in the books of each school. During the union between Egypt and Syria in 1958 AD, a joint committee between the two regions was formed to cooperate on this great project. Then with the separation of the union in 1961 AD, work stopped. Then the project was pursued slowly in Egypt. Then the Ministry of Endowments and Islamic Affairs in the State of Kuwait decided to adopt the project, established a body for it, and proceeded in it for five years with rapid steps. This aroused enthusiasm in the Egyptian Ministry of Endowments to follow up the project on their side, so they became active in its implementation. Thus two fiqh encyclopedia projects emerged, one in Egypt and the second in Kuwait. From each, by 1414 AH (1994 AD), more than twenty volumes had been published, and neither had reached the midpoint of the alphabet in its topics. If this project is completed, and the fiqh encyclopedia becomes available in world libraries, and is translated into the languages of countries concerned with law, then this encyclopedia will be a rich resource of Islamic fiqh, facilitating for legislators, and will be counted significantly among sources of legislation in the future world, not only in Arab and Islamic countries.

7/17 - Fiqh of Banking Operations and Islamic Investment Methods.
Since the mid-1970s until today, dozens of banks and financial and investment institutions have appeared in the Islamic world and beyond, wishing to adhere to Islamic Shariah in their operations, especially concerning the prohibition of usurious bank interest (riba), both taking and giving. There has also emerged a strong trend in a number of countries in the Islamic world to eliminate interest from their entire banking system. This development posed a great challenge to Islamic fiqh and to the fuqaha' to practice ijtihad and develop practical formulas for financing and investment that are Shariah-acceptable and economically effective. We note that the fuqaha' response to this contemporary challenge was very good and is a practical expression of the modern approach to renewal in fiqh: A - Many Islamic banks established Shariah supervisory boards that practice collective ijtihad on a small scale. This type of ijtihad also emerged through dozens of conferences and specialized workshops held by those banks, which issued many specific recommendations and fiqh fatwas.
(1) Now, in this new edition of this Fiqh Introduction (1997 AD), the Kuwaiti encyclopedia has reached thirty-five volumes and reached the letter Lam, nearly completion.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage belongs to the historical overview section of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm, describing the eighth period (1355 AH to present). It highlights two major contemporary developments in Islamic fiqh: the compilation of comprehensive fiqh encyclopedias and the rise of Islamic banking. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a noted Hanafi jurist, presents these as evidence of the vitality and adaptability of Islamic jurisprudence. The fiqh encyclopedia project, initiated by the University of Damascus and later taken up by Egypt and Kuwait, aims to compile rulings from all four Sunni schools in an alphabetically arranged reference, making fiqh accessible to non-specialists. This reflects a modern methodological shift towards codification and simplification without abandoning the classical heritage. The second development is the Islamic banking movement, which demanded fresh ijtihad to create Shariah-compliant financial instruments avoiding riba (interest). The response included establishing Shariah supervisory boards within banks and holding conferences for collective ijtihad. From a Hanafi perspective, this demonstrates the principle that ijtihad remains valid and necessary in new matters, and that collective ijtihad (al-ijtihad al-jama'i) is a legitimate method for deriving rulings. The author emphasizes that these efforts are practical expressions of renewal (tajdid) in fiqh.

Key Terms
الموسوعة الفقهية (al-mawsū‘ah al-fiqhiyyah): A comprehensive reference work that compiles fiqh rulings from all four Sunni schools arranged alphabetically by topic for easy access.
الألفاظ العنوانية (al-alfaẓ al-‘unwāniyyah): Index terms or keyword headings used to organize fiqh topics alphabetically in the encyclopedia.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the primary reason for arranging the fiqh encyclopedia alphabetically by index terms?
A) To follow the traditional chapter arrangement of Hanafi fiqh.
B) To facilitate easy access for non-specialists who do not know the location of topics in each school's books.
C) To privilege the Shafi'i school over others.
D) To replace all existing fiqh books.

Key Takeaway
The fiqh encyclopedia is a modern tool that makes the rulings of all four schools accessible by topic, but it does not replace the Hanafi mufti's reliance on original school sources and tarjih principles.

History and Development of Fiqh

Islamic Banking and Collective Ijtihad: Responding to Contemporary Challenges
Discussion of Islamic banking and the use of collective ijtihad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the role of collective ijtihad (al-ijtihad al-jama'i) in developing Shariah-compliant financial instruments and apply the Hanafi acceptance of this method.

Core Idea
Collective ijtihad through Shariah boards enables contemporary fiqh to address modern financial challenges.

The prohibition of riba (interest) forced Islamic banks to seek new contractual forms for financing and investment. This created an urgent need for ijtihad. The response was the establishment of Shariah supervisory boards and conferences, embodying collective ijtihad (al-ijtihad al-jama'i). This lesson explores how this method operates and why it is accepted within the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the challenge
What triggered the need for new ijtihad? The passage cites the prohibition of riba (usurious interest) in banking operations. Banks wanted to be Shariah-compliant, requiring contracts that avoid interest.
This is a modern nawazil (new occurrence) not explicitly covered in classical texts.
2
Describe the response
What institutions were created? Islamic banks established Shariah supervisory boards (hay'at shar'iyyah) that practice collective ijtihad on a small scale. Also, conferences and workshops were held to issue fatwas and recommendations.
These are examples of structured collective ijtihad.
3
Define collective ijtihad
How does it work? Collective ijtihad is a group of qualified jurists deliberating together to derive rulings on new issues. They may adopt a majority or consensus opinion.
In Hanafi usul, ijtihad was traditionally individual, but collective ijtihad is accepted as a valid method for contemporary needs.
4
Examine the Hanafi perspective
Is collective ijtihad valid in Hanafi usul? While classical Hanafi theory emphasizes individual ijtihad (taqlid of a single mujtahid), later Hanafi scholars have accepted collective ijtihad as necessary for complex modern issues. The passage describes it as a practical expression of tajdid (renewal).
The Hanafi school's acceptance is based on the principle that ijtihad is not closed; new issues require new solutions.
5
Assess the outcomes
What has been achieved? The passage notes that the response was 'very good' and produced many specific fatwas and operational formulas (e.g., murabahah, musharakah, ijarah) that are economically effective and Shariah-compliant.
This demonstrates the vitality of fiqh in responding to modern challenges.

Source Text
