۷/۱۷ - فقه الأعمال المصرفية وطرق الاستثمار الإسلامية:
ظهر في العالم الإسلامي وخارجه منذ منتصف السبعينيات وحتى اليوم عشرات من المصارف والمؤسسات المالية والاستثمارية التي ترغب في الالتزام بالشريعة الإسلامية في أعمالها وبخاصة فيما يتصل بتحريم الفوائد الربوية المصرفية أخذاً وإعطاء. كما برز توجه قوي في عدد من الدول في العالم الإسلامي للتخلص من الفوائد في نظامها المصرفي كله.
وشكّل هذا التطور تحدياً كبيراً للفقه الإسلامي وللفقهاء أن يمارسوا الاجتهاد ويطوروا صيغاً عملية للتمويل والاستثمار مقبولة شرعاً وناجعة اقتصادياً.
ونلاحظ أنَّ استجابة الفقهاء لهذا التحدي المعاصر كانت جيدة جداً وتعبر تعبيراً عملياً عن المنهج العصري نحو التجديد في الفقه:
أ - فقد أُنشئت في كثير من المصارف الإسلامية هيئات شرعية تمارس الاجتهاد الجماعي بصورة مصغرة. كما برز هذا النوع من الاجتهاد من خلال عشرات المؤتمرات وحلقات العمل المتخصصة التي عقدتها تلك المصارف والتي أصدرت كثيراً من التوصيات والفتاوى الفقهية المحددة.
(۱) الآن هذا الإخراج الجديد لهذا المدخل الفقهي عام ١٤١٧هـ = ١٩٩٧م بلغ ما أخرج من الموسوعة الكويتية خمسة وثلاثين جزءاً ووصلوا بها إلى حرف اللام فقاريت الانتهاء .
Chapter Three: Stages of Development of Islamic Fiqh and Its Characteristics
Appointment of Shariah judges to be holders of a license in law (Bachelor of Law). Hence, anyone wishing to be a Shariah judge was forced to enter and graduate from a law faculty. The Shariah judge became comparable to the judge of the regular courts in everything, perhaps even superior because he studied his studies and exceeds him in Shariah culture. This measure did not occur in other Arab countries.

6/17 - The Fiqh Encyclopedia Project.
After the College of Islamic Shariah was established at the Syrian University (currently University of Damascus) in 1954 AD, one of its earliest activities was implementing the recommendation of the Islamic Fiqh Week in Paris regarding the compilation of a fiqh encyclopedia. The college formed a committee for this project in 1956 AD. The committee began its work by extracting the index terms for fiqh topics, issues, and rulings from various books of the four schools to form the verbal structure of the encyclopedia, in which the rulings of fiqh from all four schools would be presented arranged alphabetically by their index terms. Thus anyone who wishes can refer to it and know what the schools say about each fiqh topic simply by looking up its index term alphabetically, facilitating access for non-specialists who do not know the location of issues in the books of each school. During the union between Egypt and Syria in 1958 AD, a joint committee between the two regions was formed to cooperate on this great project. Then with the separation of the union in 1961 AD, work stopped. Then the project was pursued slowly in Egypt. Then the Ministry of Endowments and Islamic Affairs in the State of Kuwait decided to adopt the project, established a body for it, and proceeded in it for five years with rapid steps. This aroused enthusiasm in the Egyptian Ministry of Endowments to follow up the project on their side, so they became active in its implementation. Thus two fiqh encyclopedia projects emerged, one in Egypt and the second in Kuwait. From each, by 1414 AH (1994 AD), more than twenty volumes had been published, and neither had reached the midpoint of the alphabet in its topics. If this project is completed, and the fiqh encyclopedia becomes available in world libraries, and is translated into the languages of countries concerned with law, then this encyclopedia will be a rich resource of Islamic fiqh, facilitating for legislators, and will be counted significantly among sources of legislation in the future world, not only in Arab and Islamic countries.

7/17 - Fiqh of Banking Operations and Islamic Investment Methods.
Since the mid-1970s until today, dozens of banks and financial and investment institutions have appeared in the Islamic world and beyond, wishing to adhere to Islamic Shariah in their operations, especially concerning the prohibition of usurious bank interest (riba), both taking and giving. There has also emerged a strong trend in a number of countries in the Islamic world to eliminate interest from their entire banking system. This development posed a great challenge to Islamic fiqh and to the fuqaha' to practice ijtihad and develop practical formulas for financing and investment that are Shariah-acceptable and economically effective. We note that the fuqaha' response to this contemporary challenge was very good and is a practical expression of the modern approach to renewal in fiqh: A - Many Islamic banks established Shariah supervisory boards that practice collective ijtihad on a small scale. This type of ijtihad also emerged through dozens of conferences and specialized workshops held by those banks, which issued many specific recommendations and fiqh fatwas.
(1) Now, in this new edition of this Fiqh Introduction (1997 AD), the Kuwaiti encyclopedia has reached thirty-five volumes and reached the letter Lam, nearly completion.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage belongs to the historical overview section of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm, describing the eighth period (1355 AH to present). It highlights two major contemporary developments in Islamic fiqh: the compilation of comprehensive fiqh encyclopedias and the rise of Islamic banking. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, a noted Hanafi jurist, presents these as evidence of the vitality and adaptability of Islamic jurisprudence. The fiqh encyclopedia project, initiated by the University of Damascus and later taken up by Egypt and Kuwait, aims to compile rulings from all four Sunni schools in an alphabetically arranged reference, making fiqh accessible to non-specialists. This reflects a modern methodological shift towards codification and simplification without abandoning the classical heritage. The second development is the Islamic banking movement, which demanded fresh ijtihad to create Shariah-compliant financial instruments avoiding riba (interest). The response included establishing Shariah supervisory boards within banks and holding conferences for collective ijtihad. From a Hanafi perspective, this demonstrates the principle that ijtihad remains valid and necessary in new matters, and that collective ijtihad (al-ijtihad al-jama'i) is a legitimate method for deriving rulings. The author emphasizes that these efforts are practical expressions of renewal (tajdid) in fiqh.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد الجماعي (al-ijtihād al-jamā‘ī): Collective ijtihad performed by a group of qualified jurists, often through Shariah boards or conferences, to derive rulings on contemporary issues.
هيئات شرعية (hay’āt shar‘iyyah): Shariah supervisory boards established within Islamic banks and financial institutions to ensure compliance with Islamic law.
التجديد (al-tajdīd): Renewal; the process of reviving and adapting fiqh to contemporary contexts while remaining faithful to its foundations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the primary challenge that led to the establishment of Shariah supervisory boards in Islamic banks?
A) The need to issue more fatwas for the general public.
B) The need to develop Shariah-compliant financial instruments avoiding riba.
C) The desire to create a unified Islamic legal code.
D) The need to train new judges.

Key Takeaway
Collective ijtihad through Shariah boards is a modern Hanafi-accepted method to address contemporary financial challenges, demonstrating fiqh's capacity for renewal.

Collective Ijtihad in Islamic Banking
Historical overview of juristic response to Islamic banking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the emergence of Islamic banking as a modern challenge to fiqh and describe the institutional response of sharia boards practicing collective ijtihad.

Core Idea
Islamic banks posed a fiqh challenge; the response was collective ijtihad via sharia boards.

This passage describes how Islamic banks seeking to avoid riba created a need for new financial instruments. Jurists responded by establishing sharia boards that engage in collective ijtihad, a modern adaptation of classical legal methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the challenge
Note that Islamic banks want to avoid riba, requiring permissible alternatives.
The prohibition of riba is a core principle.
2
Recognize the need for ijtihad
Scholars must develop new financial instruments that are Sharia-compliant.
This calls for ijtihad, not taqlid.
3
Observe the institutional response
Sharia boards are formed within banks to oversee compliance.
These boards consist of jurists.
4
Understand collective ijtihad
Boards engage in collective deliberation rather than individual opinion.
This is called ijtihad jama'i.

7/17 - Fiqh of Banking Operations and Islamic Investment Methods: Since the mid-1970s until today, dozens of banks and financial and investment institutions have appeared in the Islamic world and beyond that desire to adhere to Islamic law in their operations, especially regarding the prohibition of usurious banking interest, both taking and giving. Also, a strong trend emerged in a number of countries in the Islamic world to eliminate interest in their entire banking system. This development posed a great challenge to Islamic fiqh and to jurists to exercise ijtihad and develop practical formulas for financing and investment that are legally acceptable and economically effective. We note that the response of jurists to this contemporary challenge was very good and practically expressed the modern approach toward renewal in fiqh: a) In many Islamic banks, sharia boards were established that practice collective ijtihad on a small scale. This type of ijtihad also emerged through dozens of conferences and specialized workshops held by those banks, which issued many specific juristic recommendations and fatwas. b) In the juristic activity in this field, full openness between the madhhabs emerged, as none of the sharia boards in Islamic banks are restricted to a particular madhhab; rather, all are guided by all madhhabs in their ijtihads and fatwas.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the development of Islamic banking and finance from the mid-1970s onward. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, describes how the emergence of Islamic financial institutions seeking Sharia compliance, particularly in avoiding interest (riba), presented a major challenge to Islamic jurisprudence. The jurists' response was positive and practical: they revived collective ijtihad through sharia boards and specialized conferences, and they adopted a cross-madhhab approach, drawing from all schools of law without being confined to one. This reflects the modern methodology of renewal (tajdid) in fiqh.

Key Terms
هيئات شرعية (hay'at shar'iyya): Sharia supervisory boards established in Islamic banks to ensure compliance with Islamic law.
اجتهاد جماعي (ijtihad jama'i): Collective ijtihad performed by a group of scholars, as opposed to individual ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary mechanism by which Islamic banks ensure Sharia compliance according to the passage?
A) Following a single madhhab strictly.
B) Establishing sharia boards that practice collective ijtihad.
C) Hiring a single scholar to oversee all transactions.
D) Avoiding all financial transactions in favor of charity.

Key Takeaway
The juristic response to Islamic banking included establishing sharia boards for collective ijtihad, a modern institutional innovation.

Cross-Madhhab Openness in Contemporary Fatwa
Describes cross-madhhab openness as a feature of contemporary juristic activity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the principle of cross-madhhab openness in contemporary fatwa and identify its methodological implications for iftaa.

Core Idea
In modern Islamic finance, sharia boards draw from all madhhabs, not just one, to find viable solutions.

This passage highlights a key development: sharia boards are not bound to a single madhhab. This openness allows them to choose opinions from any school, a departure from traditional taqlid. It reflects a modern methodology of renewal (tajdid) in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Note the openness
The passage says sharia boards are not restricted to any particular madhhab.
Contrast with classical practice of bound fatwa within a madhhab.
2
Understand the rationale
This allows drawing from a wider pool of rulings to find Sharia-compliant solutions.
Especially useful for new financial instruments.
3
Recognize the methodological shift
It represents a move from strict taqlid to a more ecumenical ijtihad.
This is part of tajdid (renewal) in fiqh.
4
Consider potential challenges
Without discipline, it could lead to picking and choosing (talfiq).
Proper methodology requires consistency.

Codification of Islamic Fiqh in Modern Legal Systems
Describes efforts to codify Islamic law as main source of legislation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the modern movement to codify Islamic fiqh and identify the Arab countries that have implemented such codification.

Core Idea
Islamic fiqh has been successfully codified into civil law codes in several Arab countries, with Jordan as a pioneer.

This passage from Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa's work discusses the post-independence movement to make Islamic fiqh the main source of legislation in Arab countries. It highlights the practical steps taken to align existing laws with fiqh and mentions pioneering countries like Jordan, the UAE, and Sudan. The text also points to a larger project of a unified Arab civil code derived from fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the constitutional basis
Note that after independence, Arab constitutions declared Islamic fiqh as the main source of legislation.
This set the stage for codification demands.
2
Understand the demands for harmonization
Calls emerged to align existing laws with fiqh and change contradicting provisions.
These demands focused on civil law.
3
List the countries that implemented codification
Jordan is cited as a pioneer, followed by the UAE and Sudan.
Each country's code is derived from fiqh.
4
Recognize the unified Arab civil code project
A project for a unified civil code from fiqh has been initiated.
The author promises to detail this later.

۸/۱۷ - التقنين من الفقه الإسلامي:
منذ أن وضع في دساتير البلاد العربية بعد استقلالها نص على أن الفقه الإسلامي هو المصدر الرئيسي للتشريع - كما سبقت الإشارة إليه بدأت مطالبات بتوفيق القوانين القائمة مع أحكام الفقه الإسلامي وتغيير ما يخالف. وركزت المطالبات في مصر وفي سورية والعراق والأردن على وضع قانون مدني حديث مستمد من الفقه الإسلامي بمعناه الواسع، كما سبق بيانه .
وقد تحققت هذه الفكرة في بعض البلاد العربية وهي الأردن الذي كان رائداً في ذلك، ثم دولة الإمارات العربية والسودان. كما بدأ إعداد مشروع قانون مدني عربي موحد مستمد من الفقه الإسلامي، وأنجز فعلا قسم مهم منه، وسنبين ذلك كله في الفصل الثاني والعشرين وملاحقة عن القوانين المدنية وفضل بنائها من الفقه الإسلامي.
8/17 - Codification from Islamic Fiqh: Since the constitutions of Arab countries after their independence included a clause stating that Islamic fiqh is the main source of legislation - as previously indicated - demands began to harmonize existing laws with the rulings of Islamic fiqh and change what contradicts them. The demands focused in Egypt, Syria, Iraq, and Jordan on developing a modern civil code derived from Islamic fiqh in its broad sense, as previously explained. This idea has been realized in some Arab countries, namely Jordan, which was a pioneer in this, then the United Arab Emirates and Sudan. Also, preparation began for a unified Arab civil code project derived from Islamic fiqh, and a significant part of it has actually been completed. We will explain all this in Chapter Twenty-Two and the appendices on civil codes and the merit of building them from Islamic fiqh.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage discusses the movement to codify Islamic fiqh into modern legal codes, particularly civil law. After Arab independence, constitutions declared Islamic fiqh as the main source of legislation, prompting calls to align existing laws with fiqh. The author notes that Jordan led this effort, followed by the UAE and Sudan. There is also a project for a unified Arab civil code derived from fiqh. This reflects a practical application of fiqh in modern state legal systems.

Key Terms
تقنين (taqnin): The process of codifying fiqh rulings into statutory law codes.
قانون مدني (qanun madani): Civil law code governing private transactions and obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ottoman Family Law: Borrowing from the Maliki School
Historical example of cross-madhhab codification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa applying the Maliki position on the missing husband case adopted in the Ottoman code, and evaluate the maqasid rationale for cross-madhhab borrowing.

Core Idea
Cross-madhhab borrowing in codification can resolve hardship within Hanafi framework without abandoning it.

This passage marks a historic moment in Islamic legal history: the Ottoman state codified family law by selectively adopting rulings from other madhahib. The goal was to address social problems that the Hanafi school alone could not solve. Here we see two key adoptions from the Maliki school: judicial separation through arbitration, and a fixed waiting period for the wife of a missing husband. Understanding this episode illuminates the relationship between madhhab loyalty and public interest (maslahah) in modern Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Maliki rulings adopted
Read the passage and list the two specific family law provisions that the Ottoman code took from the Maliki school. Note the social problem each sought to remedy.
One concerns ongoing marital discord, the other concerns a missing spouse.
2
Compare Hanafi and Maliki positions on each issue
For each adopted ruling, state the default Hanafi position and the Maliki position as described in the passage. Identify the key difference in legal reasoning.
The Hanafi position on missing husband relies on presumption of life; Maliki emphasizes harm prevention.
3
Apply Maqasid al-Shari'ah to explain the choice
For each adoption, explain how the Maliki ruling better serves the Maqasid (e.g., hifz al-nafs, hifz al-nasl, or preventing hardship).
Think about the wife's well-being and the purpose of marriage.
4
Assess the limits of cross-madhhab borrowing
Note that the Ottoman code did not abandon Hanafi fiqh completely. It borrowed only where the Hanafi position caused significant hardship. What constraints might a mufti face when adopting a ruling from another madhhab?
Consider the hierarchy of evidences and the need for public interest.

وقد ظهرت هذه الحاجة إلى فقه المذاهب الأخرى لجمعية المجلة ويشعر بذلك ما جاء في تقرير لائحة الأسباب الموجبة التي صدرت بها المجلة من بحث الجمعية المذكورة عن مذهب ابن شبرمة في اعتبار الشروط مطلقاً في العقود، ومن المناقشات التي جرت حول الأخذ بمذهبه، ثم ترجيحها الاقتصار في ذلك على المذهب الحنفي لتوسطه ولعدم الحاجة (في نظرهم إلى الأخذ بمذهب ابن شبرمة لأن الاجتهاد الحنفي يعتبر ويصحح كل شرط جرى عليه العرف إذا لم يكن هناك نص يمنعه بخصوصه ولا ينافي مقاصد الشريعة العامة.
۲/۱۸ - بدء تنفيذ الفكرة في تقنين الأحوال الشخصية :
ثم بدىء رسمياً بتنفيذ هذه الفكرة فكرة الاستفادة من مختلف المذاهب الفقهية عن طريق التقنين في أحكام الأحوال الشخصية أواخر العهد العثماني، إذ وضعت الحكومة العثمانية قانون حقوق العائلة في سنة ۱۳۳۳ ،رومية وأخذت فيه من المذهب المالكي حكم التفريق الإجباري القضائي بين الزوجين عن طريق التحكيم المنصوص عليه في القرآن عند اختلافهما، وتوسعت فيه. وبذلك مكنت المرأة أن تتخلص من زوج السوء بطلبها التفريق كما يتمكن الرجل أن يتخلص من امرأة السوء بالتطليق، كما أخذت أحكاماً إصلاحية أخرى من مذاهب أخرى.
وقد أخذ القانون المذكور أيضاً من مذهب مالك إطلاق حرية الزواج لزوجة المفقود بعد أربع سنين من فقدانه، بينما يقضي المذهب الحنفي بانتظار وفاة جميع أقرانه في العمر، فتبقى زوجة المفقود معلقة حتى شيخوختها.
And this need for the fiqh of other madhahib appeared to the committee of the Majalla, and that is felt by what came in the report of the preamble of reasons issued with the Majalla: the committee's research into the madhhab of Ibn Shubrumah regarding considering conditions absolutely in contracts, and the discussions that took place about adopting his madhhab, then their preference to restrict that to the Hanafi madhhab because of its moderation and because there was no need (in their view) to adopt Ibn Shubrumah's madhhab, since Hanafi ijtihad considers and validates every condition that custom has established, as long as there is no specific text prohibiting it and it does not contradict the general objectives of Sharia. 2/18 – Beginning of Implementation of the Idea in Codification of Personal Status: Then officially began the implementation of this idea – the idea of benefiting from various fiqh madhahib through codification – in the rulings of personal status at the end of the Ottoman era, when the Ottoman government issued the Law of Family Rights in the year 1333 Rumi, and took from the Maliki madhhab the ruling of compulsory judicial separation between spouses through arbitration stipulated in the Quran when they differ, and expanded it. Thus it enabled the woman to free herself from a bad husband by requesting separation, just as the man can free himself from a bad wife by divorce. It also took other reformist rulings from other madhahib. The aforementioned law also took from the Maliki madhhab freeing the wife of a missing person to remarry after four years of his disappearance, whereas the Hanafi madhhab requires waiting for the death of all his age peers, so the wife of the missing person remains suspended until her old age.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage illustrates the practical application of the principle from the previous lesson. It begins by noting that even the committee drafting the Ottoman Majalla considered adopting the view of Ibn Shubramah (who upheld all conditions in contracts) but ultimately stayed with Hanafi law because Hanafis already validate custom-based conditions. Then it moves to the Ottoman Law of Family Rights (1917), which officially borrowed from the Maliki school on two points: (1) judicial separation through arbitration when spouses are in conflict – this gave women a way to seek divorce, whereas Hanafi law generally restricts divorce to the husband; (2) allowing the wife of a missing husband to remarry after four years, whereas Hanafi law requires waiting until all male contemporaries of the missing person have died, often leaving the wife indefinitely suspended. The text highlights the social benefit of these adoptions.

Key Terms
التفريق الإجباري القضائي (al-tafriq al-ijbari al-qada'i): Compulsory judicial separation of spouses through court arbitration.
المفقود (al-mafqud): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and life uncertain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did the Ottoman Law of Family Rights adopt from the Maliki madhhab regarding a missing husband?
A) The wife must wait until her husband's age peers die.
B) The wife may remarry after four years of the husband's disappearance.
C) The wife must remain married indefinitely.
D) The wife may divorce immediately without waiting.

Key Takeaway
The Ottoman family law code exemplified cross-madhhab borrowing for public interest, particularly adopting the Maliki position on missing spouse to prevent indefinite suspension of marriage.

Taqnin: Codifying Islamic Law Across Madhhabs
Historical account of codification project

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the process of taqnin (codification) and identify how a modern personal status law project draws from multiple Hanafi and non-Hanafi sources.

Core Idea
Taqnin selects from all madhhabs to create a unified code; it is not bound to a single school.

This lesson explores a modern example of taqnin: the Egyptian-Syrian Personal Status Law Project (1958-1961). You will learn how codification committees, guided by maqasid and practical needs, select rulings from various madhhabs and even from early jurists without formal schools. This approach demonstrates the flexibility of Islamic law in addressing contemporary family issues while remaining rooted in the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Project and Its Context
Locate the project in time (1958-1961) and place (Egypt and Syria under the United Arab Republic). Understand that it was part of a broader effort to unify four laws, with personal status as the priority.
The political union was short-lived; the project was completed but never enacted.
2
Understand the Methodology of Selection
The committee was not bound to a single madhhab. They adopted provisions from multiple schools and also from early jurists whose opinions were recorded in books of ikhtilaf. This is a key feature of modern taqnin.
The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, chaired the committee alongside the Mufti of Egypt and a legal expert.
3
Note the Comprehensive Scope
The draft covered all areas of personal status: marriage, divorce, custody, maintenance, bequests, and inheritance. It aimed to solve practical problems by consulting Sharia judges about recurring litigation.
This shows that taqnin is problem-driven, not purely theoretical.
4
Recognize the Outcome and Its Political Fate
The completed draft was submitted for enactment by the President, but the military coup in Syria (1961) dissolved the union, preventing its issuance. The project remains as a historical precedent.
This illustrates how political factors can impact legal codification.

Source Text

وألفت لكل قانون لجنة ثلاثية مشتركة من ذوي الاختصاص. وقد اختاروني لرئاسة لجنة الأحوال الشخصية، ومعي مفتي مصر ووكيل مجلس الدولة المصري أعضاء فوضعنا مشروعاً كاملاً لقانون موحد للأحوال الشخصية شاملاً لجميع أبوابه من النكاح إلى الميراث وما بينهما من أبواب وفصول. وعالجنا فيه جميع العلل والمشكلات التي تعاني منها حياة الأسرة الإسلامية، سواء منها ما يتعلق بأحكام الزواج أو الطلاق، أو الحضانة والنفقة، أو الوصايا أو الميراث.. وسألنا القضاة الشرعيين عن المشكلات التي تقام بشأنها دعاوى لديهم من أحد الزوجين، ووضعنا في مشروع القانون المذكور أحكاماً تعالج وتحل معظم المشكلات في حياة الأسرة دون أن نتقيد بمذهب معين كما اقتبسنا من اجتهادات فقهاء السلف الذين لم تدوّن لهم مذاهب كاملة ولكن نُقِلَت لهم آراء صالحة في كتب اختلاف الفقهاء، وقد رفعنا مشروع القانون مع مذكرة إيضاحية ترافق مواده وتبين المصدر الذي استمدت منه والذي كان أول قانون إصلاحي شامل في الأحوال الشخصية إلى وزيري العدل المركزي والإقليمي، لكي يصدره رئيس الجمهورية العربية المتحدة. ولكن حال دون إصداره الانقلاب العسكري الذي حصل في سورية سنة ١٩٦١م وفصل الوحدة، وبقي مشروع القانون المذكور في ذمة التاريخ (۱)!!
Appendix to Chapter Eighteen: Contemporary Examples of Codification of Islamic Fiqh in its Various Schools. 1. Project of Personal Status Law for Egypt and Syria during the era of unity between them. 5/18 - During the era of unity established between Syria and Egypt (1958-1961 AD), leaders in the two regions decided to unify some laws that scientific need required to be unified. This direction included four laws, at the top of which was the Personal Status Law. A joint tripartite committee of specialists was formed for each law. I was chosen to chair the Personal Status Committee, along with the Mufti of Egypt and the Deputy of the Egyptian Council of State as members. We produced a complete draft of a unified personal status law covering all its sections from marriage to inheritance and the sections and chapters in between. We addressed all the ills and problems afflicting Islamic family life, including those related to marriage, divorce, custody, maintenance, bequests, and inheritance. We asked Sharia judges about the problems that gave rise to lawsuits before them from either spouse, and we included in the draft law provisions that treat and resolve most family problems without being bound to a particular school, as we also borrowed from the ijtihadat of early jurists who did not have complete recorded schools but whose valid opinions were transmitted in books of juristic differences. We submitted the draft law with an explanatory memorandum accompanying its articles and indicating the source from which it was derived—which was the first comprehensive reformist law in personal status—to the Ministers of Central and Regional Justice, so that the President of the United Arab Republic could enact it. But its issuance was prevented by the military coup that occurred in Syria in 1961 AD and the separation of the union, and the draft law remained in the custody of history!!
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a historical account from the author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, describing his role in drafting a unified personal status law for Egypt and Syria during their short-lived union. It exemplifies the modern trend of codifying Islamic fiqh by selecting from multiple madhhabs and even from opinions of early jurists not associated with a formal school. The project was comprehensive, covering all family law topics, and was designed to address practical problems faced by couples. Despite being submitted for enactment, it was never promulgated due to the political breakup. This illustrates the interaction between fiqh and state legislation, and the challenges of pan-Islamic legal unification.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnin): The process of codifying Islamic fiqh into statutory legal codes
الأحوال الشخصية (al-ahwal al-shakhsiyyah): Personal status law, covering marriage, divorce, custody, maintenance, inheritance, etc.
تلفيق (talfiq): Combining rulings from different madhhabs in a way that may create a new opinion
تخير (takhayyur): Selection of a ruling from among different madhhabs without combining them

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The committee could not agree on the final draft
B) A military coup in Syria dissolved the union
C) The Egyptian government rejected the proposal
D) The draft was lost before submission

Key Takeaway
Taqnin is a legitimate and flexible method of applying Islamic law in modern states, drawing from multiple madhhabs to address contemporary needs while remaining rooted in the tradition.

The Unified Arab Personal Status Law: The Role of Istislah in Modern Codification
Historical description of Arab codification effort invoking istislah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Arab jurists' reliance on istislah in crafting a unified personal status code and identify the Hanafi position on istislah.

Core Idea
Modern codification often uses istislah, but Hanafis restrict public interest to what is recognized by Sharia, preferring istihsan.

In the late 20th century, Arab justice ministers sought to unify personal status law. They instructed a committee to derive rulings from all madhhabs and the principle of istislah. This lesson explores istislah's role and the Hanafi school's cautious approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal basis for unification
Examine the conference's recommendation: following Islamic Sharia is safest, but the committee should draw from all madhhabs and istislah. Note the phrase: 'مستمد من مختلف المذاهب ومن قاعدة الإستصلاح'.
This shows a departure from binding oneself to a single school.
2
Understand the Hanafi view on istislah
Compare istislah (Maliki/Hanbali) with Hanafi istihsan. Hanafis do not accept maslaha mursala as an independent source; they require a basis in revealed sources (istihsan).
Hanafi usul limits public interest to what is indicated by explicit texts or derived via qiyas.
3
Analyze the Saudi objection
Saudi Arabia's representative withheld approval, likely due to strict Hanbali adherence and rejection of eclecticism (talfiq) and istislah as an independent source.
This highlights practical challenges in cross-madhhab codification.
4
Assess the project's outcome
The project was not adopted at the Arab level. The author later published the Egyptian-Syrian project (from the prior lesson) as a historical model.
Codification efforts often face stalemate due to methodological differences.

Source Text

مشروع قانون الأحوال الشخصية الموحد للبلاد العربية ٦/١٨ - أكد المؤتمر الأول لوزراء العدل العرب (في الرباط ١٢/ : (۱۹۷۷ على أهمية وحدة التشريع بين الدول العربية، وأن اتباع أحكام الشريعة الإسلامية هو أسلم الطرق وأجداها للوصول إلى هذه الغاية .

Project of Unified Personal Status Law for the Arab Countries. 6/18 - The first conference of Arab Ministers of Justice (in Rabat, 12/? 1977) affirmed the importance of unity of legislation among Arab countries, and that following the rulings of Islamic Sharia is the safest and most effective way to achieve this goal. Then a ministerial committee emanating from that conference recommended the necessity of gathering efforts at the Arab level to codify the rulings of Islamic Sharia, and to give special priority to unifying personal status legislation in the Arab countries in a way that achieves necessary reforms in the family system derived from various schools and from the principle of istislah (public interest). A committee of Sharia judges and specialized jurists was formed to prepare the project, and they worked on it for three years (3/1982 - 2/1985). Then it was presented to a general assembly representing Arab countries that met in Amman, the capital of Jordan, in the late 1980s. The representative of the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia expressed reservations and withheld approval. (1) I have finally succeeded in publishing this project with its text and explanatory memorandum under the name (Project of Unified Personal Status Law for the Egyptian and Syrian Regions in the Era of Union Between Them), Dar al-Qalam, Damascus, 1416 AH = 1996 AD.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage describes a later pan-Arab effort to unify personal status law, initiated by the Arab Ministers of Justice. The committee was instructed to draw from all madhhabs and the principle of istislah (public interest) to achieve necessary reforms. The project faced opposition from Saudi Arabia, likely due to differences in fiqh methodology (e.g., preference for Hanbali school and rejection of istislah in certain forms). The author notes that he eventually published the earlier Egyptian-Syrian project (mentioned in the previous lesson) in 1996, indicating the continuity of these codification efforts.

Key Terms
الإستصلاح (al-istislah): The principle of public interest as a source of law, often equated with maslaha mursala.
الإستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference based on a more suitable legal analogy or textual indication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The unified Arab personal status law project illustrates the tension between classical Hanafi usul, which rejects independent istislah, and modern attempts at cross-madhhab codification based on public interest.

Unified Personal Status Codification: From Pan-Arab Dreams to Madhhab Obstacles
Historical account of two failed unified personal status codification projects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main attempts at unified personal status codification in the Arab world and evaluate the obstacles arising from madhhab differences and political events.

Core Idea
Unified codification of Islamic family law across Arab states remains elusive due to madhhab diversity and political instability.

This lesson examines two major attempts to codify Islamic personal status law at the pan-Arab level: the Egypt-Syria unified project (1958-1961) and the Arab League project (1977-1980s). By exploring why these projects stalled, you will understand the practical challenges of modern legislative reform from fiqh across different schools and political contexts.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two codification projects
Identify the first project: the Egypt-Syria unified draft law during the United Arab Republic. Identify the second: the Unified Draft Law for Arab Countries initiated by the Arab League in 1977.
The first is described as 'the first comprehensive reformist law'; the second explicitly uses istislah.
2
Identify the obstacles each project faced
The first failed due to the 1961 Syrian military coup and dissolution of the union. The second faced opposition from Saudi Arabia and Gulf states regarding obligatory bequest and other reforms that conflicted with their prevailing madhhab (Hanbali).
Political events halted the first; doctrinal/political differences limited the second.
3
Analyze the role of istislah and talfiq
The second project explicitly drew from multiple madhhabs and the principle of istislah (public interest). Note that Hanafis do not typically use istislah, but it was invoked as a reform tool.
This cross-madhhab approach (talfiq/takhayyur) is common in modern codification.
4
Evaluate the outcome
Both projects failed to become binding law. The second was made optional for each state. The passage notes a lack of coordination between the two initiatives.
The projects remain in the archives as historical precedents.

Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh

Complete schools of thought were recorded for them, but sound opinions were transmitted to them in books of juristic disagreement. We submitted the draft law together with an explanatory memorandum accompanying its articles and clarifying the source from which it was derived—which was the first comprehensive reformist law on personal status—to the Ministers of Central and Regional Justice, so that the President of the United Arab Republic could issue it. But the military coup that occurred in Syria in 1961 and the dissolution of the union prevented its issuance, and the aforementioned draft law remained in the annals of history (1)!!
-
Unified Draft Law on Personal Status for the Arab Countries 6/18 - The First Conference of Arab Ministers of Justice (in Rabat 12/ : 1977) affirmed the importance of legislative unity among Arab states, and that adherence to the rulings of Islamic Sharia is the safest and most effective way to achieve this goal.
Then a ministerial committee emanating from the aforementioned conference recommended the necessity of pooling efforts at the Arab level to codify the rulings of Islamic Sharia, giving special priority to unifying the legislation of personal status in Arab countries in a manner that achieves necessary reforms in the family system derived from various schools of thought and from the principle of istislah (public interest). A committee of Sharia judges and specialized jurists was formed to draft the project, working on it for three years (1982/3 - 1985/2), then it was presented before a general body representing Arab countries that met in Amman, the capital of Jordan, in the late 1980s. The representative of the Kingdom of Saudi Arabia (1) expressed reservations and stopped approval regarding the inclusion of obligatory bequest (al-wasiyya al-wajiba) in the project and some other reformist rulings contrary to the prevailing juristic school in those countries. Then it was decided to leave the project as a reference for each Arab state to take from it what it wishes if it desires (1). It does not appear from the content of this project that its drafters were aware of the unified project of Egypt and Syria.
(1) The text of this project was published in the Arab Journal of Fiqh and Judiciary, issued by the General Secretariat of the Council of Arab Ministers of Justice in Morocco, Issue 2 - Year 2/1985, pp. 267 (262-19).
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage, from al-Zarqa's Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm, presents two modern attempts to codify Islamic personal status law at the Arab level. The first was the unified draft for Egypt and Syria during the United Arab Republic (1958-1961), which al-Zarqa himself participated in submitting. It was described as the first comprehensive reformist law, but was thwarted by the 1961 Syrian coup. The second was a project initiated by the Arab League in 1977, developed over three years by a committee of judges and jurists, drawing from multiple madhhabs and the principle of istislah. It faced opposition from Saudi Arabia and other Gulf states specifically regarding the inclusion of obligatory bequest (al-wasiyya al-wajiba) and other reforms that conflicted with the dominant Hanbali school in those states. Consequently, the project was made optional for each state. Al-Zarqa notes that the drafters of the second project seem unaware of the earlier unified Egypt-Syria project. This passage illustrates the practical challenges of codifying fiqh across madhhab and political boundaries.

Key Terms
الوصية الواجبة (al-wasiyya al-wajiba): A compulsory bequest to certain heirs (e.g., orphaned grandchildren) who would otherwise be excluded from inheritance; a reform adopted in some modern codes.
الإستصلاح (al-istislah): A source of legislation based on public interest (maslaha) not explicitly addressed by primary texts; used to justify reforms that serve societal welfare.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the main reason Saudi Arabia and other Gulf states opposed the second unified draft law on personal status?
A) They wanted to codify only Hanafi fiqh.
B) They objected to the inclusion of obligatory bequest and other reforms contrary to their prevailing madhhab.
C) They found the draft too lengthy.
D) They preferred to adopt the earlier Egypt-Syria project.

Key Takeaway
The two failed pan-Arab codification projects reveal that unified Islamic personal status law requires balancing madhhab diversity, political will, and reformist goals like obligatory bequest.

Contemporary Codification: The Obligatory Will and Reform Provisions
Appendix note on codification projects and the obligatory will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of the obligatory will (al-wasiyya al-wajiba) and evaluate the approach of modern codification in reconciling different juristic schools while identifying the practical implications for the mufti.

Core Idea
Modern codification may incorporate provisions outside the dominant madhhab, as seen with the obligatory will in Gulf state reforms.

This appendix note presents a contemporary example of codification from fiqh: the inclusion of the obligatory will in a draft project for Gulf states. The obligatory will is a reform provision that deviates from the classical Hanafi school, which only permits optional bequests. Understanding how such provisions are adopted helps the mufti navigate the landscape of modern codified law. In this lesson, we will parse the note to extract the key elements of this codification process and reflect on its implications for legal practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal reform
Locate the mention of 'al-wasiyya al-wajiba' (obligatory will) and the 'other reform provisions'. Understand what makes them reformative: they are contrary to the dominant madhhab in the region.
The obligatory will is not a classical Hanafi institution.
2
Examine the codification approach
Note that the project was left as an optional reference for each state. This indicates a flexible codification strategy that respects national sovereignty.
3
Assess the drafters' awareness
The author observes that the drafters seem unaware of the unified project of Egypt and Syria. Reflect on the importance of awareness of parallel codification efforts.
4
Connect to modern mufti practice
Consider how a mufti in a Gulf state would apply the obligatory will provision. It is binding in codified form, even if contrary to the classical Hanafi fatwa.

Hanafi Flexibility on Riba: Classical Solutions
Author explains the classical Hanafi solutions to the riba problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two classical Hanafi-based solutions for the riba problem and apply the principle of darurah to a contemporary financial scenario.

Core Idea
Sharia's prohibition of riba can be navigated via necessity or narrower definition without abandoning the principle.

In this passage, Dr. al-Zarqa revises his earlier treatment of riba, clarifying that the absolute prohibition need not paralyze modern economies. He outlines classical solutions within the Hanafi tradition: temporary reliance on necessity (darurah) until an Islamic system is established, or restricting the definition of riba to the exploitative practice of pre-Islamic Arabs. This lesson examines these approaches as legitimate Hanafi ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core tension
State the conflict between Islamic riba prohibition and modern interest-based banking.
2
Classify the proposed solutions
Note the two classical solutions: darurah/hajah and narrowing the definition of riba.
3
Apply the Hanafi maxim on necessity
Recall the qa'ida 'al-darurat tubih al-mahzurat' and discuss its temporary nature.
4
Evaluate the second solution
Consider the implications of defining riba narrowly: does it permit fixed interest?

Appendix 2 to Chapter 21: The Problem of the Usurious Interest System - Introduction: I have rewritten this subject in this new output completely to remove, first, confusion that occurred in the minds of some readers from some phrases that appeared in previous editions, and to add, second, some important economic and Sharia facts indicated by scientific research that appeared during the forty years that passed after writing the previous version of this subject in the early fifties, and which were also indicated to some extent by the activities of Islamic financial institutions since their beginning in the mid-seventies.

11/21 - The previous version of this subject in past editions of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi:
The third problem: The system of interest in commercial transactions and banking operations. Islam prohibits riba definitively and fights it relentlessly, while today's commercial and banking transactions are all based on interest, which is a part whose rate is determined by laws. So applying the Islamic theory prohibiting riba apparently conflicts with the interest system. The answer: This problem can be solved within the principles of Sharia in many ways: either by relying on the principle of necessities or needs and temporary exceptional measures until an integrated economic system is established in Islamic society that makes people independent of resorting to the interest system, or by returning to defining the usurious situation that the Arabs were upon and which Sharia came to prohibit.

Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Legislation from Fiqh
where the usurers used to control the poor person in need of a loan to sustain his life as they wished. From this study and in its light, what is permissible and what is prohibited in Sharia regarding banking matters and operations of banks is then known. And do not forget that the prohibition of riba in Islam cannot be definitively judged as unsuitable for a modern economic life, for we now see an extreme economic system that threatens the whole world with its power, which is based on preventing capitalism and riba (1).

12/21 - In 1953 CE, I was invited to present a paper at the Islamic Culture Conference at Princeton University in America entitled 'Islamic Sharia and Family Rights therein' (2), and I later considered making it the first chapter in al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi because it summarizes the foundations upon which the legal system in Sharia is based in all its branches. That paper included a section on contemporary problems facing Sharia, including the problem of the interest system, and it included two additional ideas that I put forward for discussion, among the debatable solutions regarding interest, but I did not find them suitable for inclusion in al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi in its previous editions. They are:

1 - Nationalization of banks on behalf of the state; then the meaning of riba disappears from the partial interest taken on the loan, as it then returns to the state treasury for the benefit of the whole, and the danger of concentration of capital in the hands of a group of usurers is eliminated.

2 - The view of later Hanafi jurists who explicitly permitted the ruler to determine a financial benefit on loans concluded with an attached contract due to necessity, and they said that then it is not permissible for people in Sharia to exceed that interest.

(1) It is the communist socialist system established in the Soviet Union since 1918 CE.
(2) This paper was published in the book 'Islamic Culture and Contemporary Life', edited by Professor Muhammad Khalfallah, published by Franklin Printing and Publishing Foundation, Cairo - New York. 1st ed., 1956, 2nd ed., 1962, pp. 138-160.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a revised appendix by Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa on the problem of the interest (riba) system in modern economies. The author opens by explaining his revision: to clarify confusion from earlier editions and to incorporate new economic and Sharia insights from the past forty years, including the experience of Islamic financial institutions.

He then reproduces the earlier formulation of the problem: Islamic law absolutely prohibits riba, yet modern banking and commerce are built on interest. The earlier proposed solutions were either (a) temporary reliance on necessity (darurah) or need (hajah) until an alternative Islamic economic system is established, or (b) redefining riba to match the specific exploitative practice of pre-Islamic Arabs, thus potentially permitting fixed interest as non-riba.

The author adds two new ideas from his 1953 Princeton paper that he had not previously included in al-Madkhal. First, nationalization of banks: if the state owns the banks, interest payments go to the public treasury, eliminating private enrichment and the social harm of riba. Second, the view of later Hanafi jurists who allowed the ruler (sultan) to set a fixed rate of interest on loans through an attached contract (عقد ملحق) based on public need, and forbade anyone from exceeding that rate.

This passage demonstrates the intellectual rigor of the Hanafi tradition in addressing contemporary challenges. It shows how classical principles (necessity, public interest, and later juristic opinion) can be used to navigate modern financial systems without abandoning the core prohibition of riba. The author does not settle on a single solution but opens the door for scholarly debate and ijtihad.

Key Terms
ربا (riba): Usury or interest prohibited in Islam; the author distinguishes between the absolute prohibition and the possibility of defining it narrowly to the exploitative practice of pre-Islamic Arabs.
فائدة (fa'ida): Interest, the predetermined return on a loan; the author discusses whether fixed interest can be distinguished from riba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Outright ban on all banks
B) Temporary reliance on necessity
C) Charging interest if it is used for charity
D) Allowing interest only for Muslims

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school addresses the riba problem through flexible principles like necessity and contextual definition, not a blanket ban on all modern finance.

New Hanafi-Inspired Solutions: Nationalization and Ruler-Set Interest
Author presents two novel Hanafi-inspired solutions to the riba problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal reasoning behind nationalization of banks as a solution to riba, and evaluate the later Hanafi view on ruler-set interest.

Core Idea
If the state owns banks, interest becomes public benefit; the ruler may set a fixed rate via an attached contract due to need.

In this section, Dr. al-Zarqa presents two additional ideas from his 1953 Princeton paper: (1) nationalization of banks removes the exploitative element of riba since interest goes to the public treasury; (2) later Hanafi jurists allowed the ruler to determine a financial benefit on loans through an attached contract (عقد ملحق) based on public need. These show the depth of Hanafi ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nationalization proposal
How does state ownership change the nature of the interest? It becomes public revenue, not private exploitation.
The author argues that the prohibition of riba targets the concentration of wealth in the hands of usurers. If the state collects the interest, that harm is eliminated.
2
Identify the legal basis
The prohibition of riba is primarily about exploitation; state ownership removes the exploitative element.
This is a maqasid-based argument, not a direct textual proof.
3
Analyze the later Hanafi view
What is the 'aqd mulhaq? How does the ruler's authority extend to setting rates?
Later Hanafi jurists permitted the ruler to attach a fixed benefit to loan contracts due to public need (hajah). This is based on the ruler's authority to regulate transactions for the public good.
4
Compare the two proposals
Which is more practical? Which has stronger textual support?
Nationalization is a structural solution; the ruler-set interest is a regulatory one. The latter has direct basis in later Hanafi fiqh.

الباب الرابع: استمداد التقنين المعاصر من الفقه
حيث كان المرابون يتحكمون كما يشاءون بالفقير المحتاج إلى القرض لإقامة أود حياته. ومن هذه الدراسة وفي ضوئها يعرف عندئذ ما يجوز وما يمتنع شرعاً من الشؤون المصرفية وأعمال المصارف. ولا ننسى أن تحريم الربا في الإسلام لا يمكن أن أحد يجزم بعدم صلاحه لحياة اقتصادية عصرية فإننا نرى الآن نظاماً اقتصادياً متطرقاً يهدد العالم أجمع بقوته وهو يقوم على منع الرأسمالية والربا (١)

١٢/٢١ - وكنت في عام ١٩٥٣ م دُعيت إلى تقديم بحث في مؤتمر الثقافة الإسلامية في جامعة برنستون في أمريكا بعنوان «الشريعة الإسلامية وحقوق الأسرة فيها (۲)، واستحسنتُ فيما بعد أن أجعله فصلاً أول في المدخل الفقهي لأنه يلخص الأسس التي يقوم عليها النظام القانوني في الشريعة بجميع فروعه وكان البحث المذكور يشتمل على قسم عن المشكلات العصرية التي تواجهها الشريعة ومنها مشكلة نظام الفائدة، ويتضمن فكرتين إضافيتين طرحتهما للنقاش، ضمن الحلول القابلة للنقاش حول الفائدة، لكنني لم أجدهما مناسبتين للإدراج في المدخل الفقهي في طبعاته السابقة، وهما:

۱ - تأميم المصارف لحساب الدولة فينتفى عندئذ معنى الربا من الفائدة الجزئية التي تُؤخذ عن القرض إذ تعود عندئذ إلى خزينة الدولة لمصلحة المجموع وينتفي محذور تمركز رؤوس الأموال في أيدي فريق من المرابين .

Appendix 2 to Chapter 21: The Problem of the Usurious Interest System - Introduction: I have rewritten this subject in this new output completely to remove, first, confusion that occurred in the minds of some readers from some phrases that appeared in previous editions, and to add, second, some important economic and Sharia facts indicated by scientific research that appeared during the forty years that passed after writing the previous version of this subject in the early fifties, and which were also indicated to some extent by the activities of Islamic financial institutions since their beginning in the mid-seventies.

11/21 - The previous version of this subject in past editions of al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi:
The third problem: The system of interest in commercial transactions and banking operations. Islam prohibits riba definitively and fights it relentlessly, while today's commercial and banking transactions are all based on interest, which is a part whose rate is determined by laws. So applying the Islamic theory prohibiting riba apparently conflicts with the interest system. The answer: This problem can be solved within the principles of Sharia in many ways: either by relying on the principle of necessities or needs and temporary exceptional measures until an integrated economic system is established in Islamic society that makes people independent of resorting to the interest system, or by returning to defining the usurious situation that the Arabs were upon and which Sharia came to prohibit.

where the usurers used to control the poor person in need of a loan to sustain his life as they wished. From this study and in its light, what is permissible and what is prohibited in Sharia regarding banking matters and operations of banks is then known. And do not forget that the prohibition of riba in Islam cannot be definitively judged as unsuitable for a modern economic life, for we now see an extreme economic system that threatens the whole world with its power, which is based on preventing capitalism and riba (1).

12/21 - In 1953 CE, I was invited to present a paper at the Islamic Culture Conference at Princeton University in America entitled 'Islamic Sharia and Family Rights therein' (2), and I later considered making it the first chapter in al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi because it summarizes the foundations upon which the legal system in Sharia is based in all its branches. That paper included a section on contemporary problems facing Sharia, including the problem of the interest system, and it included two additional ideas that I put forward for discussion, among the debatable solutions regarding interest, but I did not find them suitable for inclusion in al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi in its previous editions. They are:

1 - Nationalization of banks on behalf of the state; then the meaning of riba disappears from the partial interest taken on the loan, as it then returns to the state treasury for the benefit of the whole, and the danger of concentration of capital in the hands of a group of usurers is eliminated.

2 - The view of later Hanafi jurists who explicitly permitted the ruler to determine a financial benefit on loans concluded with an attached contract due to necessity, and they said that then it is not permissible for people in Sharia to exceed that interest.

Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a revised appendix by Dr. Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa on the problem of the interest (riba) system in modern economies. The author opens by explaining his revision: to clarify confusion from earlier editions and to incorporate new economic and Sharia insights from the past forty years, including the experience of Islamic financial institutions.

He then reproduces the earlier formulation of the problem: Islamic law absolutely prohibits riba, yet modern banking and commerce are built on interest. The earlier proposed solutions were either (a) temporary reliance on necessity (darurah) or need (hajah) until an alternative Islamic economic system is established, or (b) redefining riba to match the specific exploitative practice of pre-Islamic Arabs, thus potentially permitting fixed interest as non-riba.

The author adds two new ideas from his 1953 Princeton paper that he had not previously included in al-Madkhal. First, nationalization of banks: if the state owns the banks, interest payments go to the public treasury, eliminating private enrichment and the social harm of riba. Second, the view of later Hanafi jurists who allowed the ruler (sultan) to set a fixed rate of interest on loans through an attached contract (عقد ملحق) based on public need, and forbade anyone from exceeding that rate.

This passage demonstrates the intellectual rigor of the Hanafi tradition in addressing contemporary challenges. It shows how classical principles (necessity, public interest, and later juristic opinion) can be used to navigate modern financial systems without abandoning the core prohibition of riba. The author does not settle on a single solution but opens the door for scholarly debate and ijtihad.

Key Terms
ربا (riba): Usury or interest prohibited in Islam; the author distinguishes between the absolute prohibition and the possibility of defining it narrowly to the exploitative practice of pre-Islamic Arabs.
فائدة (fa'ida): Interest, the predetermined return on a loan; the author discusses whether fixed interest can be distinguished from riba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi tradition offers creative solutions like nationalization and ruler-set interest to address the riba problem, showing its adaptability.

Need for a New Islamic Civil Code Based on All Madhabs
Advocates for comprehensive Islamic law reform

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rationale for basing a modern Islamic civil code on the collective fiqh of all madhabs rather than a single school.

Core Idea
Islamic civil law must draw from all madhabs to meet modern needs and surpass secular codes.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa argues against the exclusive reliance on Hanafi fiqh in the Majalla. He advocates for a comprehensive Islamic civil code built from the entire tradition of ijtihad across schools, claiming it would surpass modern civil codes in its breadth and adaptability. He also criticizes the Syrian government's adoption of the foreign-based Egyptian Civil Code, which he sees as a betrayal of Islamic legal heritage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central thesis
Read the passage and identify the author's main argument: that a new Islamic civil code should be derived from the totality of Islamic fiqh, not just the Hanafi school.
The author calls it 'renewal in the construction of the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya'.
2
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
Why does the author believe a multi-madhhab approach is superior? Because the diverse ijtihadat contain principles, foundations, and rulings that can accommodate all present and anticipated legislative needs. Also, the sources are 'near to us in lineage and easy to access'.
Note the claim that such a code would 'surpass the best known civil laws today'.
3
Examine the historical critique
The author laments that in 1949, after a military coup in Syria, the government adopted the Egyptian Civil Code (based on French law) instead of an Islamic code. He attributes this to anti-Islamic (Shu'ubiyyah) influences that cut the connection to Islamic fiqh.
This reflects the author's view that political decisions can thwart proper Islamic legal development.
4
Evaluate the implications for iftaa and codification
Consider how adopting a multi-madhhab approach changes the mufti's role: it requires knowledge of multiple schools and the ability to select the strongest or most appropriate ruling for the time and place.
This approach is known as takhayyur (selection) and talfiq (piecing together).

Source Text

He attributes this to anti-Islamic (Shu'ubiyyah) influences. The passage reflects the author's advocacy for an authentic Islamic legal revival through comprehensive ijtihad that transcends madhhab boundaries.

History and Development of Fiqh

Modern Codification of Islamic Fiqh: The Jordanian Civil Code as a Model
Historical account of modern codification from Islamic fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi usul al-iftaa permits deriving modern civil codes from classical fiqh and evaluate the role of madhhab diversity in selecting appropriate rulings.

Core Idea
Islamic fiqh is not a rigid relic but a dynamic source capable of forming the basis of modern civil legislation.

This passage presents a modern application of Islamic legal theory: the codification of civil law from the substantive rulings of the madhahib. Mustafa al-Zarqa, a Hanafi jurist, describes how the Jordanian Civil Code of 1976 was derived from Islamic fiqh, using all schools and principles like masalih mursalah. This lesson examines the legal and methodological basis for such codification within the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the historical context
The passage describes a shift in the mid-20th century from foreign-derived civil codes to codes based on Islamic fiqh. Identify the catalysts: Egyptian legal scholars, popular demands, and the need for authenticity.
This is not a classical fiqh issue but a modern nāzila requiring uṣūl al-iftā’ reasoning.
2
Analyze the Jordanian Civil Code's methodology
The code was drafted by a committee of fiqh and law scholars, with an explanatory memorandum linking each article to its fiqh source. It drew from all schools, not just Hanafi.
The use of multiple schools is key: it leverages ikhtilāf for flexibility, a principle accepted in Hanafi uṣūl.
3
Identify the sources and principles used
The code relied on established fiqh rulings (ẓāhir al-riwāya), but also on qiyās and masāliḥ mursalah. Note the reliance on the 'texts of jurists' and 'rules of fiqh'.
This demonstrates that codification does not abandon classical authority but adapts it.
4
Evaluate the authority of codification in Hanafi uṣūl al-iftā’
In Hanafi fatwa methodology, codification is permissible if it does not contradict definitive texts and if it serves the public interest. The Jordanian code's explanatory memorandum preserves the link to sources, satisfying the requirement of referencing ẓāhir al-riwāya.
The code's acceptance in the UAE and Sudan shows its operative (muftā bihi) status in those jurisdictions.
5
Apply the lesson to the unified Arab code project
The passage notes the shift from foreign models to fiqh-based codification in the League of Arab States project. This shows how a collective ijtihād can address modern legal needs while remaining rooted in tradition.
Al-Zarqa himself served on the expert committee, giving a direct Hanafi perspective.

Chapter (22): Civil Laws and the Merit of Building Them from Fiqh. And this determination is within the authority of the rulers legally. These are a few examples from many; tracing this subject does not end; and they indicate the reality we want to be known, which is that Islamic ijtihads do not [؟] from their totality a wise legal principle or a reasonable theory. 3/22 - Some Fruits of Codification from Fiqh. In Egypt, a group of senior jurists and scholars of Sharia arose since the drafting of the new Egyptian Civil Code in the 1940s, which was a foreign-origin law, and they called for deriving it from Islamic Sharia fiqh with its various schools, and they formulated the theory of contracts contained therein entirely in a new formulation that includes the same legal provisions derived from the schools of Islamic fiqh, with each article referencing the fiqh source from which it was derived, thus proving the possibility of creating the most modern contemporary laws from Islamic fiqh. These scientific efforts and their like began in other countries, accompanied by strong popular demands, and began to bear fruit in some Arab countries, where the first modern civil code in its style, arrangement, and chapterization was born, derived from Islamic fiqh with its various schools, in the Hashemite Kingdom of Jordan at the hands of a committee of fiqh and law scholars, with an explanatory memorandum that accompanies its articles article by article, connecting them to their sources from the fiqh of the schools, or supporting them with ijtihadi analogy or by derivation based on the principle of istislah and masalih mursalah. That law was officially issued in 1976 CE, replacing the Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya that was in effect in Jordan until that date. The issuance of this law broke the barrier of difficulties that had obstructed the realization of this idea since the middle of this century. The diversity of fiqh schools and the flexibility of Sharia texts were the greatest aid in selecting appropriate rulings suitable for the needs of the age. The United Arab Emirates took it in full and issued it as its civil code, and the Republic of Sudan also adopted it in full in 1983. The Jordanian Civil Code has become a conclusive argument for those advocating the necessity of realizing this idea, by virtue of the comprehensive explanatory memorandum that linked its articles to their evidence from the rules of fiqh and its principles from various schools. In fact, this law borrowed much from the new Egyptian Civil Code of foreign origin in arrangement and drafting, and from a substantive perspective, everything it found of principles and rulings that were capable of derivation based on the rules of Islamic fiqh, the objectives of Sharia, and masalih mursalah. It is possible in another revision of it and its explanatory memorandum that it become more complete and sound in formulation, and be tied more firmly to fiqh and its principles. From 4/22 - This bold official step in Jordan paved the way for the next step: which is (the project of the Unified Arab Civil Code derived from Islamic fiqh). In the mid-1970s, the League of Arab States had tasked its legal department with drafting a unified civil code project for Arab states to adopt as they wished, to unify civil legislation among them. For that purpose, a committee of legal experts was formed and began drafting the project in the 1970s, derived from foreign codifications. Then it became clear to them that the peoples of Arab countries could not accept a law far from their fiqh originality and foreign in sources, especially after the issuance of the Jordanian Civil Code, so they changed direction and formed a committee of experts from Sharia and law scholars to draft the project derived from Islamic fiqh and its principles without being bound to a particular school, and foundationalized with an explanatory memorandum linking each article to its source and reference from fiqh sources and the texts of jurists. (And I was one of the members of this expert committee). The committee began its work with periodic meetings at the beginning of the 1980s, and completed until 1984 the drafting of the introductory part of the law and the texts of the general theory.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a historical and legal-philosophical exposition on the modern codification of civil law from Islamic jurisprudence. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, a prominent Hanafi scholar and jurist, presents the argument that Islamic fiqh is not only a source of religious law but also a viable foundation for contemporary civil legislation. He begins by asserting that Islamic ijtihad collectively provides wise legal principles and rational theories. He then recounts the efforts in Egypt to base the new civil code on Islamic fiqh, which proved that modern laws could be derived from fiqh. The climax is the Jordanian Civil Code of 1976, the first modern civil code derived from Islamic fiqh across all schools, replacing the Ottoman Majalla. This code was accompanied by an explanatory memorandum linking each article to its fiqh sources. The author highlights the flexibility of fiqh and the role of ikhtilaf (diversity of schools) in choosing suitable rulings. He notes that the UAE and Sudan adopted the Jordanian code. Finally, he discusses the unified Arab civil code project, which initially relied on foreign models but shifted to Islamic fiqh after the Jordanian example. al-Zarqa himself was a member of the expert committee. This passage underscores the relevance of classical fiqh in modern legislative contexts, a key concern in Hanafi usul al-ifta.

Key Terms
تقنين (taqnin): The process of codifying law into a systematic legislative code.
فقه (fiqh): Islamic jurisprudence derived from sources, here used to refer to the substantive legal rulings of the schools.
مذاهب (madhabib): The various schools of Islamic law (Hanafi, Maliki, Shafi‘i, Hanbali).
استصلاح (istislah): The principle of considering public interest (maslaha) when it is not directly addressed in the primary sources.
مصالح مرسلة (masalih mursalah): Unrestricted public interests that are not explicitly mentioned in the sources but are consistent with the objectives of Sharia.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the significance of the Jordanian Civil Code of 1976 according to the passage?
A) It was the first civil code in the Arab world.
B) It was a modern civil code derived from Islamic fiqh across different schools, replacing the Ottoman Majalla.
C) It was a direct translation of the Egyptian Civil Code.
D) It was based solely on Hanafi fiqh.

Key Takeaway
Modern civil codes can be derived from Islamic fiqh using multiple schools and principles like maslaha, provided an explanatory memorandum links each provision to its classical source.

Deriving Modern Civil Codes from Islamic Fiqh
Describes efforts to derive modern civil codes from Islamic jurisprudence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the process of deriving a modern civil code from Islamic fiqh sources, including the use of explanatory memoranda and the role of multiple madhhabs.

Core Idea
Modern codification of Islamic law is possible by drawing on fiqh rules from all schools, supported by explanatory memoranda linking each article to its sources.

This passage by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa recounts the pioneering efforts to codify civil law based on Islamic jurisprudence. The Jordanian Civil Code demonstrated that fiqh rules from various madhhabs can be systematically compiled into a modern statute. This lesson explores how such codification works in practice, especially through explanatory memoranda and the Arab League's unified project.

Guided Path
1
Identify the need for codification
Understand why modern states seek to codify fiqh: to provide accessible, uniform legal rules while remaining grounded in Islamic tradition.
The Jordanian Code was a response to the gap between imported foreign codes and Islamic legal heritage.
2
Analyze the Jordanian model
Examine how the Jordanian Civil Code used an explanatory memorandum (mudhakkirah īḍāḥiyyah) to link each article to its fiqh sources from multiple madhhabs.
This memorandum is the key tool that makes the derivation transparent and authoritative.
3
Trace the Arab League project's shift
See how the initial foreign-based draft was replaced by a fiqh-based approach after the Jordanian success, with a committee of Sharia specialists.
The author served on this committee, underscoring the practical involvement of usul experts.
4
Recognize individual codification efforts
Note al-Qari's Hanbali code as an example of single-madhhab codification, contrasting with the multi-madhhab approach.
Both approaches are valid, depending on the legal context and objectives.

Chapter Four: Derivation of Contemporary Codification from Fiqh

... as a civil law for them, as the Republic of Sudan fully adopted it in 1983. The Jordanian Civil Code came as a conclusive proof for those advocating the necessity of realizing this idea, through the comprehensive explanatory memorandum that linked its articles to their sources from the rules of fiqh and its principles from various schools. The reality is that this law borrowed much from the new Egyptian Civil Code of foreign origins in terms of arrangement and drafting, and from the substantive perspective, all the principles and rulings it found amenable to derivation from the rules of Islamic fiqh, the objectives of Sharia, and public interests (masalih mursalah). A committee of legal experts was formed and began drafting the project in the 1970s, deriving from foreign codifications. Commentary
This passage is a historical and methodological overview of modern attempts to codify civil law based on Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh). The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, highlights the Jordanian Civil Code as a turning point because it included an explanatory memorandum connecting its articles to fiqh sources from multiple madhhabs, thus demonstrating the feasibility of deriving modern codes from classical fiqh. He then describes the subsequent project of the Arab League to create a unified Arab civil law initially based on foreign codes, but after the Jordanian model, the project shifted to deriving from Islamic fiqh without restricting to one madhhab. The author himself served on the expert committee. The passage concludes by mentioning an individual effort: Ahmad bin Abd Allah al-Qari's compilation of a Hanbali legal code modeled after the Ottoman Majalla. This passage illustrates the practical application of usul al-iftaa in modern legislation.

Key Terms
التقنين (al-taqnīn): Codification; the process of compiling and systematizing legal rules into a statute.
المذكرة الإيضاحية (al-mudhakkirah al-īḍāḥiyyah): Explanatory memorandum; a document explaining the sources and rationale behind each article of a codified law.
المصالح المرسلة (al-maṣāliḥ al-mursalah): Unrestricted public interests; considerations of benefit not explicitly regulated by revealed texts, used as a basis for legal rulings.

Hanbali Codification Following the Ottoman Majalla
Bibliographic note on a Hanbali civil code

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure and significance of Ahmad ibn Abdullah al-Qari's 'Majallat al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya' as a Hanbali codification modeled after the Ottoman Majalla and evaluate its contribution to Islamic legal codification.

Core Idea
A Hanbali Majalla: Ahmad al-Qari's 2,382-article code mirrors the Ottoman Majalla but grounds itself in Hanbali fiqh, demonstrating the adaptability of codification across madhhabs.

This passage from al-Zarqa's work notes a recent valuable individual effort: Ahmad ibn Abdullah al-Qari's Hanbali codification. Modeled after the Ottoman Majalla, which was Hanafi-based, al-Qari's work applies the same structure to Hanbali fiqh, covering obligations comprehensively. Understanding this helps us see how modern codifications (qanun) can be rooted in classical fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Ottoman Majalla Context
Recall that the Ottoman Majalla (Majallat al-Ahkam al-Adliyya) was a codification of Hanafi fiqh. This passage cites it as the model for al-Qari's work.
2
Identify al-Qari's Adaptation
Identify that al-Qari's 'Majallat al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyya' follows the Majalla's structure but is built on Hanbali jurisprudence, making it a Hanbali counterpart.
It covers obligations (iltizamat) completely, from sources to extinction.
3
Assess Significance for Ifta
Consider how such codifications provide ready references for muftis in modern contexts, streamlining the fatwa process while maintaining madhhab specificity.
The publication details (authored 1343 AH, published 1401 AH) show the ongoing relevance.

Historical Origins of the Contract: From Barter to Obligation
Historical narrative of contract evolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the historical evolution of contracts from immediate barter to deferred obligations and explain its relevance to Hanafi legal theory.

Core Idea
Contracts began as immediate exchanges; the concept of future obligations is a later human development.

This lesson provides a brief historical background on the contract. Understanding that future obligations were not always present helps us appreciate the Shariah's robust framework for binding promises. The Hanafi school, with its emphasis on custom (urf) and necessity (darura), accommodates this evolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the earliest form of exchange
According to the passage, the first known transaction was immediate barter (al-tabādul al-fawrī), where giving and taking occurred simultaneously.
This is a historical claim, not a fiqh ruling.
2
Recognize the absence of future obligations
Contracts for future performance were not known in early eras; all exchanges were spot transactions.
Highlight that this is a foundational shift in human economic activity.
3
Note the speculative nature of historical origins
The author admits that the precise origin of contractual obligations is unknown and is inferred from social evolution.
This humility about historical knowledge is important for legal theory.
4
Connect to Hanafi legal thought
Consider how the Hanafi school's flexibility with ‘urf (custom) and its recognition of ijmā‘ as a source allow it to validate modern deferred contracts that were not practiced initially.
The Hanafi school views the essence of contract as mutual consent (ījāb wa qabūl), not the timing of performance.
