This passage offers a brief historical overview of the contract (العقد). The author notes that contracts have been known since ancient times, possibly originating after acquiring permissible goods (مباحات) as a means of ownership. The exact origin is obscure, known only through conjecture based on social evolution. The author reports the view of some foreign researchers that the earliest form of exchange was immediate barter (التبادل الفوري), where giving and receiving were simultaneous. Future obligations—promises to perform later—were not known in early times. This historical perspective underscores that the concept of deferred obligations is a later development in human civilization.

Historical Origins of Binding Contracts: Blood Money Deferment
Tracing the birth of contractual obligation from blood feud to deferred payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the historical origin of binding contracts according to Islamic legal theory and explain how the practice of diyah deferment shaped the concept of contractual obligation.

Core Idea
The first binding agreement was a promise to pay blood money later — debt was born from peace.

While we often think of contracts as everyday sales and leases, Islamic legal historians trace the very idea of a binding obligation to a darker origin: the need to prevent blood feuds. This lesson explores how the pre-Islamic custom of blood money (diyah) led to the earliest form of a contractual promise — a deferment of payment secured by a pledge. Understanding this context reveals why Islamic law places such emphasis on fulfilling promises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-Islamic problem
What was the original consequence of aggression in Arab society? The passage states that aggression required actual retaliation (tha'r) by war between families.
2
Trace the shift to diyah
Society moved from retaliation to monetary compensation. The aggressor could pay blood money instead of facing revenge.
This shows the evolution from violence to negotiation.
3
Identify the contractual element
When the aggressor could not pay immediately, he asked for a delay (istimhal) and gave a pledge (rahina). That agreement to defer payment is the historical origin of the binding contract.
The passage explicitly says: 'the historical origin of the binding contract is the agreement to grant a delay in paying diyah.'
4
Recognize other origin theories
Others view arbitration (tahkim) as the origin of contracts. The passage mentions this alternative briefly.
Both theories highlight the need for trust and commitment.
5
Connect to Islamic respect for contracts
Religions, especially Islam, amplified the sanctity of such obligations. The passage states that religions had the greatest effect in the emergence of contract and respect for obligations.
This sets the stage for Islamic contract law.

الباب السادس : نظرية العقود وما إليها في الفقه
تولد ذلك عن طريق فكرة دية القتلى. فقد كان الاعتداء يوجب الثأر الفعلي بالحرب بين الأسرتين ثم عرف أخذ الدية مكان الثأر، وقد لا يجدها لديه المعتدي، فيستمهل لجمعها من أسرته أو قبيلته، ويضع في مقابلها رهينة إلى أجل.
وعلى هذا يكون الأصل التاريخي للتعاقد الالتزامي هو الاتفاق الذي يتم على إعطاء مهلة في دفع الدية.
ويرى آخرون أن أصل العقد هو اتفاق الخصمين على التحكيم بينهما. ولكن على كل تقدير قد كان للأديان أعظم الأثر في ظهور التعاقد واحترام الالتزامات.
ر: نظرية العقد للأستاذ عبد الرزاق أحمد السنهوري في بحث سلطان الإرادة، ف/ ۸۹).
والعقد من أهم الظواهر المدنية ومقاييس حرمة الإرادة وحريتها. وقد اعترت العقد أطوار مختلفة في تاريخ التشريع من حيث الشكلية والحرية وقوة الاعتبار :
وكلما تحرر العقد من قيود الشكلية فيما لا تتعلق به مصلحة عامة كان أقرب نسباً إلى الرقي الإنساني والحضارة الكاملة. وقد كان العقد لدى بعض الأمم قبل الإسلام مطوقاً ومثقلاً بالشكليات :
أ - ففي التشريع الروماني القديم كان لأنواع العقود من بيع ونكاح وغيرهما مراسم وأشكال لا تعتبر العقود إذا لم تمر بها.
فمن ذلك الطريقة المعروفة في البيع عند الرومانيين باسم «مانسیباسيو mancipatio» وقد يسمونها أيضاً طريقة النحاس والميزان لأنهم يوجبون فيها حمل الميزان والضرب بالنقد النحاسي عليه وكان يشترط لديهم في البيع حضور المبيع في مجلس البيع، فلم يكن قابلاً للبيع إلا الأموال المنقولة. ولما سوغوا بيع الأراضي كان لا بد في عقد «المانسيباسيو» في أول الأمر من إحضار جزء من تراب الأرض رمزاً إلى حضورها في مجلس العقد كالمنقولات .
فكانت العقود لديهم خاضعة لصور من الشكلية المادية الابتدائية (١).
ب - وفي جاهلية العرب كان بيوع تطغى فيها الشكلية على حرية أحد العاقدين وإرادته كبيع الملامسة والمنابذة وإلقاء الحجر، فقد تعورف فيها أن المشتري إذ لمس المبيع أو ألقى عليه حجراً، أو نبذه إليه البائع فقد لزم العقد.
وقد نهى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن هذه البيوع، وجاء التشريع الإسلامي على يديه بإطلاق العقود من القيود السخيفة وإخضاعها لمجرد الإيجاب والقبول الصادِرَيْن بالتراضي والأهلية واعتبر فيها هذا التراضي هو الملزم دون الأشكال والمراسم .
حتى لقد اعتد الفقه الإسلامي بما يغني عن الإيجاب والقبول في الدلالة على توافق الإرادتين، ذلك التوافق الذي هو الأصل في اعتبار العقود، فسوّغ التعاطي في البيع ونحوه ، واعتبر به العقد منعقداً في التعاطي يكون بقبض المبيع بعد معرفة الثمن، كما لو وجد إنسان متاعاً في بيع حانوت مكتوباً عليه ثمنه أو كان معروف الثمن، فأخذه وناول البائع ثمنه، فقبضه منه راضياً دون إيجاب وقبول باللفظ، فإن البيع ينبرم بينهما بذلك (ر: ف ۹/۳۰ - ۱۰).
(1) انظر الحقوق الرومانية Droit romain لكل من الأستاذين مونيه و «جيفار» الفرنسيين . ويقول الأستاذ الدكتور محسن البرازي في كتابه الحقوق الرومانية ١٥٤/٢ : إن المراسم والأشكال ركن أساسي في الحقوق الرومانية التي تمتاز بهذا الطابع. ولئن صارت فيها بعض العقود تتم بالتراضي كالبيع والإجارة والشركة فما ذلك إلا للضرورة على أثر اتساع الحياة الاقتصادية وكاستثناء القاعدة .
Chapter Six: Theory of Contracts and Related Matters in Fiqh. This arose from the concept of blood money for the slain. Aggression required actual retaliation by war between the two families, then they came to know taking blood money in place of retaliation. The aggressor may not have it with him, so he asks for a delay to collect it from his family or tribe, and gives a pledge in return until a term. Upon this, the historical origin of binding contract is the agreement to grant a delay in paying blood money. Others see that the origin of contract is the agreement of the two litigants to arbitration between them. But in any case, religions had the greatest effect in the emergence of contract and respect for obligations. Cf: ‘Theory of Contract’ by Professor Abd al-Razzaq Ahmad al-Sanhuri in the study ‘Sultan al-Irada’, p. 89. Contract is one of the most important civil phenomena and measures of the sanctity and freedom of will. Contract has undergone various phases in the history of legislation in terms of formality, freedom, and binding force. The more a contract is freed from the constraints of formality in matters where no public interest is involved, the closer it is to human progress and complete civilization. Contract among some nations before Islam was fettered and burdened with formalities: A) In ancient Roman legislation, various types of contracts like sale, marriage, and others had ceremonies and forms; contracts were not considered if they did not go through them. Among them is the well-known method in sale among the Romans called ‘mancipatio’ which they also call the ‘copper and scale’ method because they required carrying the scale and striking copper coin upon it. They required in sale the presence of the sold item in the session of sale, so only movable property was eligible for sale. When they permitted sale of land, in the beginning it was necessary in the mancipatio contract to bring a part of the earth’s soil as a symbol of its presence in the contract session like movables. So contracts among them were subject to forms of primitive material formality (1). B) In the pre-Islamic pagan period, there were sales in which formality dominated the freedom and will of one of the contracting parties, like the sale of touching, throwing, and stone-throwing. It was customary that if the buyer touched the goods or threw a stone on it, or the seller threw it to him, the contract became binding. The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade these sales, and Islamic legislation came at his hands to release contracts from ridiculous constraints and subject them merely to offer and acceptance made by mutual consent and capacity, and it considered this mutual consent as binding without forms and ceremonies. So much so that Islamic jurisprudence recognized what suffices in place of offer and acceptance in indicating the agreement of the two wills, which agreement is the basis for considering contracts valid. It permitted mu'atah (transaction by mutual giving) in sale and the like, and considered the contract concluded by mu'atah, which is by taking possession of the sold item after knowing the price, such as if a person finds goods in a shop with a price written on it or it has a known price, takes it and hands the price to the seller, and the seller takes it willingly without verbal offer and acceptance, then the sale is concluded between them (cf: Fiqh 9/30-10). (1) See ‘Roman Law’ by the French professors Monier and Giffard. Professor Dr. Muhsin al-Barazi says in his book ‘Roman Law’ vol. 2 p. 154: “Ceremonies and forms are an essential pillar of Roman law which is characterized by this stamp. Even if some contracts among them are concluded by mutual consent like sale, lease, and partnership, that is only out of necessity due to the expansion of economic life and as an exception to the rule.”
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a historical and philosophical introduction to the theory of contracts in Islamic law. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by tracing the origin of binding contracts to the institution of blood money (diyah) in pre-Islamic Arab custom. Originally, aggression led to actual blood feud, but gradually the payment of diyah replaced retaliation, and when the aggressor could not pay immediately, he would seek a delay and pledge a surety. The agreement for deferment is seen as the historical root of contractual obligation. Some scholars, however, trace contract origins to arbitration agreements. Regardless, religions—especially Islam—greatly influenced the development and respect for contracts. The author then notes that contract is a measure of the sanctity and freedom of will, and its history shows evolution from formality to freedom. He contrasts pre-Islamic Roman formalism (e.g., mancipatio requiring scales and copper, and the presence of the object) with pre-Islamic Arab practices like the sales of touch, throw, and stone-throwing, where irrational formalities dictated binding effect. The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade these sales, and Islamic law liberated contracts by making mutual consent (offer and acceptance) the sole criterion, eliminating pointless rituals. In fact, Islamic law went further: it recognized transactions by mutual giving (mu'atah) without verbal offer and acceptance, if the circumstances clearly indicate mutual agreement. For example, taking a priced item from a shop and handing over the price with mutual consent concludes the sale. This shows Islam's superior legal philosophy that prioritizes substance over form. The passage is purely descriptive and theoretical, establishing the Islamic legal philosophy of contract freedom.

Key Terms
عقد (ʿaqd): A legal obligation arising from offer and acceptance, or any agreement indicating mutual consent. The passage traces its historical origins to agreements for diyah deferment or arbitration.
التعاطي (al-taʿāṭī): A transaction concluded by mutual giving without verbal offer and acceptance, where the action clearly indicates consent, such as taking a priced item and handing over the price.
شكلية (shakliyya): Formalism; the requirement of specific ceremonies, acts, or words for a contract to be valid, as seen in Roman and pre-Islamic Arab customs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the historical origin of the binding contract?
A) The sale of goods at a market
B) The agreement to grant a delay in paying blood money
C) The ceremony of mancipatio
D) The marriage contract

Key Takeaway
The historical origin of binding contracts lies in the practice of deferring blood money payment, which laid the foundation for Islamic contract law.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
10 min
Islamic Reform: Contracts Freed from Formality and the Validity of Mu'atah
Analyzing the Islamic revolution in contract law and the Hanafi acceptance of conduct-based contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Islamic law abolished irrational formalities in contracts and evaluate the Hanafi ruling on mu'atah (transaction by conduct) as a valid contract conclusion.

Core Idea
Islam replaced pagan and Roman rituals with simple mutual consent — even silence can be a valid contract.

Imagine a contract where touching the goods or throwing a stone makes it binding. This was the reality in pre-Islamic Arabia. The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade such sales, and Islamic law established that only free consent matters. This lesson explores the transformation and the remarkable Hanafi doctrine of mu'atah, where even verbal words are unnecessary if actions clearly show agreement.

Guided Path
1
Review pre-Islamic Arab sales
Describe the sales of touch (al-mulamasa), throw (al-munabadha), and stone-throwing (ilqa' al-hajar). The passage explains that in these sales, a mere physical act (touching, throwing, or tossing a stone) made the contract binding, disregarding actual consent.
These sales were later prohibited by the Prophet.
2
Identify the Prophet's prohibition
The Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade these sales because they were based on arbitrary actions rather than free mutual consent.
The phrase 'نهى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن هذه البيوع' is key.
3
State the Islamic principle
Islamic legislation released contracts from such 'ridiculous constraints' (قيود سخيفة) and subjected them merely to offer and acceptance (ijab wa qabul) made by mutual consent (taradi) and capacity (ahliyya). Mutual consent is the sole binding element.
This is the core reform: replacing form with substance.
4
Recognize the Hanafi extension to mu'atah
Hanafi law went further: it recognized that actions alone can indicate agreement, dispensing with verbal offer and acceptance. This is called mu'atah (transaction by mutual giving). The passage gives the example of taking a priced item from a shop and paying the price, which concludes the sale.
Mu'atah is valid in any transaction where the subject and price are known, based on custom.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
How does mu'atah map to a modern vending machine purchase? The machine displays a price, the customer inserts money and takes the item — no verbal exchange occurs. According to Hanafi law, this is a valid mu'atah sale.
This shows the practical relevance of the historical doctrine.

What does the passage identify as the core Islamic reform regarding contracts?

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
The Subject Matter Must Be Lawful: A Qāʿidah in Contract Validity
Introduces the condition that the subject matter must be permissible and critiques a judicial misapplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qāʿidah that the subject matter must be lawful (mubāḥ) to identify void contracts in contemporary legal contexts and distinguish ownership-transferring contracts from trusts.

Core Idea
A contract's object must be permissible; if the object is inherently forbidden, the contract is void.

A contract is built upon its subject matter (mahall). If the subject matter itself is unlawful—whether by Sharīʿah or by positive law—the contract cannot stand. This lesson explores this foundational principle through classical and modern examples, and examines a real case where misapplication led to injustice.

Guided Path
1
State the Qāʿidah
Identify the general ruling: the subject matter of a contract must be permissible (mubāḥ) for the contract to be valid. If the object is inherently prohibited, the contract is void (bāṭil).
This is a sub-maxim of 'al-aṣl fī al-ʿuqūd al-ṣiḥḥah' but specifies a condition.
2
Identify the Scope of the Qāʿidah
List which types of contracts this condition applies to. From the passage: it applies to contracts that transfer ownership (sale, lease, settlement, marriage dower).
The scope includes all muʿāwaḍāt (exchange contracts).
3
Analyze the Examples (Furūʿ)
Work through the two examples: (a) game hunted outside the Ḥaram is like carrion, so its sale is void. (b) Contracts in gold currency after a prohibition by law are void. Identify the underlying cause (ʿillah): the object is legally forbidden.
The first example is based on a sharʿī prohibition; the second on a positive law (qānūn).
4
Identify the Exception (Istithnāʾ)
The author distinguishes deposits (wadīʿah): even if the deposited item is gold, the deposit contract does not transfer ownership, so the prohibition does not apply. The qāʿidah is restricted to contracts that transfer ownership.
This is a crucial distinction for iftāʾ: not all dealings with gold are void.
5
Apply the Qāʿidah in a Fatwā Context
Construct a fatwā for a deposit case: the depositary must return the gold in specie because the undertaking is not a transfer of ownership. The misapplication by some judges is identified as incorrect tatbīq.
The fatwā must cite the qāʿidah and the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۳۱) الشرائط العامة لانعقاد العقود
اصطاد من خارج الحرم، فبيعه باطل كما أن أكله حرام لأنه في حكم الميتة بمقتضى النص الشرعي الذي يمنع ذلك
ومثل ذلك أيضاً في سورية اليوم كل عقد يعقد بالنقود الذهبية، فإنه باطل بعد أن منع التعاقد بها بقانون خاص
(1) هذا القانون قد صدر عن المفوض الفرنسي في ٢٦ كانون الثاني سنة ١٩٤٠ م تحت رقم ١٨ ل ر/ في عهد الانتداب الفرنسي على البلاد السورية واللبنانية، ولا يزال معمولاً به إلى اليوم. فقد اعتبر التعامل بالذهب لاغياً، وأوجب في العقود السابقة أن يدفع العاقد مقابل الذهب الملتزم به ورقاً نقدياً سورياً بمعدل السعر الرسمي الذي تذيعه المفوضية الفرنسية.
وقد جاء فيه التعبير والترجمة بلفظ التعهدات engagements المعقودة بالذهب. وقد تخبط لدينا القضاء الأجنبي الزائل والوطني الحاضر كثيراً في تطبيق هذا القانون، حتى قضى بعض الحكام بأن الودائع الذهبية لا يكلف الوديع بإعادتها عيناً، بل بإعادة ما يعادلها من الورق النقدي بحسب التسعيرة الرسمية لا بسعر السوق. وبذلك يضيع على المودع أكثر من نصف قيمة الوديعة، لأن التسعيرة الرسمية لقيمة الذهب بالورق النقدي السوري أقل من نصف السعر الواقعي في السوق. وهذا خطأ والصواب أن المراد بلفظ التعهدات الوارد في القانون المذكور إنما هو التعهدات التي يكون تنفيذها ناقلاً للملكية كالبيع والإجارة والصلح، حتى النكاح إذا عقد على مهر بالنقود الذهبية وسائر المعاملات التي فيها تعهد بأداء مال يخرج من ملك ويدخل في ملك آخر .
فلا يشمل هذا عقد الإيداع بل يجب أن تعاد الوديعة الذهبية عيناً، لأن التعهد بإعادتها ليس فيه نقل ملكية بل هي واجبة الحفظ على ملكية صاحبها المودع، بدليل أن القانون المذكور لم يعتبر الذهب مادة ممنوعة الاقتناء وواجبة المصادرة، بل منع التعامل به فمن كان يحق له اقتناء الذهب وحفظه بنفسه يحق له حفظه إبداعاً بواسطة غيره، فيلزم الوديع رد عينه. وبذلك تزول بعض مظالم هذا القانون السياسي وسيئاته
Chapter 31: General Conditions for Contract Formation. If someone hunts outside the Haram, its sale is void just as eating it is forbidden because it is like carrion by virtue of the legal text that prohibits that. Similarly, in Syria today, every contract made with gold currency is void after transactions in it were prohibited by a special law. (1) This law was issued by the French High Commissioner on January 26, 1940 under number 18 L/R during the French mandate over Syria and Lebanon, and it is still in effect today. It considered dealing in gold void, and required in previous contracts that the contracting party pay for the gold obligated in Syrian paper currency at the official rate announced by the French High Commission. The expression used was 'engagements' contracted in gold. Our foreign (now defunct) and domestic judiciary have faltered greatly in applying this law, to the extent that some judges ruled that gold deposits do not require the depositary to return the actual gold, but only to return its equivalent in paper currency according to the official rate, not the market price. Thus, the depositor loses more than half the value of the deposit because the official rate of gold to Syrian paper currency is less than half the actual market price. This is erroneous. The correct interpretation is that the word 'engagements' in the said law refers to engagements whose execution transfers ownership, such as sale, lease, and settlement, even marriage if the dower is in gold currency, and all transactions that involve an undertaking to pay money that leaves one ownership and enters another's. It does not include the contract of deposit (wadi'ah); rather, the gold deposit must be returned as the same gold because the undertaking to return it does not involve transfer of ownership; it is merely an obligation to preserve it on behalf of its owner, the depositor. Evidence: the said law did not consider gold a prohibited item subject to confiscation; it merely prohibited dealing in it. Thus, whoever has the right to possess and preserve gold himself has the right to preserve it by deposit through another, so the depositary is obliged to return its actual substance. This removes some of the injustices and evils of this political law.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a general condition for contract formation: the subject matter (محل) must be permissible (مباح) according to Islamic law or positive law. The author gives two examples: (1) selling game hunted outside the Haram is void because the animal is like carrion (حرام بسبب النص الشرعي). (2) contracts in gold currency in Syria after a French mandate law prohibited them are void. The footnote explains the gold law in detail, noting that it only applies to contracts that transfer ownership, not to deposits (وديعة), which are trusts (أمانة) and must be returned in kind. The principle is that if a ruling prohibits the object itself, the contract is void (باطل) because the object is like a dead animal.

Key Terms
محل العقد (mahall al-‘aqd): The subject matter of the contract, e.g., the goods or service exchanged.
باطل (bāṭil): Void; the contract has no legal effect from its inception.
إيداع (īdāʿ): Deposit; a contract where one party entrusts property to another for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the sale of game hunted outside the Haram void?
A) Because the hunter lacked proper equipment
B) Because the game is legally treated as carrion, making the subject matter impermissible
C) Because the sale occurred outside the Haram
D) Because the game was not slaughtered properly

Key Takeaway
A contract's subject matter must be permissible; if it is not, the contract is void—but this rule only applies to contracts that transfer ownership, not to trusts.

History and Development of Fiqh
intermediate
8 min
The Rule of Borrowed Name (Al-Ism al-Musta'ar) in Contractual Simulation
Explains a form of contractual simulation involving the person of the contracting party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rule of the borrowed name (al-ism al-musta'ar) and apply its legal effects while identifying restrictions to protect third-party rights.

Core Idea
In a borrowed-name simulation, the real principal is the true owner — but third-party rights can override this.

This lesson explains a specific form of contractual simulation where the simulation concerns the person of the contracting party, not the contract itself. You will learn how Hanafi fiqh treats the 'borrowed name' arrangement, its legal effect, and the crucial restrictions that safeguard creditors and other third parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of simulation
Recognize that in this case, the simulation is in the person of the contracting party, not in the essence of the contract. The outward contractor is not the real party.
This distinguishes it from other simulations like sale without true intent.
2
Define the borrowed name arrangement
Understand the collusion: a person contracts in his own name but agrees that all benefits belong to another (the real principal). The name is 'borrowed'.
It is a form of hidden agency (wakala sirriyya).
3
Apply the legal effect
The real principal replaces the apparent one. All fruits of contracts and acquisitions belong to the real principal.
This is the default rule.
4
Recognize the restrictions
Third-party rights, especially creditors, can limit or nullify this effect. Declarations of borrowed name during death illness or insolvency are scrutinized to prevent fraud.
The law protects those who relied on the apparent ownership.
5
Locate the codification
The Ottoman Majalla (articles 1591-1593) codified this rule. Familiarize yourself with these references.
These articles are primary sources for Hanafi fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (٣٣) صورية العقود
٤/٣٣ - ج : المواضعة في الشخص، أو قضية «الاسم المستعار». وقد تكون الصورية المواضع عليها في الشخص المتعاقد لا في أصل العقد، وذلك كما في الشخص المسخّر الذي يتعاقد ويعمل ويكتب باسمه لمصلحته الشخصية في الظاهر، ولمصلحة غيره في الباطن، ثم يعلن أن كل عقوده وأملاكه أو بعضها هي في الواقع لذلك الغير، وأن اسمه فيها مستعار اسمه ، وهي قضية: الاسم المستعار .
عن
فالمواضعة هنا إنما هي بين العامل باسمه ظاهراً، ومن يعمل لمصلحته ،باطناً فهي من قبيل الوكالة السرية يتواطأ الطرفان على إخفائها، وظهور الوكيل بمظهر الأصيل.
فعندئذ يحل الشخص الحقيقي محل الشخص الصوري، أي يحل المستعار له الاسم محل المعير فتكون ثمرات العقود والاكتسابات للمستعار له، ضمن حدود وقيود شرعية وقانونية ترى في محلاتها، لصيانة حقوق الأشخاص الآخرين، خوفاً من أن يتخذ إعلان عارية الاسم وسيلة لتهريب المال من بين يدي من تعلق به حقهم؛ كما لو أقر بذلك المريض مرض الموت، أو المفلس.
وقد تناولت المجلة مسألة الاسم المستعار وبينت حكمها إجمالاً في الفصل الثاني من كتاب الإقرار المجلة/ ١٥٩١ - ١٥٩٣).
Chapter (33): Simulation of Contracts
4/33 - C: Collusion regarding the person, or the issue of the 'borrowed name'. Simulation may be agreed upon regarding the person of the contracting party, not in the essence of the contract. This is like the case of a person who is suborned, who contracts, works, and writes in his own name for his apparent personal interest, but in reality for the interest of someone else, then announces that all his contracts and properties, or some of them, are in reality for that other person, and that his name in them is a borrowed name. This is the issue of the borrowed name.
So the collusion here is between the one acting in his own name outwardly and the one for whose benefit he acts inwardly. It is a form of secret agency where the two parties agree to conceal it, and the agent appears as the principal.
Then the real person takes the place of the apparent person, i.e., the person for whom the name is borrowed takes the place of the lender, so the fruits of the contracts and acquisitions belong to the one for whom the name is borrowed, subject to legal and Shariah limits and restrictions to be seen in their proper places, to protect the rights of other persons, for fear that the declaration of a borrowed name might be used as a means to smuggle property away from those who have a right to it; such as if a person in his death illness or an insolvent debtor makes such a declaration.
The Majalla has dealt with the issue of the borrowed name and stated its ruling in summary in the second chapter of the Book of Iqrār (art. 1591-1593).
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage discusses a specific form of simulation (sūriyya) in contracts: collusion regarding the person of the contracting party, known as the 'borrowed name' (al-ism al-musta‘ār). The author explains that here the simulation is not in the contract itself but in the identity of the contracting party. A person outwardly contracts and acts in his own name, but in reality he is acting for another – the name is borrowed. This is a form of hidden agency where the agent appears as the principal. The legal effect is that the real principal replaces the apparent one, and the benefits of the contracts go to the real principal, subject to conditions to protect third-party rights. For example, if a person in his death illness or an insolvent debtor declares that his property is held under a borrowed name, this could harm creditors. Therefore, such declarations are subject to scrutiny. The passage refers to the Ottoman Majalla (articles 1591-1593) which codified this ruling.

Key Terms
الاسم المستعار (al-ism al-musta‘ār): A situation where a person contracts in his own name but acts for the benefit of another, with the agreement that the name is borrowed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the borrowed name (al-ism al-musta‘ār) scenario, who ultimately owns the fruits of the contracts?
A) The apparent contracting party
B) The real principal for whom the name is borrowed
C) It depends on the contract type
D) The court decides

Key Takeaway
The borrowed name simulation transfers beneficial ownership to the real principal, but this is subject to restrictions that protect creditors and other third parties.
